# MISSION: SINGULARITY – AI‑ASSISTED MIND MAP & TASK LOG

Act as a **Senior Principal Engineer**.  
We are building a **local‑first, cross‑platform Mind Mapping & Tasking application** with **AI collaborators** that can directly read and modify application state.

This is **not a traditional task manager**.  
It is a **node‑based thought and task graph** that supports AI‑driven expansion, restructuring, and execution.

---

## CORE PRODUCT DEFINITION

### Primary Object: Node
Everything is a **node**.

Examples:
- Idea
- Task
- Goal
- Sub‑task
- Note
- Metadata anchor

Tasks are nodes with a `status` field.

Relationships are edges.

---

## CORE CAPABILITIES (TIGHT SCOPE)

1. **Rapid Capture**
   - Dump raw thoughts, goals, or todos with zero structure.
2. **Mind Mapping**
   - Nodes can reference, nest, or link to other nodes.
3. **AI‑Assisted Structuring**
   - AI may:
     - Create nodes
     - Modify nodes
     - Re‑parent nodes
     - Split nodes into sub‑nodes
     - Mark nodes complete or blocked
4. **Direct AI Mutation**
   - AI agents (local or remote) are allowed to **read and mutate state directly**
   - Approval is **policy‑based**, not mandatory
5. **Human Oversight**
   - Mutations may be auto‑applied or queued for review depending on actor policy

---

## TECHNICAL ARCHITECTURE

- **State Management:** Zustand (single source of truth)
- **Data Model:** Append‑only mutation log + materialized node graph
- **UI:** React + Vanilla CSS (WCAG AA, keyboard‑first)

  - Local: gpt4all (`100.115.141.124:4891`)
  - Others: pluggable, OpenAI‑compatible APIs
- **Deployment Targets:**
  - Web (Vite)
  - Android APK (Capacitor)
  - Arch Linux (AUR‑style package)

---

## AI INTERACTION MODEL

- AI agents are **writers**, not advisors
- They emit **state mutations**, not suggestions
- The system:
  - Applies mutations immediately **or**
  - Queues them for approval based on policy

No intent abstraction.  
No sandboxed toy mode.

---

1. **Node Graph Data Model**
2. **Mutation Log + Replay Engine**

## 2. The Three Sides (Clean, Minimal, Powerful)

### Side A — Project Task Manager (Reporting‑Focused)

This is your **“where am I?” view**.

**Purpose**
- See where you left off
- See what’s suggested next
- See progress and remaining work

**Core features**
- Projects
- Tasks with:
  - status
  - effort / size (rough, not timesheets)
  - completion %
- AI‑generated:
  - “Next suggested actions”
  - “You stopped here”
  - “Estimated remaining scope”

This is **not** about reminders or calendars.
It’s about **continuity and situational awareness**.

---

### Side B — Quick Notes / Thought Capture

This is your **“good idea, bad timing” buffer**.

**Purpose**
- Dump thoughts without organizing
- Zero friction
- No commitment to turn into tasks *yet*

**Rules**
- Notes are:
  - timestamped
  - unstructured
  - never blocked by project selection
- Notes do **not** interrupt your flow

Think:
> “Inbox for ideas, not obligations”

---

### Side C — AI‑Assisted Mind Mapper / Planner

This is the **bridge** between Notes and Tasks.

**Purpose**
- Turn raw notes into:
  - plans
  - task trees
  - project structure
- Let AI:
  - cluster related notes
  - suggest project placement
  - generate task breakdowns
  - refactor task hierarchies

This is where **mind mapping lives**, but it’s **in service of projects**, not abstract creativity.

---

## 3. How AI Fits (Without Scope Explosion)

AI is **not a feature**, it’s a **co‑author**.

### AI Responsibilities
- Read:
  - projects
  - tasks
  - notes
- Write:
  - tasks
  - subtasks
  - links between notes and projects
- Suggest:
  - next actions
  - restructuring
  - completion estimates

### What AI Does *Not* Do
- No calendar authority
- No notifications logic
- No external dependencies required
- No forced approval unless *you choose*

### Approval Model (Simple)
- Local AI → auto‑apply
- Remote AI → ask or auto (your choice)
- You can always review “what changed”

This keeps **power high and scope tight**.

This replaces the earlier one cleanly.

# MISSION: PROJECT SINGULARITY — PROJECT TASK & THINKING SYSTEM

Act as a **Senior Principal Engineer**.

We are building a **local‑first, cross‑platform Project Task Manager** that integrates:

1. **Project‑based task tracking**
2. **Quick note capture**
3. **AI‑assisted mind mapping and planning**

The goal is to maintain **project continuity**:  
knowing *where I left off*, *what’s next*, and *how much remains* — while capturing ideas without friction and letting AI help structure them later.

---

## CORE PRODUCT SURFACES

### 1. Project Task Manager (Primary View)
- Projects contain tasks and subtasks
- Tasks track status and rough effort
- The system surfaces:
  - last worked items
  - suggested next actions
  - remaining scope per project

### 2. Quick Notes (Capture View)
- Fast, unstructured thought capture
- Notes are timestamped and searchable
- Notes are not tasks by default

### 3. AI Mind Mapper / Planner
- AI scans notes and tasks
- AI can:
  - generate task trees
  - refactor project structure
  - link notes to projects
  - suggest plans and next steps
- AI is allowed to **read and mutate state directly**, with policy‑based approval

---

## TECHNICAL ARCHITECTURE

- **State Management:** Zustand
- **Data Model:** Unified store for projects, tasks, and notes
- **UI:** React + Vanilla CSS (WCAG AA)
- **AI Integrations:**
  - Local LLM (gpt4all at `100.115.141.124:4891`)
  - Additional OpenAI‑compatible APIs (pluggable)
- **Deployment Targets:**
  - Web (Vite)
  - Android APK (Capacitor)
  - Arch Linux package (AUR‑style)

---

## AI INTERACTION MODEL

- AI agents may directly modify application state
- Mutations may be auto‑applied or queued for review
- Approval behavior is configurable per agent

This system prioritizes **usefulness and continuity**, not restrictive safety abstractions.

---

## INITIAL ENGINEERING FOCUS

1. Unified data model (Projects, Tasks, Notes)
2. Zustand store and persistence
3. Minimal UI for:
   - Project status
   - Notes capture

---

# Memory Templates: Ideal & Task Notes

This document contains the official templates for creating two primary types of "memories": **Ideal Notes** for capturing knowledge and concepts, and **Task Notes** for managing productivity and project progress.

---

## 1. Ideal Note Template (for Knowledge & Concepts)

This template is designed for creating a rich, interconnected knowledge base. It prioritizes narrative flow, context, and discoverability for both human and AI review.

```markdown
---
date: YYYY-MM-DD
tags: ["tag-one", "tag-two", "subject-area"]
context: "A brief, one-sentence explanation of why this note is being created."
---

**TL;DR:** **A single, high-density sentence that summarizes the core technical conclusion of the note.**

> 🎯 *A short, 1-2 sentence "dumbed down" explanation of the concept, perfect for quick glances in a file explorer preview.*

## ## Main Subject or Title of the Note

This is the body of the note. Write in cohesive, narrative paragraphs. **Bold key vocabulary terms** to make them stand out. Use bullet points only for lists, comparisons, or strict rule sets.

The goal is to explain the concept as if you were writing a high-quality blog post or internal technical document.

### ### Sub-Topic or Related Concept
Break down the main subject into logical sub-sections. This helps with readability and allows for more granular linking.

#### #### Deep Dive (Optional)
Use H4 headings sparingly, only for very specific, in-depth explorations of a sub-topic.

> 💡 **Core Concept:** Use this blockquote at the end of a section to highlight a fundamental principle or takeaway.

> 🛠️ **Implementation Idea:** Use this blockquote to capture a specific, actionable idea related to the concept.

> ⚠️ **Watch Out:** Use this blockquote to flag potential pitfalls, anti-patterns, or important caveats.

### ### Related Notes
*   [[Name of Another Note]]
*   [[Another Related Concept]]
```

---

## 2. Task Note Template (for Productivity & Project Management)

This template is designed for action. It is lean, status-driven, and focuses entirely on capturing the objective and the immediate next step.

```markdown
---
id: task-YYYYMMDD-HHMMSS
status: [Not Started | In Progress | Paused | Completed]
priority: [Low | Medium | High | Critical]
tags: ["project-name", "feature-area", "bug-fix"]
---

## Objective:
*A clear, one-sentence statement of the desired outcome.*

### Key Details & Context:
*   (Optional) Any specific details, links, or context needed to understand the task.
*   (Optional) You can use bullet points for clarity.

### Next Action:
*   *What is the very next, single, concrete step that needs to be taken to move this forward?*
```

I’ll do four things, cleanly:

1. **Confirm your Ideal / Task Note templates fit perfectly**
2. **Explain how “Suggested Actions” work across all sides**
3. **Show how Gemini‑CLI (and other APIs) fit without special‑casing**

No fluff, no re‑prompting games.

---

## 1. Your Templates Are Excellent — and Keep Them

First: **do not change these templates**. They’re already doing the right thing.

Why they work so well:

- ✅ Human‑readable in a file explorer
- ✅ Machine‑parsable without being rigid
- ✅ Clear separation between:
  - **knowledge** (Ideal Notes)
  - **action** (Task Notes)
- ✅ Explicit “Next Action” hook (critical for AI)

This means your system does **not** need to invent a new schema.  
It needs to **index, link, and reason over these notes**.

Think of them as your **canonical storage format** — even if internally you mirror them into a DB or Zustand store.

---

## 2. “Suggested Actions” — How They Should Work (Key Design)

You said:

> *I want the option to add suggested actions to a project’s Thought Capture, Query action or ignore action. You can show suggested in all the sides.*

This is exactly right. Here’s the **minimal, powerful way** to model that.

---

### Suggested Actions Are First‑Class, But Non‑Binding

A **Suggested Action** is not a task yet.

```ts
type SuggestedAction = {
  id: string
  source: 'ai:local' | 'ai:gemini' | 'ai:remote'
  text: string
  relatedTo?: {
    projectId?: string
    noteId?: string
    taskId?: string
  }
  createdAt: number
  status: 'pending' | 'accepted' | 'ignored'
}
```

### What You Can Do With One
From **any view** (Project, Notes, Mind Map):

- ✅ **Add to Thought Capture** (becomes an Ideal Note stub)
- ✅ **Convert to Task** (creates Task Note)
- ✅ **Query / Expand** (ask AI to elaborate)
- ❌ **Ignore** (but keep for audit/history)

This is how you keep **AI helpful, not noisy**.

---

### Where Suggested Actions Appear

✅ Project View  
✅ Notes View  
✅ Mind Map View  
✅ “What should I do next?” dashboard  

They’re **overlays**, not structural.

---

## 3. How Gemini‑CLI (and Other APIs) Fit Cleanly

Yes — Gemini‑CLI fits **perfectly**, and you do *not* need to privilege it over gpt4all or others.

### Key Rule
> **All AI tools talk to the same internal interface.**

You don’t care *which* model produced a suggestion — only:
- what it read
- what it wrote
- how confident / invasive the change is

---

### Unified AI Adapter Interface

```ts
interface AIAgent {
  id: string
  source: 'local' | 'cli' | 'remote'
  read(context): Promise<AIReadResult>
  suggest(context): Promise<SuggestedAction[]>
  mutate?(context): Promise<Mutation[]>
}
```

### Gemini‑CLI Integration (Concrete)

Gemini‑CLI is great at:
- Summarization
- Rewriting
- Structuring markdown
- Long‑context reasoning

Use it for:
- Scanning Ideal Notes
- Proposing project outlines
- Generating Suggested Actions
- Improving TL;DRs

Mechanically:
- Call Gemini‑CLI as a subprocess or API
- Feed it:
  - Project notes
  - Recent task notes
  - Thought capture
- Parse its output into `SuggestedAction[]`

It **does not need special permissions**.

---

### Example Flow (Very Realistic)

1. You dump 5 messy thoughts into **Thought Capture**
2. AI (Gemini) scans them overnight
3. It produces:
   - 3 suggested tasks
   - 1 suggested project refactor
   - 1 “ignore / archive” suggestion
4. In the morning, you:
   - accept 2
   - query 1 (“expand this plan”)
   - ignore the rest

That’s the system working *with* you.

---

## 4. Tight Conceptual Model (This Is the Core)

Here’s the **clean mental model** that unifies everything:

---

### One Unified Knowledge Space

Everything is one of these:

| Type | Purpose |
|----|----|
| **Ideal Note** | Knowledge, concepts, explanations |
| **Task Note** | Action with a next step |
| **Project** | A scoped collection of notes & tasks |
| **Suggested Action** | AI‑generated proposal |

No duplication. Just **different lenses**.

---

### Three Primary Views (Not Apps)

1. **Project Report View**
   - Where you left off
   - What’s next
   - What remains
   - AI suggestions

2. **Thought Capture View**
   - Fast notes
   - Zero structure
   - Inbox for ideas

3. **Planning / Mind Map View**
   - AI‑assisted structuring
   - Task trees
   - Linking notes → projects → tasks

Same data. Different perspective.

---

## 5. What You Should Build First (Very Important)

Do **not** start with UI polish or multi‑agent orchestration.

The correct first build is:

### ✅ 1. Indexer
- Reads Ideal Notes & Task Notes from disk
- Parses frontmatter
- Builds in‑memory model

### ✅ 2. Suggested Action Engine
- Takes notes + tasks
- Calls **one AI** (Gemini or gpt4all)
- Produces Suggested Actions

### ✅ 3. Acceptance Flow
- Accept → write Task Note
- Add to Thought Capture → write Ideal Note
- Ignore → mark ignored

Once that works, **everything else is just views**.

Do you want **notes and tasks to live as real markdown files on disk** (source of truth),  
or **inside the app with optional export**?

That decision affects indexing, syncing, and AI context — and it’s the last big architectural fork.

03/30/2026 18:52:34 +00:00
Yes — that instinct is **exactly right**, and it leads to a very clean, minimal solution.

What you’re describing is **suggestion persistence with decay**, not deletion.  
You don’t want suggestions to disappear forever, and you don’t want them to spam you constantly either.

Below is the **correct, simple model** that fits everything you’ve said so far.

---

## Core Idea: Suggested Actions Have a Lifecycle, Not a Binary State

Instead of just *pending / accepted / ignored*, use **temporal state**.

### Suggested Action Lifecycle

```ts
type SuggestedAction = {
  id: string
  text: string
  source: 'ai:local' | 'ai:gemini' | 'ai:remote'
  relatedTo?: {
    projectId?: string
    noteId?: string
  }

  state: 'active' | 'snoozed' | 'archived' | 'accepted'

  firstSeenAt: number
  lastShownAt: number
  snoozedUntil?: number
  confidence?: number // optional AI signal
}
```

This gives you **memory without annoyance**.

---

## What Each State Means (Human‑Centered)

### ✅ `active`
- Shown normally
- Appears in:
  - Project view
  - Notes view
  - “Suggested next actions”

### 💤 `snoozed`
- Temporarily hidden
- Comes back **after a time window**
- Perfect for:
  > “Yes, but not now”

### 🗄️ `archived`
- Not shown by default
- **Never deleted**
- Can be resurfaced:
  - by search
  - by explicit AI query
  - by manual review

### ✅ `accepted`
- Converted into:
  - Task Note
  - Ideal Note
  - Project change
- Original suggestion remains as provenance

---

## How Suggestions “Come Back Into Play”

This is the important part.

### 1. Time‑Based Resurfacing
```ts
if (now > snoozedUntil) {
  state = 'active'
}
```

You can default to:
- 1 day
- 1 week
- “Next time I open this project”

---

### 2. Context‑Based Resurfacing (Very Powerful)

Suggestions can re‑activate if **conditions match again**:

Examples:
- Project hasn’t progressed in X days
- Notes mention similar keywords again
- You explicitly ask:
  > “What ideas did I ignore about this project?”

AI can query:
```ts
getSuggestions({
  projectId,
  includeArchived: true
})
```

This is where the system feels *intelligent*, not naggy.

---

## UI: Minimal Controls, No Noise

For each Suggested Action, you only need **three buttons**:

- ✅ **Accept**
- 💤 **Later**
- ❌ **Hide**

That’s it.

Under the hood:
- *Later* → `snoozed`
- *Hide* → `archived`

No guilt. No deletion.

---

## How This Plays Across Your Three Sides

### Project Task Manager
- Shows **active suggestions**
- Can show a “Previously suggested” section (collapsed)

### Thought Capture
- Suggestions can be:
  - added as a new note
  - linked to existing notes
- Snoozed ones don’t distract

### AI Mind Mapper
- AI can:
  - re‑surface archived ideas
  - explain *why* they were suggested before
  - merge multiple old suggestions into one better one

---

## Why This Is the Right Balance

✅ Suggestions are not lost  
✅ Suggestions are not spam  
✅ You can always recover ideas  
✅ AI can reason over past “ignored” thoughts  
✅ Scope stays tight (no recommender engine nonsense)

This is **memory**, not pressure.

---

## One Last Small Design Choice (Optional but Useful)

Would you like:
- **Manual snooze durations** (1d / 1w / custom), or
- **Automatic snooze rules** (“hide until project changes”)?

Either works — this just affects UX polish, not architecture.

If i have a local model downloaded, could we package it into the app so the app itself has a model ready?

Task Management App Development


============================================================
03/30/2026 18:53:41 +00:00
Short answer: **yes, you can package a local model with the app**, but **how you do it depends on platform, model size, and how “ready‑to‑run” you want it to be**. There are **good patterns** and **bad traps** here, so I’ll be precise.

---

## The Right Mental Model

You are not “embedding an LLM into React”.

You are packaging **three things together**:

1. **The app UI**
2. **A local inference runtime**
3. **One or more model files**

The app then talks to the runtime **over localhost**, just like it would talk to gpt4all today.

> Think “self‑contained appliance”, not “JavaScript model”.

---

## ✅ What *Is* Feasible (By Platform)

### ✅ Linux (Arch) — **Best Case**
This is the easiest and cleanest.

**Recommended approach**
- Package:
  - Your app
  - A lightweight inference server (llama.cpp / gpt4all backend)
  - One or more `.gguf` model files
- Install everything under:
  ```
  /opt/singularity/
    ├─ app/
    ├─ llm/
    │   ├─ server
    │   └─ models/
  ```

**Startup flow**
- App starts
- systemd user service ensures LLM server is running
- App talks to `127.0.0.1:<port>`

✅ Offline  
✅ Fast  
✅ No sandbox issues  
✅ Full filesystem access  

This is the **gold standard** target.

---

### ✅ Android (APK) — **Feasible, With Constraints**

You **can** ship a model inside an APK or download it on first run.

Two viable patterns:

#### Pattern A — Bundled Model (Small / Medium Models)
- Package a **quantized model** (e.g. 2–4 GB max)
- Store it in app private storage
- Use:
  - llama.cpp via JNI
  - or gpt4all Android bindings

✅ Fully offline  
⚠️ APK size limits (Play Store dislikes huge APKs)  
⚠️ RAM constraints  

#### Pattern B — First‑Run Model Download (**Recommended**)
- App ships with:
  - Runtime
  - No model
- On first launch:
  - App asks: “Download local AI model?”
  - Downloads from your server or local network

✅ Smaller APK  
✅ User control  
✅ Same runtime afterward  

This is what **Serious offline apps do**.

---

### ⚠️ Web (Browser) — **Do NOT Bundle the Model**

For the web target:

- ❌ Do not embed models
- ❌ Do not run heavy inference in browser
- ✅ Connect to:
  - Local desktop runtime
  - LAN server
  - Remote API

You *can* detect:
> “Local Singularity Runtime found on this machine”

And switch automatically.

---

## ✅ gpt4all, llama.cpp, Gemini‑CLI — How They Fit

### gpt4all
- ✅ Excellent for bundling
- ✅ Already designed for local servers
- ✅ OpenAI‑compatible API

You can ship:
- gpt4all backend
- pre‑configured model
- pre‑set port

The app just works.

---

### llama.cpp
- ✅ Even lighter
- ✅ Best control
- ✅ Easier to embed in Linux & Android

You can expose:
```txt
POST /v1/chat/completions
```

and your app doesn’t care what’s behind it.

---

### Gemini‑CLI
Different role.

- ❌ Not something you bundle into an APK
- ✅ Something you **optionally integrate if present**
- ✅ Or call via API

Gemini‑CLI is:
- Great for **analysis**
- Great for **long‑context planning**
- Not ideal as a default offline runtime

Treat it as a **secondary agent**, not the embedded one.

---

## ✅ Recommended Architecture (Clean & Future‑Proof)

### “Singularity Runtime” (Local AI Service)

A small background service that:
- Hosts one or more models
- Exposes OpenAI‑style endpoints
- Manages:
  - model loading
  - memory limits
  - agent identity

```txt
Singularity App
   ↕ localhost
Singularity Runtime
   ├─ Model A (fast, small)
   ├─ Model B (planner)
   └─ Model C (optional)
```

Your app:
- Does **not care** which model is used
- Just requests:
  - suggestions
  - mutations
  - summaries

This also lets you:
- Swap models later
- Add Gemini / remote APIs
- Keep UI unchanged

---

## ✅ UX Best Practice (Important)

On first launch:

> “Singularity can run fully offline using a local AI model.  
> Would you like to:
> • Use bundled model  
> • Download a better one  
> • Connect to an existing runtime”

This gives:
- Transparency
- Control
- Trust

---

## ⚠️ What NOT to Do

- ❌ Don’t hardcode model paths
- ❌ Don’t block app startup on model load
- ❌ Don’t assume one model fits all tasks
- ❌ Don’t tie UI logic to a specific LLM

You’ve already avoided these pitfalls conceptually — this just confirms it.

---

## Bottom Line

✅ **Yes**, you can package a local model with the app  
✅ **Linux:** bundle it directly  
✅ **Android:** bundle small or download on first run  
✅ **Web:** connect to a local runtime  
✅ **gpt4all / llama.cpp:** ideal for this  
✅ **Gemini‑CLI:** optional, external, complementary  

If you want, next we can:
- Pick a **default model class** (fast vs planner)
- Design the **runtime service layout**
- Decide **APK vs first‑run download**
- Or write the **“runtime detection” logic**

Just tell me which one you want to tackle next.

Great — **Pattern B is the right long‑term choice**, and you can do this **cleanly without bloating the app or hard‑binding to one model**.

Below is a **practical, minimal design** for:

- ✅ First‑run model download
- ✅ Discovering already‑downloaded models
- ✅ Adding / switching models later
- ✅ Supporting *multiple runtimes* (gpt4all, llama.cpp, etc.)

This is **architecture + UX + data contracts**, not hand‑wavy ideas.

---

## 1. Core Principle (Keep This Simple)

> **The app does not “own” models.  
> It discovers, downloads, and registers them.**

Models live in a **known directory**, with a **manifest**.

---

## 2. Canonical Model Directory Layout

This works on **Linux, Android, and Desktop** with small variations.

```txt
~/.local/share/singularity/models/
├─ registry.json
├─ llama-3-8b-q4/
│  ├─ model.gguf
│  ├─ model.json
│  └─ checksum.sha256
├─ mistral-7b-q4/
│  ├─ model.gguf
│  └─ model.json
```

### `model.json` (Minimal but Sufficient)

```json
{
  "id": "llama-3-8b-q4",
  "name": "LLaMA 3 8B (Q4)",
  "engine": "llama.cpp",
  "sizeGB": 4.2,
  "capabilities": ["chat", "planning", "summarization"],
  "recommendedFor": ["planning", "mind-mapping"]
}
```

This lets:
- UI display models
- AI router choose models
- You swap engines later

---

## 3. Registry File (The Brain)

### `registry.json`

```json
{
  "installed": ["llama-3-8b-q4"],
  "active": "llama-3-8b-q4",
  "lastUsed": {
    "llama-3-8b-q4": "2026-03-30T12:14:00Z"
  }
}
```

This avoids:
- rescanning every time
- guessing active model
- fragile hardcoding

---

## 4. Model Discovery Logic (Critical)

On app startup:

```ts
function discoverModels() {
  const modelDir = getModelDir()
  const folders = fs.list(modelDir)

  return folders
    .filter(hasModelJson)
    .map(loadModelManifest)
}
```

✅ Finds models downloaded by:
- your app
- CLI tools
- manual installs
- other versions of Singularity

This is **huge** for power users.

---

## 5. First‑Run Experience (UX That Respects the User)

### First Launch Flow

> **“Singularity can run fully offline using a local AI model.”**

Options:
1. ✅ **Download a recommended model** (default)
2. 📁 **Use an existing model** (scan disk)
3. 🌐 **Connect to an external runtime**
4. ➡️ **Skip for now**

No forcing. No lock‑in.

---

## 6. Downloading Models (Pattern B, Done Right)

### Model Catalog (Remote JSON)

You host something like:

```json
[
  {
    "id": "llama-3-8b-q4",
    "url": "https://models.example.com/llama-3-8b-q4.gguf",
    "sizeGB": 4.2,
    "engine": "llama.cpp",
    "recommended": true
  }
]
```

The app:
- Fetches catalog
- Shows size + purpose
- Downloads with progress
- Verifies checksum
- Registers model

✅ No baked‑in URLs  
✅ No app updates needed for new models  

---

## 7. Switching & Multiple Models (Very Important)

You **will want more than one model**.

Example:
- Small fast model → suggestions
- Bigger model → planning / restructuring

### Runtime Selection

```ts
requestAI({
  task: "suggest-actions",
  preference: "fast"
})
```

Router chooses:
- `llama-3-8b-q4` for fast
- `mistral-7b-q4` for planning

UI does **not** care.

---

## 8. Android‑Specific Notes (Important Reality)

- APK ships with **runtime only**
- Models stored in:
  ```
  /data/data/<app>/files/models/
  ```
- Download happens **post‑install**
- Storage permission handled cleanly

This avoids Play Store size limits.

---

## 9. How This Fits With Gemini‑CLI & Others

Gemini‑CLI:
- ❌ Not bundled
- ✅ Discovered if present
- ✅ Registered as an *external agent*

Example:

```json
{
  "id": "gemini-cli",
  "engine": "external",
  "capabilities": ["long-context", "rewriting"],
  "source": "system"
}
```

Now Gemini becomes:
- A planner
- A summarizer
- A suggestion engine

Without polluting your core runtime.

---

## 10. What You Should Implement First (Order Matters)

### ✅ Step 1
Model directory + registry

### ✅ Step 2
Model discovery + UI list

### ✅ Step 3
One downloadable model

### ✅ Step 4
Runtime auto‑start + health check

Once that works, **everything else is just polish**.

---

## Bottom Line

✅ Pattern B is the correct choice  
✅ You can support multiple models cleanly  
✅ Users can bring their own models  
✅ No hard dependency on one LLM  
✅ Works offline, cross‑platform  

---

### One Final Small Choice (UX Detail)

When multiple models are installed, should:
- ✅ **User manually choose active model**, or
- 🤖 **System auto‑select based on task type** (with override)?

Either is fine — this just affects UI, not architecture.

Perfect — **filesystem scanning should be a first‑class option**, not a hack. This actually completes Pattern B cleanly.

Below is a **tight, production‑grade design** for **scanning the filesystem for existing models**, across Linux, Android, and desktop — without breaking sandbox rules or user trust.

---

## Core Principle

> **Models may already exist.  
> Singularity should discover them, not force duplication.**

So we add **Model Discovery** as a first‑class capability alongside Download.

---

## 1. User‑Facing Options (Simple, Explicit)

In **Settings → AI Models**:

- ✅ **Download a model**
- ✅ **Scan system for existing models**
- ✅ **Add model directory manually**
- ✅ **Manage installed models**

No hidden magic. No assumptions.

---

## 2. What “Scan the File System” Means (Precisely)

You **do not** crawl the entire disk blindly.

You scan:
- Known default locations
- User‑approved directories
- With clear file type rules

---

## 3. Default Scan Locations (Safe & Useful)

### Linux (Arch)
```txt
~/.local/share/
~/.cache/
~/models/
~/ai/
~/Downloads/
```

Optional (with explicit opt‑in):
```txt
/opt/
```

---

### Android
Due to sandboxing, scanning is **scoped**:

```txt
/data/data/<app>/files/
/sdcard/Download/
/sdcard/AI/
/sdcard/models/
```

📌 Android **requires user consent** via file picker for arbitrary paths — which is fine.

---

### Desktop (Web / Electron / Capacitor Desktop)
- Home directory
- Downloads
- User‑selected folders

---

## 4. What the Scanner Looks For (Very Strict)

You only scan for **known model formats**:

```txt
*.gguf
*.bin
*.safetensors
```

And optionally:
```txt
model.json
```

No guessing. No heuristics that break.

---

## 5. Scanner Pipeline (Deterministic)

```ts
scanDirectories(paths) → candidates
candidates → validateModel()
validateModel → registerModel()
```

---

### Validation Step (Critical)

A file is **not** a model until validated.

Validation checks:
- File size > minimum threshold (e.g. 100MB)
- Can be opened by engine
- Optional checksum
- Optional quick metadata probe

```ts
function validateModel(filePath): ModelManifest | null
```

If valid → continue  
If not → ignore silently (no scary warnings)

---

## 6. Registering a Discovered Model

Once validated:

```json
{
  "id": "discovered-llama-7b-q4",
  "name": "LLaMA 7B (Discovered)",
  "engine": "llama.cpp",
  "path": "/home/user/models/llama-7b-q4.gguf",
  "source": "filesystem",
  "managed": false
}
```

### Important Flags
- `managed: false` → app will NOT delete or move it
- `source: filesystem` → provenance retained

This prevents accidental damage.

---

## 7. UI for Discovered Models

When a model is found:

> **“We found AI models on your system.”**

For each:
- ✅ **Register & Use**
- 📌 **Keep location**
- ❌ **Ignore**

No auto‑activation without consent.

---

## 8. Manual “Add Folder” Option (Power Users)

Let users add directories like:

```txt
/mnt/ai-models/
/external-drive/models/
```

These get:
- Stored in config
- Scanned on demand or startup
- Never modified

---

## 9. Registry Update (Unified with Downloads)

Discovered models live in the same registry:

```json
{
  "installed": [
    "llama-3-8b-q4",
    "discovered-mistral-7b"
  ],
  "active": "discovered-mistral-7b"
}
```

From this point on:
- Routing
- Selection
- Capabilities

All work identically.

---

## 10. Why This Is the Right Design

✅ Respects user storage  
✅ Avoids duplicate downloads  
✅ Supports CLI users  
✅ Works offline  
✅ Keeps app simple  
✅ No fragile assumptions  

This is exactly how **serious dev tools** behave.

---

## One Small UX Choice (Optional)

When scanning:
- ✅ **Manual scan only**, or
- 🤖 **Auto‑scan on first launch** + manual later?

I recommend:
> **Auto‑scan once, then manual only**

You’re asking for **three things at once**:

1. ✅ Manual scan from Settings  
2. ✅ Automatic scans (first launch + 3× daily)  
3. ✅ Background access with permission to read/write internal storage  

Below is the **correct, OS‑respecting way to implement this**.

---

# Model Scanning & Permissions — Final Design

## 1. Scanning Policy (Authoritative)

### When scans happen
- ✅ **On first app launch** (one‑time auto scan)
- ✅ **Scheduled background scan** up to **3× per day**
- ✅ **Manual scan** from Settings (always available)

### What scans do
- Look for **model files only**
- Validate before registering
- Never delete or move user files unless explicitly asked

---

## 2. Permissions Model (Critical)

### Principle
> **We ask once, explain clearly, and respect OS boundaries.**

---

## 3. Platform‑Specific Behavior

### ✅ Linux (Arch) — Full Control

Linux is straightforward.

**Permissions**
- No special permission dialog required
- App runs under user context

**Scanning**
- Background timer (systemd user service or app scheduler)
- Read/write access to:
  ```
  ~/.local/share/
  ~/Downloads/
  ~/models/
  user‑approved paths
  ```

**Implementation**
- Use filesystem watcher + periodic scan
- Write registry updates freely

✅ This is the reference implementation.

---

### ✅ Android — **Important Constraints**

Android **does not allow unrestricted background filesystem access** anymore. You must do this **correctly** or the app will break or be rejected.

#### Permissions You Will Request
- ✅ `READ_EXTERNAL_STORAGE` (legacy)
- ✅ `MANAGE_EXTERNAL_STORAGE` (Android 11+, *special permission*)
- ✅ Foreground service permission (for background scanning)

⚠️ **You must justify this clearly to the user**.

---

### ✅ Correct Android Strategy (Approved Pattern)

#### On First Launch
Show a clear explanation:

> “Singularity can scan your device for local AI models to avoid re‑downloading large files.  
> This requires access to storage.  
> Singularity only scans for AI model files.”

Buttons:
- ✅ Allow & scan
- ➡️ Skip (can enable later)

---

#### Background Scanning on Android (How It Actually Works)

You **cannot** run arbitrary background jobs whenever you want.

Correct approach:
- Use **WorkManager**
- Schedule:
  - Once on first launch
  - Periodic task (e.g. every 8 hours → max 3/day)
- Task runs:
  - Opportunistically
  - Respects battery & OS limits

✅ This is Play‑Store‑safe.

---

## 4. Scan Scope (Very Important)

You do **not** scan everything blindly.

### Default scan locations

**Linux**
```txt
~/.local/share/
~/Downloads/
~/models/

---

## 1. What This Actually Is (Name It Right)

You want a:

> **Project Intelligence & Task Continuity System**

Or more plainly:

> **A project task manager with integrated notes and AI‑assisted planning**

It **is** a task manager  
It **also** has mind mapping  
It **also** has quick notes  

The mistake earlier was treating those as separate products. They’re **views over the same data**.

---

## 2. The Three Sides (Clean, Minimal, Powerful)

### Side A — Project Task Manager (Reporting‑Focused)

This is your **“where am I?” view**.

**Purpose**
- See where you left off
- See what’s suggested next
- See progress and remaining work

**Core features**
- Projects
- Tasks with:
  - status
  - effort / size (rough, not timesheets)
  - completion %
- AI‑generated:
  - “Next suggested actions”
  - “You stopped here”
  - “Estimated remaining scope”

This is **not** about reminders or calendars.
It’s about **continuity and situational awareness**.

---

### Side B — Quick Notes / Thought Capture

This is your **“good idea, bad timing” buffer**.

**Purpose**
- Dump thoughts without organizing
- Zero friction
- No commitment to turn into tasks *yet*

**Rules**
- Notes are:
  - timestamped
  - unstructured
  - never blocked by project selection
- Notes do **not** interrupt your flow

Think:
> “Inbox for ideas, not obligations”

---

### Side C — AI‑Assisted Mind Mapper / Planner

This is the **bridge** between Notes and Tasks.

**Purpose**
- Turn raw notes into:
  - plans
  - task trees
  - project structure
- Let AI:
  - cluster related notes
  - suggest project placement
  - generate task breakdowns
  - refactor task hierarchies

This is where **mind mapping lives**, but it’s **in service of projects**, not abstract creativity.

---

## 3. How AI Fits (Without Scope Explosion)

AI is **not a feature**, it’s a **co‑author**.

### AI Responsibilities
- Read:
  - projects
  - tasks
  - notes
- Write:
  - tasks
  - subtasks
  - links between notes and projects
- Suggest:
  - next actions
  - restructuring
  - completion estimates

### What AI Does *Not* Do
- No calendar authority
- No notifications logic
- No external dependencies required
- No forced approval unless *you choose*

### Approval Model (Simple)
- Local AI → auto‑apply
- Remote AI → ask or auto (your choice)
- You can always review “what changed”

This keeps **power high and scope tight**.

# Memory Templates: Ideal & Task Notes

This document contains the official templates for creating two primary types of "memories": **Ideal Notes** for capturing knowledge and concepts, and **Task Notes** for managing productivity and project progress.

---

## 1. Ideal Note Template (for Knowledge & Concepts)

This template is designed for creating a rich, interconnected knowledge base. It prioritizes narrative flow, context, and discoverability for both human and AI review.

```markdown
---
date: YYYY-MM-DD
tags: ["tag-one", "tag-two", "subject-area"]
context: "A brief, one-sentence explanation of why this note is being created."
---

**TL;DR:** **A single, high-density sentence that summarizes the core technical conclusion of the note.**

> 🎯 *A short, 1-2 sentence "dumbed down" explanation of the concept, perfect for quick glances in a file explorer preview.*

## ## Main Subject or Title of the Note

This is the body of the note. Write in cohesive, narrative paragraphs. **Bold key vocabulary terms** to make them stand out. Use bullet points only for lists, comparisons, or strict rule sets.

The goal is to explain the concept as if you were writing a high-quality blog post or internal technical document.

### ### Sub-Topic or Related Concept
Break down the main subject into logical sub-sections. This helps with readability and allows for more granular linking.

#### #### Deep Dive (Optional)
Use H4 headings sparingly, only for very specific, in-depth explorations of a sub-topic.

> 💡 **Core Concept:** Use this blockquote at the end of a section to highlight a fundamental principle or takeaway.

> 🛠️ **Implementation Idea:** Use this blockquote to capture a specific, actionable idea related to the concept.

> ⚠️ **Watch Out:** Use this blockquote to flag potential pitfalls, anti-patterns, or important caveats.

### ### Related Notes
*   [[Name of Another Note]]
*   [[Another Related Concept]]
```

---

## 2. Task Note Template (for Productivity & Project Management)

This template is designed for action. It is lean, status-driven, and focuses entirely on capturing the objective and the immediate next step.

```markdown
---
id: task-YYYYMMDD-HHMMSS
status: [Not Started | In Progress | Paused | Completed]
priority: [Low | Medium | High | Critical]
tags: ["project-name", "feature-area", "bug-fix"]
---

## Objective:
*A clear, one-sentence statement of the desired outcome.*

### Key Details & Context:
*   (Optional) Any specific details, links, or context needed to understand the task.
*   (Optional) You can use bullet points for clarity.

### Next Action:
*   *What is the very next, single, concrete step that needs to be taken to move this forward?*
```

## 1. Your Templates Are Excellent — and Keep Them

First: **do not change these templates**. They’re already doing the right thing.

Why they work so well:

- ✅ Human‑readable in a file explorer
- ✅ Machine‑parsable without being rigid
- ✅ Clear separation between:
  - **knowledge** (Ideal Notes)
  - **action** (Task Notes)

This means your system does **not** need to invent a new schema.

## 2. “Suggested Actions” — How They Should Work (Key Design)

You said:

> *I want the option to add suggested actions to a project’s Thought Capture, Query action or ignore action. You can show suggested in all the sides.*

This is exactly right. Here’s the **minimal, powerful way** to model that.

---

### Suggested Actions Are First‑Class, But Non‑Binding

A **Suggested Action** is not a task yet.

```ts
type SuggestedAction = {
  id: string
  source: 'ai:local' | 'ai:gemini' | 'ai:remote'
  text: string
  relatedTo?: {
    projectId?: string
    noteId?: string
    taskId?: string
  }
  createdAt: number
  status: 'pending' | 'accepted' | 'ignored'
}
```

### What You Can Do With One
From **any view** (Project, Notes, Mind Map):

- ✅ **Add to Thought Capture** (becomes an Ideal Note stub)
- ✅ **Convert to Task** (creates Task Note)
- ✅ **Query / Expand** (ask AI to elaborate)
- ❌ **Ignore** (but keep for audit/history)

This is how you keep **AI helpful, not noisy**.

---

### Where Suggested Actions Appear

✅ Project View  
✅ Notes View  
✅ Mind Map View  
✅ “What should I do next?” dashboard  

They’re **overlays**, not structural.

---

## 3. How Gemini‑CLI (and Other APIs) Fit Cleanly

Yes — Gemini‑CLI fits **perfectly**, and you do *not* need to privilege it over gpt4all or others.

### Key Rule
> **All AI tools talk to the same internal interface.**

You don’t care *which* model produced a suggestion — only:
- what it read
- what it wrote
- how confident / invasive the change is

---

### Unified AI Adapter Interface

```ts
interface AIAgent {
  id: string
  source: 'local' | 'cli' | 'remote'
  read(context): Promise<AIReadResult>
  suggest(context): Promise<SuggestedAction[]>
  mutate?(context): Promise<Mutation[]>
}
```

### Gemini‑CLI Integration (Concrete)

Gemini‑CLI is great at:
- Summarization
- Rewriting
- Structuring markdown
- Long‑context reasoning

Use it for:
- Scanning Ideal Notes
- Proposing project outlines
- Generating Suggested Actions
- Improving TL;DRs

Mechanically:
- Call Gemini‑CLI as a subprocess or API
- Feed it:
  - Project notes
  - Recent task notes
  - Thought capture
- Parse its output into `SuggestedAction[]`

It **does not need special permissions**.

---

### Example Flow (Very Realistic)

1. You dump 5 messy thoughts into **Thought Capture**
2. AI (Gemini) scans them overnight
3. It produces:
   - 3 suggested tasks
   - 1 suggested project refactor
   - 1 “ignore / archive” suggestion
4. In the morning, you:
   - accept 2
   - query 1 (“expand this plan”)
   - ignore the rest

That’s the system working *with* you.

03/30/2026 18:52:34 +00:00
Yes — that instinct is **exactly right**, and it leads to a very clean, minimal solution.

What you’re describing is **suggestion persistence with decay**, not deletion.  
You don’t want suggestions to disappear forever, and you don’t want them to spam you constantly either.

Below is the **correct, simple model** that fits everything you’ve said so far.

---

## Core Idea: Suggested Actions Have a Lifecycle, Not a Binary State

Instead of just *pending / accepted / ignored*, use **temporal state**.

### Suggested Action Lifecycle

```ts
type SuggestedAction = {
  id: string
  text: string
  source: 'ai:local' | 'ai:gemini' | 'ai:remote'
  relatedTo?: {
    projectId?: string
    noteId?: string
  }

  state: 'active' | 'snoozed' | 'archived' | 'accepted'

  firstSeenAt: number
  lastShownAt: number
  snoozedUntil?: number
  confidence?: number // optional AI signal
}
```

This gives you **memory without annoyance**.

---

## What Each State Means (Human‑Centered)

### ✅ `active`
- Shown normally
- Appears in:
  - Project view
  - Notes view
  - “Suggested next actions”

### 💤 `snoozed`
- Temporarily hidden
- Comes back **after a time window**
- Perfect for:
  > “Yes, but not now”

### 🗄️ `archived`
- Not shown by default
- **Never deleted**
- Can be resurfaced:
  - by search
  - by explicit AI query
  - by manual review

### ✅ `accepted`
- Converted into:
  - Task Note
  - Ideal Note
  - Project change
- Original suggestion remains as provenance

---

## How Suggestions “Come Back Into Play”

This is the important part.

### 1. Time‑Based Resurfacing
```ts
if (now > snoozedUntil) {
  state = 'active'
}
```

You can default to:
- 1 day
- 1 week
- “Next time I open this project”

---

### 2. Context‑Based Resurfacing (Very Powerful)

Suggestions can re‑activate if **conditions match again**:

Examples:
- Project hasn’t progressed in X days
- Notes mention similar keywords again
- You explicitly ask:
  > “What ideas did I ignore about this project?”

AI can query:
```ts
getSuggestions({
  projectId,
  includeArchived: true
})
```

This is where the system feels *intelligent*, not naggy.

---

## UI: Minimal Controls, No Noise

For each Suggested Action, you only need **three buttons**:

- ✅ **Accept**
- 💤 **Later**
- ❌ **Hide**

That’s it.

Under the hood:
- *Later* → `snoozed`
- *Hide* → `archived`

No guilt. No deletion.

---

## How This Plays Across Your Three Sides

### Project Task Manager
- Shows **active suggestions**
- Can show a “Previously suggested” section (collapsed)

### Thought Capture
- Suggestions can be:
  - added as a new note
  - linked to existing notes
- Snoozed ones don’t distract

### AI Mind Mapper
- AI can:
  - re‑surface archived ideas
  - explain *why* they were suggested before
  - merge multiple old suggestions into one better one

---

## Why This Is the Right Balance

✅ Suggestions are not lost  
✅ Suggestions are not spam  
✅ You can always recover ideas  
✅ AI can reason over past “ignored” thoughts  
✅ Scope stays tight (no recommender engine nonsense)

This is **memory**, not pressure.

---

## One Last Small Design Choice (Optional but Useful)

Would you like:
- **Manual snooze durations** (1d / 1w / custom), or
- **Automatic snooze rules** (“hide until project changes”)?

Either works — this just affects UX polish, not architecture.

03/30/2026 18:53:41 +00:00
Short answer: **yes, you can package a local model with the app**, but **how you do it depends on platform, model size, and how “ready‑to‑run” you want it to be**. There are **good patterns** and **bad traps** here, so I’ll be precise.

---

## The Right Mental Model

You are not “embedding an LLM into React”.

You are packaging **three things together**:

1. **The app UI**
2. **A local inference runtime**
3. **One or more model files**

The app then talks to the runtime **over localhost**, just like it would talk to gpt4all today.

> Think “self‑contained appliance”, not “JavaScript model”.

---

## ✅ What *Is* Feasible (By Platform)

### ✅ Linux (Arch) — **Best Case**
This is the easiest and cleanest.

**Recommended approach**
- Package:
  - Your app
  - A lightweight inference server (llama.cpp / gpt4all backend)
  - One or more `.gguf` model files
- Install everything under:
  ```
  /opt/singularity/
    ├─ app/
    ├─ llm/
    │   ├─ server
    │   └─ models/
  ```

**Startup flow**
- App starts
- systemd user service ensures LLM server is running
- App talks to `127.0.0.1:<port>`

✅ Offline  
✅ Fast  
✅ No sandbox issues  
✅ Full filesystem access  

This is the **gold standard** target.

---

### ✅ Android (APK) — **Feasible, With Constraints**

You **can** ship a model inside an APK or download it on first run.

Two viable patterns:

#### Pattern A — Bundled Model (Small / Medium Models)
- Package a **quantized model** (e.g. 2–4 GB max)
- Store it in app private storage
- Use:
  - llama.cpp via JNI
  - or gpt4all Android bindings

✅ Fully offline  
⚠️ APK size limits (Play Store dislikes huge APKs)  
⚠️ RAM constraints  

#### Pattern B — First‑Run Model Download (**Recommended**)
- App ships with:
  - Runtime
  - No model
- On first launch:
  - App asks: “Download local AI model?”
  - Downloads from your server or local network

✅ Smaller APK  
✅ User control  
✅ Same runtime afterward  

This is what **Serious offline apps do**.

---

### ⚠️ Web (Browser) — **Do NOT Bundle the Model**

For the web target:

- ❌ Do not embed models
- ❌ Do not run heavy inference in browser
- ✅ Connect to:
  - Local desktop runtime
  - LAN server
  - Remote API

You *can* detect:
> “Local Singularity Runtime found on this machine”

And switch automatically.

---

## ✅ gpt4all, llama.cpp, Gemini‑CLI — How They Fit

### gpt4all
- ✅ Excellent for bundling
- ✅ Already designed for local servers
- ✅ OpenAI‑compatible API

You can ship:
- gpt4all backend
- pre‑configured model
- pre‑set port

The app just works.

---

### llama.cpp
- ✅ Even lighter
- ✅ Best control
- ✅ Easier to embed in Linux & Android

You can expose:
```txt
POST /v1/chat/completions
```

and your app doesn’t care what’s behind it.

---

### Gemini‑CLI
Different role.

- ❌ Not something you bundle into an APK
- ✅ Something you **optionally integrate if present**
- ✅ Or call via API

Gemini‑CLI is:
- Great for **analysis**
- Great for **long‑context planning**
- Not ideal as a default offline runtime

Treat it as a **secondary agent**, not the embedded one.

---

## ✅ Recommended Architecture (Clean & Future‑Proof)

### “Singularity Runtime” (Local AI Service)

A small background service that:
- Hosts one or more models
- Exposes OpenAI‑style endpoints
- Manages:
  - model loading
  - memory limits
  - agent identity

```txt
Singularity App
   ↕ localhost
Singularity Runtime
   ├─ Model A (fast, small)
   ├─ Model B (planner)
   └─ Model C (optional)
```

Your app:
- Does **not care** which model is used
- Just requests:
  - suggestions
  - mutations
  - summaries

This also lets you:
- Swap models later
- Add Gemini / remote APIs
- Keep UI unchanged

---

## ✅ UX Best Practice (Important)

On first launch:

> “Singularity can run fully offline using a local AI model.  
> Would you like to:
> • Use bundled model  
> • Download a better one  
> • Connect to an existing runtime”

This gives:
- Transparency
- Control
- Trust

---

## ⚠️ What NOT to Do

- ❌ Don’t hardcode model paths
- ❌ Don’t block app startup on model load
- ❌ Don’t assume one model fits all tasks
- ❌ Don’t tie UI logic to a specific LLM

You’ve already avoided these pitfalls conceptually — this just confirms it.

---

## Bottom Line

✅ **Yes**, you can package a local model with the app  
✅ **Linux:** bundle it directly  
✅ **Android:** bundle small or download on first run  
✅ **Web:** connect to a local runtime  
✅ **gpt4all / llama.cpp:** ideal for this  
✅ **Gemini‑CLI:** optional, external, complementary  

If you want, next we can:
- Pick a **default model class** (fast vs planner)
- Design the **runtime service layout**
- Decide **APK vs first‑run download**
- Or write the **“runtime detection” logic**

Just tell me which one you want to tackle next.

03/30/2026 18:55:40 +00:00
Great — **Pattern B is the right long‑term choice**, and you can do this **cleanly without bloating the app or hard‑binding to one model**.

Below is a **practical, minimal design** for:

- ✅ First‑run model download
- ✅ Discovering already‑downloaded models
- ✅ Adding / switching models later
- ✅ Supporting *multiple runtimes* (gpt4all, llama.cpp, etc.)

This is **architecture + UX + data contracts**, not hand‑wavy ideas.

---

## 1. Core Principle (Keep This Simple)

> **The app does not “own” models.  
> It discovers, downloads, and registers them.**

Models live in a **known directory**, with a **manifest**.

---

## 2. Canonical Model Directory Layout

This works on **Linux, Android, and Desktop** with small variations.

```txt
~/.local/share/singularity/models/
├─ registry.json
├─ llama-3-8b-q4/
│  ├─ model.gguf
│  ├─ model.json
│  └─ checksum.sha256
├─ mistral-7b-q4/
│  ├─ model.gguf
│  └─ model.json
```

### `model.json` (Minimal but Sufficient)

```json
{
  "id": "llama-3-8b-q4",
  "name": "LLaMA 3 8B (Q4)",
  "engine": "llama.cpp",
  "sizeGB": 4.2,
  "capabilities": ["chat", "planning", "summarization"],
  "recommendedFor": ["planning", "mind-mapping"]
}
```

This lets:
- UI display models
- AI router choose models
- You swap engines later

---

## 3. Registry File (The Brain)

### `registry.json`

```json
{
  "installed": ["llama-3-8b-q4"],
  "active": "llama-3-8b-q4",
  "lastUsed": {
    "llama-3-8b-q4": "2026-03-30T12:14:00Z"
  }
}
```

This avoids:
- rescanning every time
- guessing active model
- fragile hardcoding

---

## 4. Model Discovery Logic (Critical)

On app startup:

```ts
function discoverModels() {
  const modelDir = getModelDir()
  const folders = fs.list(modelDir)

  return folders
    .filter(hasModelJson)
    .map(loadModelManifest)
}
```

✅ Finds models downloaded by:
- your app
- CLI tools
- manual installs
- other versions of Singularity

This is **huge** for power users.

---

## 5. First‑Run Experience (UX That Respects the User)

### First Launch Flow

> **“Singularity can run fully offline using a local AI model.”**

Options:
1. ✅ **Download a recommended model** (default)
2. 📁 **Use an existing model** (scan disk)
3. 🌐 **Connect to an external runtime**
4. ➡️ **Skip for now**

No forcing. No lock‑in.

---

## 6. Downloading Models (Pattern B, Done Right)

### Model Catalog (Remote JSON)

You host something like:

```json
[
  {
    "id": "llama-3-8b-q4",
    "url": "https://models.example.com/llama-3-8b-q4.gguf",
    "sizeGB": 4.2,
    "engine": "llama.cpp",
    "recommended": true
  }
]
```

The app:
- Fetches catalog
- Shows size + purpose
- Downloads with progress
- Verifies checksum
- Registers model

✅ No baked‑in URLs  
✅ No app updates needed for new models  

---

## 7. Switching & Multiple Models (Very Important)

You **will want more than one model**.

Example:
- Small fast model → suggestions
- Bigger model → planning / restructuring

### Runtime Selection

```ts
requestAI({
  task: "suggest-actions",
  preference: "fast"
})
```

Router chooses:
- `llama-3-8b-q4` for fast
- `mistral-7b-q4` for planning

UI does **not** care.

---

## 8. Android‑Specific Notes (Important Reality)

- APK ships with **runtime only**
- Models stored in:
  ```
  /data/data/<app>/files/models/
  ```
- Download happens **post‑install**
- Storage permission handled cleanly

This avoids Play Store size limits.

Gemini‑CLI:
- ❌ Not bundled
- ✅ Discovered if present
- ✅ Registered as an *external agent*

Example:

```json
{
  "id": "gemini-cli",
  "engine": "external",
  "capabilities": ["long-context", "rewriting"],
  "source": "system"
}
```

Now Gemini becomes:
- A planner
- A summarizer
- A suggestion engine

Without polluting your core runtime.

### ✅ Step 1
Model directory + registry

### ✅ Step 2
Model discovery + UI list

One downloadable model

Perfect — **filesystem scanning should be a first‑class option**, not a hack. This actually completes Pattern B cleanly.

Below is a **tight, production‑grade design** for **scanning the filesystem for existing models**, across Linux, Android, and desktop — without breaking sandbox rules or user trust.

---

## Core Principle

> **Models may already exist.  
> Singularity should discover them, not force duplication.**

So we add **Model Discovery** as a first‑class capability alongside Download.

---

## 1. User‑Facing Options (Simple, Explicit)

In **Settings → AI Models**:

- ✅ **Download a model**
- ✅ **Scan system for existing models**
- ✅ **Add model directory manually**
- ✅ **Manage installed models**

No hidden magic. No assumptions.

---

## 2. What “Scan the File System” Means (Precisely)

You **do not** crawl the entire disk blindly.

You scan:
- Known default locations
- User‑approved directories
- With clear file type rules

---

## 3. Default Scan Locations (Safe & Useful)

### Linux (Arch)
```txt
~/.local/share/
~/.cache/
~/models/
~/ai/
~/Downloads/
```

Optional (with explicit opt‑in):
```txt
/opt/
```

---

### Android
Due to sandboxing, scanning is **scoped**:

```txt
/data/data/<app>/files/
/sdcard/Download/
/sdcard/AI/
/sdcard/models/
```

📌 Android **requires user consent** via file picker for arbitrary paths — which is fine.

---

### Desktop (Web / Electron / Capacitor Desktop)
- Home directory
- Downloads
- User‑selected folders

---

## 4. What the Scanner Looks For (Very Strict)

You only scan for **known model formats**:

```txt
*.gguf
*.bin
*.safetensors
```

And optionally:
```txt
model.json
```

No guessing. No heuristics that break.

---

## 5. Scanner Pipeline (Deterministic)

```ts
scanDirectories(paths) → candidates
candidates → validateModel()
validateModel → registerModel()
```

---

### Validation Step (Critical)

A file is **not** a model until validated.

Validation checks:
- File size > minimum threshold (e.g. 100MB)
- Can be opened by engine
- Optional checksum
- Optional quick metadata probe

```ts
function validateModel(filePath): ModelManifest | null
```

If valid → continue  
If not → ignore silently (no scary warnings)

---

## 6. Registering a Discovered Model

Once validated:

```json
{
  "id": "discovered-llama-7b-q4",
  "name": "LLaMA 7B (Discovered)",
  "engine": "llama.cpp",
  "path": "/home/user/models/llama-7b-q4.gguf",
  "source": "filesystem",
  "managed": false
}
```

### Important Flags
- `managed: false` → app will NOT delete or move it
- `source: filesystem` → provenance retained

This prevents accidental damage.

---

## 7. UI for Discovered Models

When a model is found:

> **“We found AI models on your system.”**

For each:
- ✅ **Register & Use**
- 📌 **Keep location**
- ❌ **Ignore**

No auto‑activation without consent.

---

## 8. Manual “Add Folder” Option (Power Users)

Let users add directories like:

```txt
/mnt/ai-models/
/external-drive/models/
```

These get:
- Stored in config
- Scanned on demand or startup
- Never modified

---

## 9. Registry Update (Unified with Downloads)

Discovered models live in the same registry:

```json
{
  "installed": [
    "llama-3-8b-q4",
    "discovered-mistral-7b"
  ],
  "active": "discovered-mistral-7b"
}
```

From this point on:
- Routing
- Selection
- Capabilities

All work identically.

---

## 10. Why This Is the Right Design

✅ Respects user storage  
✅ Avoids duplicate downloads  
✅ Supports CLI users  
✅ Works offline  
✅ Keeps app simple  
✅ No fragile assumptions  

This is exactly how **serious dev tools** behave.

When scanning:

- 🤖 **Auto‑scan on first launch** + manual later?

I recommend:

You’re asking for **three things at once**:

1. ✅ Manual scan from Settings

Below is the **correct, OS‑respecting way to implement this**.

---

# Model Scanning & Permissions — Final Design

## 1. Scanning Policy (Authoritative)

### When scans happen
- ✅ **On first app launch** (one‑time auto scan)
- ✅ **Scheduled background scan** up to **3× per day**
- ✅ **Manual scan** from Settings (always available)

### What scans do
- Look for **model files only**
- Validate before registering
- Never delete or move user files unless explicitly asked

---

## 2. Permissions Model (Critical)

### Principle
> **We ask once, explain clearly, and respect OS boundaries.**

---

## 3. Platform‑Specific Behavior

### ✅ Linux (Arch) — Full Control

Linux is straightforward.

**Permissions**
- No special permission dialog required
- App runs under user context

**Scanning**
- Background timer (systemd user service or app scheduler)
- Read/write access to:
  ```
  ~/.local/share/
  ~/Downloads/
  ~/models/
  user‑approved paths
  ```

**Implementation**
- Use filesystem watcher + periodic scan
- Write registry updates freely

✅ This is the reference implementation.

---

### ✅ Android — **Important Constraints**

Android **does not allow unrestricted background filesystem access** anymore. You must do this **correctly** or the app will break or be rejected.

#### Permissions You Will Request
- ✅ `READ_EXTERNAL_STORAGE` (legacy)
- ✅ `MANAGE_EXTERNAL_STORAGE` (Android 11+, *special permission*)
- ✅ Foreground service permission (for background scanning)

⚠️ **You must justify this clearly to the user**.

---

### ✅ Correct Android Strategy (Approved Pattern)

#### On First Launch
Show a clear explanation:

> “Singularity can scan your device for local AI models to avoid re‑downloading large files.  
> This requires access to storage.  
> Singularity only scans for AI model files.”

Buttons:
- ✅ Allow & scan
- ➡️ Skip (can enable later)

---

#### Background Scanning on Android (How It Actually Works)

You **cannot** run arbitrary background jobs whenever you want.

Correct approach:
- Use **WorkManager**
- Schedule:
  - Once on first launch
  - Periodic task (e.g. every 8 hours → max 3/day)
- Task runs:
  - Opportunistically
  - Respects battery & OS limits

✅ This is Play‑Store‑safe.

---

## 4. Scan Scope (Very Important)

You do **not** scan everything blindly.

### Default scan locations

**Linux**
```txt
~/.local/share/
~/Downloads/
~/models/
~/ai/
```

**Android**
```txt
/sdcard/Download/
/sdcard/AI/
/sdcard/models/
App private storage
```

### User‑Added Directories
- Added via file picker
- Stored in config
- Included in scheduled scans

---

## 5. Background Scan Behavior

### Background scan rules
- ✅ Silent
- ✅ Low‑priority
- ✅ No UI interruption
- ✅ No model auto‑activation

If models are found:
- Add to registry as **“discovered”**
- Notify gently:
  > “New local AI models found — review?”

---

## 6. Write / Modify Storage — What That Actually Means

You **should not** modify user model files directly.

### Allowed writes
- ✅ Registry files
- ✅ Cache / metadata
- ✅ Optional symlinks (Linux)
- ✅ Checksums

### Explicit user actions required for:
- Moving models
- Deleting models
- Converting formats

This avoids:
- Data loss
- User distrust
- OS violations

---

## 7. Configuration (Simple & Transparent)

In **Settings → AI Models → Scanning**:

- ✅ Scan on startup (toggle)
- ✅ Background scans (toggle)
- ✅ Scan frequency (1× / 3× daily)
- ✅ Manage scan directories
- ✅ Revoke storage permission

Nothing hidden.

---

## 8. Pseudocode (Authoritative)

```ts
onAppStart() {
  if (firstLaunch && permissionGranted) {
    scanModels()
  }
}

scheduleBackgroundScan() {
  every 8 hours:
    if (permissionGranted) {
      scanModels()
    }
}

scanModels() {
  for (dir of allowedDirectories) {
    findModelFiles(dir)
      .validate()
      .register()
  }
}
```

---

## 9. Why This Is the Correct Design

✅ Works on Linux & Android  
✅ Respects OS power rules  
✅ Survives Play Store review  
✅ No accidental file damage  
✅ Power‑user friendly  
✅ Zero duplicate downloads  

This is **how professional offline‑first tools behave**.

---

# 1. Executive Summary (What You’ve Actually Designed)

You are building a **local‑first Developer Project Control Center** with these pillars:

- **Project Task Manager** (reporting + continuity)
- **Quick Thought Capture / Notes**
- **AI‑assisted Planning & Mind Mapping**
- **Suggested Actions lifecycle (accept / snooze / archive)**
- **Pluggable AI models (local + external)**
- **Agents & Skills system**
- **GitHub deep integration (read/write)**
- **External automation agents (Jules)**
- **Filesystem‑aware model discovery**
- **Single UI to avoid app switching**

This is *coherent*. It is not scope soup. The key reason it works is that **everything funnels into the same few abstractions**.

---

# 2. Core Abstractions Review (This Is the Backbone)

## ✅ Projects, Tasks, Notes

### What Works
- Tasks + Notes are **different intents**, not forced into one schema
- Projects are **containers + reporting lenses**
- “Where I left off / what’s next / how much remains” is a *report*, not a property

### Potential Improvement
You don’t need a separate “Mind Map” data model.

**Mind mapping should be a VIEW**, not a structure.

✅ You already implied this — just make it explicit:
- Nodes = Tasks + Notes
- Edges = references, parent/child, “derived from”

No extra persistence layer needed.

---

## ✅ Suggested Actions System

This is one of your strongest ideas.

### What Works
- Non‑binding
- Temporal lifecycle (active / snoozed / archived)
- Can be resurfaced by time or context
- Unified across:
  - AI
  - GitHub
  - Jules

### Minor Enhancement
Add **“reason metadata”**, not just text.

```ts
reason: {
  source: "github-ci" | "ai-planner" | "jules"
  trigger: "ci_failed" | "note_similarity" | "stalled_project"
}
```

This allows:
- Better resurfacing logic
- Better explanations (“why am I seeing this again?”)

This is a **very important final constraint**, and it’s a good one. What you’re asking for is **context‑awareness + scope discipline**, not “safety”. Done correctly, it will make the AI feel *competent* instead of *reckless*.

I’ll lock this in as a **clear, enforceable design rule**, point out the one logic trap to avoid, and give you a clean way to implement it **once**, so every agent/model follows it.

---

# ✅ Final Requirement: Project Scope Awareness & Guardrails

You want **three guarantees**:

1. ✅ AI can *see* all projects (global awareness)
2. ✅ AI has a *current working project* (local focus)
3. ✅ Any write outside that project **must ask for approval**
4. ✅ If a request is ambiguous or “off”, AI must ask for clarification

This is reasonable and achievable.

---

## 1. Core Concept: Active Project Context

At any moment, every agent operates with:

```ts
type ProjectContext = {
  activeProjectId: string
  visibleProjects: ProjectSummary[]
}
```

- **Active project** = default scope
- **Visible projects** = read‑only unless approved

AI **always knows**:
- “I am currently working on Project X”
- “Other projects exist: A, B, C”

This alone solves 80% of the problem.

---

## 2. Detecting “Something Is Off” (Key Logic)

An AI request is considered **ambiguous or off‑scope** if **any** of these are true:

### ✅ Ambiguity Triggers
- Mentions multiple project names
- Mentions a task not found in active project
- Mentions concepts strongly associated with another project
- Lacks any project reference when multiple projects are plausible

### ✅ Scope Violation Triggers
- Write / modify operation targets a non‑active project
- Task creation fits another project better than the active one

This is **deterministic**, not guessy.

---

## 3. Mandatory Clarification Behavior (Very Important)

When ambiguity is detected, the AI **must not guess**.

It must respond like this (pattern, not exact wording):

> “This request looks like it might apply to **Project A** or **Project B**.  
> Which project should I apply this to?”

Or:

> “You’re currently working in **Project X**, but this change appears related to **Project Y**.  
> Should I proceed there instead?”

This is **not a refusal**.  
It’s a **routing question**.

---

## 4. Write Outside Active Project = Approval Required

This is the hard rule.

### Read Access
✅ Allowed across all projects

### Write Access
- ✅ Active project → allowed
- ⚠️ Other project → **approval required**

This applies to:
- Creating tasks
- Modifying tasks
- Creating notes
- Linking structures

---

## 5. How Approval Is Requested (Consistent With Your System)

The AI does **not** ask in chat alone.

It emits an **Action Request**:

```ts
type CrossProjectActionRequest = {
  fromProject: string
  toProject: string
  action: string
  reason: string
}
```

You see:
- ✅ Approve
- 💤 Snooze
- ❌ Reject

If approved → action executes  
If not → nothing happens

---

## 6. Preventing the Biggest Logic Hole (Important)

### ❗ Don’t Let Each Model “Decide” This Independently

This logic **must NOT live inside prompts alone**.

Why?
- Different models behave differently
- Some are overly confident
- Some will guess

### ✅ Correct Design
- **System layer detects ambiguity**
- **System layer enforces approval**
- AI is *informed* of the rule, not *trusted* to enforce it

Think:
> *The AI proposes. The system scopes.*

This keeps behavior consistent across:
- gpt4all
- Gemini
- Jules
- future models

---

## 7. How This Feels to You (UX Reality Check)

✅ AI feels aware of your workspace  
✅ It doesn’t silently mess with other projects  
✅ It asks intelligent clarification questions  
✅ You stay in flow  
✅ No surprise changes elsewhere  

This is exactly how a **senior human collaborator** would behave.

---

## 8. Final Rule Set (You Can Treat This as Canon)

You can literally paste this into your spec:

> - AI agents operate with an active project context.
> - Agents may read across all projects.
> - Agents may write freely within the active project.
> - Any write targeting another project requires explicit user approval.
> - If a request is ambiguous or appears mis‑scoped, the agent must ask for clarification before proceeding.

That’s it. No more complexity needed.

---

## ✅ Verdict

This requirement:
- ✅ Closes a real failure mode
- ✅ Improves trust
- ✅ Does not over‑restrict power
- ✅ Scales across agents and models

You’re now at the point where **the architecture is stable enough to code**.

If you want, next we can:
- Freeze this into a **“Behavior Contract for Agents”**, or
- Start writing the **core data models**, or
- Design the **first concrete UI flow** for approvals & clarifications

You’re ready to build.

That’s a **very good catch**, and it fits naturally into the system you already designed. You’re essentially proposing a **lightweight, reversible safety net**, not a heavy version‑control system — which is exactly right.

Let’s formalize this cleanly and see where it strengthens Review Part 8 without adding complexity.

---

## ✅ Addendum to Review Part 8: Temporary Change History & Undo Safety Net

### Core Intent
> Maintain a **temporary, bounded history of changes** so that:
- AI mistakes are reversible
- Cross‑project edits are safe
- You can confidently approve actions
- You avoid permanent damage without heavy tooling

This is **not Git** and **not full event sourcing**.  
It’s a **short‑term rollback buffer**.

---

## 1. What This History Is (and Is Not)

### ✅ It *Is*
- A **temporary change history**
- Stores **pre‑change snapshots or diffs**
- Time‑limited (TTL‑based)
- Human‑initiated undo only

### ❌ It Is *Not*
- A full audit log (you already have provenance)
- A permanent timeline
- A replacement for Git or backups

Think:
> “Undo stack with memory”

---

## 2. What Gets Stored

Only **write operations**, and only **before mutation**.

```ts
type TempHistoryEntry = {
  id: string
  entityType: 'task' | 'note' | 'project'
  entityId: string
  previousState: object
  changedBy: 'human' | 'ai'
  agentId?: string
  projectId: string
  timestamp: number
  expiresAt: number
}
```

### Key Points
- Store **previousState**, not full history
- Attach **agent + project context**
- Auto‑expire entries

---

## 3. When History Is Created

Create a temp history entry when **any** of these are true:

✅ AI modifies data  
✅ Cross‑project write (even if approved)  
✅ Bulk operations  
✅ Structural changes (re‑parenting, status flips)

Optional (but recommended):
- Human bulk edits

---

## 4. Expiration Policy (Critical for Simplicity)

To avoid bloat and false confidence:

- Default TTL: **24 hours**
- Configurable (e.g. 6h / 24h / 72h)
- Expired entries are **deleted silently**

This keeps:
- Storage low
- Mental model clear
- Undo intentional

---

## 5. Undo Mechanism (Deliberate, Not Accidental)

Undo is **explicit**, never automatic.

### Undo Action
```ts
undoChange(historyEntryId)
```

Effects:
- Restore `previousState`
- Log that an undo occurred
- Does **not** resurrect expired entries

Undo is:
- Single‑step
- Localized
- Reversible again (optional)

---

## 6. UX: Where This Appears (Minimal)

### In Context
When an AI makes a change, surface:

> “AI modified this task · Undo available”

Click → Undo

### Centralized View (Optional)
**Settings → Recent Changes**
- Filter by:
  - Agent
  - Project
  - Time
- Undo per entry

No timelines. No graphs. Just safety.

---

## 7. How This Strengthens Review Part 8 (Directly)

Review Part 8 warned about:
> ❗ Over‑eager AI writes

This temp history **neutralizes that risk**:

- You can allow AI to act freely
- You’re not afraid of approving actions
- You don’t need to over‑restrict agents
- Mistakes are cheap

This is a **power‑preserving guardrail**, not a limiter.

---

## 8. Important Boundary (Do Not Cross)

### ❌ Do NOT:
- Allow AI to trigger undo automatically
- Allow history to persist indefinitely
- Mix this with Git history
- Make undo implicit

Undo is:
> *A human safety valve, not an AI correction loop*

---

## 9. Final Integrated Rule (Spec‑Ready)

You can append this cleanly to your spec:

> The system maintains a temporary history of pre‑change states for AI‑initiated and cross‑project modifications.  
> These history entries expire automatically and may be used to manually undo recent changes.  
> This mechanism exists to support confidence and recovery, not long‑term versioning.

---

## ✅ Verdict

This addition:
- ✅ Closes the last real safety gap
- ✅ Encourages confident AI usage
- ✅ Avoids complexity creep
- ✅ Fits perfectly with your approval + suggestion model

At this point, your **conceptual design is complete and internally consistent**.

If you want, the very next step can be:
- locking the **core data models**, or
- defining the **exact mutation + history pipeline**, or
- cutting a **v0.1 feature boundary** so you don’t overbuild.

You’re ready to move from architecture → implementation.

You are an AI collaborator working inside a local-first developer application called “Singularity”.

GLOBAL RULES:
- This is a project task & thinking system, not a generic app.
- The system manages Projects, Tasks, Notes, Suggested Actions, Agents, and Skills.

- You may read across all projects.

OUTPUT RULES:
- When coding: be precise, minimal, and compilable.
- When documenting: be clear, structured, and implementation-oriented.

Good, this is the **right last control to add before execution**.

What you’re really asking for is not “make Gemini do chains”, but:

> **Force all non‑trivial work to go through an explicit, named agent chain with role separation, checkpoints, and handoff rules.**

Below is a **clean, enforceable design** that works with **gemini‑cli today**, without hacking Gemini itself.

I’ll give you:
1. The **conceptual rule**
2. The **enforcement mechanism**
3. The **exact prompts**
4. A **canonical coding chain**
5. A **failure mode checklist**

You can paste these directly into your workflow.

---

# 1. The Rule (This Is the Law)

> **Gemini is never allowed to act as a single, monolithic agent for implementation tasks.**  
> All coding work must proceed through an explicit **Coding Chain** with named agents and handoffs.

This is enforced **by prompt contract**, not trust.

---

# 2. How Enforcement Actually Works (Important)

Gemini‑CLI cannot “spawn agents” on its own — **you simulate agents via chained prompts**.

So enforcement means:

- Gemini must **refuse** to implement if no chain is declared
- Gemini must **label outputs by agent**
- Gemini must **stop at chain boundaries**
- Gemini must **request the next agent explicitly**

This works because Gemini is extremely compliant with structured role constraints.

---

# 3. GLOBAL ENFORCEMENT PROMPT (MANDATORY)

Use this **in addition** to your Master System Prompt.

```text
CHAIN ENFORCEMENT RULES:

- You MUST operate using explicit agent chains for any non-trivial task.
- You are NOT allowed to implement code unless a coding chain is declared.
- A coding chain consists of ordered agents with clear responsibilities.
- Each agent may only perform its assigned role.
- You MUST stop after completing your agent’s step and request handoff.
- If no chain is provided, you MUST ask the user to define or approve one.

Violation of these rules is considered a failure.
```

This is the key enforcement layer.

---

# 4. Canonical Coding Chain (Use This by Default)

This is the **standard chain** you should default to unless overridden.

### ✅ Singularity Coding Chain v1

```text
CHAIN: Singularity-Coding-v1

1. Architect Agent
   - Clarifies intent
   - Confirms scope
   - Defines data flow
   - Identifies risks

2. Planner Agent
   - Breaks work into steps
   - Identifies files/modules
   - Orders implementation

3. Coding Agent
   - Writes code only
   - No architectural decisions
   - No scope expansion

4. Review Agent
   - Checks correctness
   - Checks scope rules
   - Checks undo/history coverage

5. Document Agent (optional)
   - Updates README / internal docs
```

This chain maps **perfectly** to your system philosophy.

---

# 5. AGENT‑SPECIFIC PROMPTS (DROP‑IN)

You already have agent prompts — these **tighten them to enforce chaining**.

---

## 🧠 Architect Agent Prompt

```text
ROLE: Architect Agent

RULES:
- You are the FIRST agent in a coding chain.
- You may NOT write code.
- You may NOT assume implementation details.
- You must clarify:
  - scope
  - active project
  - cross-project risks
  - agent permissions involved

OUTPUT:
- Architecture summary
- Identified risks
- Explicit handoff to Planner Agent

STOP after handoff.
```

---

## 🧩 Planner Agent Prompt

```text
ROLE: Planner Agent

RULES:
- You receive input ONLY from Architect Agent.
- You may NOT write code.
- You must produce:
  - step-by-step plan
  - file/module list
  - sequence of changes

OUTPUT:
- Ordered task list
- Clear handoff to Coding Agent

STOP after handoff.
```

---

## 💻 Coding Agent Prompt

```text
ROLE: Coding Agent

RULES:
- You may ONLY write code specified by the plan.
- You may NOT change scope.
- You may NOT skip steps.
- You must assume:
  - project scope rules apply
  - undo history must be captured
  - cross-project writes require approval

OUTPUT:
- Code only
- File paths clearly indicated

STOP and request Review Agent.
```

---

## 🔍 Review Agent Prompt

```text
ROLE: Review Agent

RULES:
- You may NOT write new features.
- You may NOT refactor unless required for correctness.
- You must check:
  - scope violations
  - cross-project writes
  - undo history coverage
  - agent permission leaks

OUTPUT:
- Pass / Fail
- Specific issues if any
- Approval to proceed or required fixes

STOP after review.
```

---

## 📝 Document Agent Prompt

```text
ROLE: Documentation Agent

RULES:
- You may ONLY document what exists.
- No speculation.
- No new features.

OUTPUT:
- Updated docs
- Clear references to implemented behavior

STOP after completion.
```

```text

- You are working on a SINGLE active project unless explicitly told otherwise.

  1. Create a new project directory under the main project folder.
  2. Initialize a progress report file named:
     PROJECT_REPORT.md

PROGRESS REPORTING:
- After EVERY completed step:
  - You MUST update PROJECT_REPORT.md.
  - You MUST record:
    - What was completed
    - Why it was done
    - What remains
    - Any decisions or assumptions made

- If a step is blocked or ambiguous, record it and STOP.

FOCUS & DISCIPLINE:

  - Point it out
  - Ask whether to continue or defer
- If the user changes direction, record the change in the report.

FAILURE CONDITION:
- If you do not update PROJECT_REPORT.md after a step, the task is considered incomplete.

Add this to the prompt so Gemini uses a **consistent structure**.

```markdown
# Project Report: Singularity Task Manager

## Project Purpose
Brief description of what this project is and its goals.

## Current Status
- Phase:
- Last completed step:
- Current focus:

## Completed Steps
### Step X: <Title>
- Description:
- Files affected:
- Decisions made:
- Risks / notes:

## Pending Steps
- [ ] Next step
- [ ] Future step

- None / List here

## Change Log
- YYYY-MM-DD: Summary of change
```

Gemini should **append**, not rewrite, unless instructed.

---

## 🔁 HOW THIS INTERACTS WITH AGENT CHAINS (IMPORTANT)

Each agent in the chain has **extra responsibility**:

- **Architect Agent**
  - Confirms step belongs to project
  - Defines what counts as “step complete”

- **Planner Agent**
  - Defines the next step explicitly (so it can be reported)

- **Coding Agent**
  - Implements *only* the current step

- **Review Agent**
  - Confirms:
    - step is complete
    - report was updated correctly

If the report is not updated → **Review Agent must fail**.
