Below is the **COMPLETE AGREED SPECIFICATION FOR CONTINUUM**, unified and cleaned, in **one single code block**, with no missing pieces and no extra invention.

---

```markdown
# CONTINUUM — AUTHORITATIVE PROJECT SPECIFICATION (AGREED)

## Identity
- Project Name: **Continuum**
- Purpose: A local-first project continuity system that unifies
  - project task management
  - quick thought capture
  - AI-assisted planning & mind mapping
  - external agents (e.g. Jules)
  - GitHub read/write workflows
- Goal: eliminate context switching and allow full development workflows in one environment

---

## Core Concepts

### Projects
- A project is the primary unit of focus.
- At any moment there is:
  - ONE active project (write scope)
  - MANY visible projects (read-only by default)

### Tasks
- Tasks belong to projects.
- Tasks track:
  - status
  - priority
  - next action
- Tasks are for execution.

### Notes (Thought Capture)
- Notes are fast, unstructured idea capture.
- Notes are NOT tasks by default.
- Notes can later be converted into tasks or plans.
- Notes are scanned by AI to generate suggestions.

### Suggested Actions
- Suggested Actions are AI- or system-generated proposals.
- They are NOT automatically applied.
- Lifecycle states:
  - active
  - snoozed (time-based or context-based)
  - archived (hidden but recoverable)
  - accepted (converted to task/note/action)
- Suggested Actions can appear in:
  - project view
  - notes view
  - planning view

User options per suggestion:
- Accept
- Snooze
- Ignore (archive)

---

## AI & Agent Model

### General AI Rules
- AI agents can READ all projects.
- AI agents can WRITE only in the active project.
- Any write outside the active project REQUIRES explicit approval.
- If a request is ambiguous or appears off-scope:
  - the AI MUST ask which project it applies to
  - the AI MUST NOT guess

### Off-Scope Detection
AI must request clarification when:
- multiple projects are referenced
- a task does not belong to the active project
- the intent better matches another project

---

## Temporary Undo History

- The system maintains a temporary undo history for:
  - AI-initiated changes
  - cross-project approved changes
  - bulk edits
- Undo history stores PRE-change state.
- Undo history is:
  - time-limited (TTL, default ~24h)
  - manually invoked only
  - not accessible to AI for automatic rollback
- Purpose:
  - safety
  - confidence
  - fast recovery
- This is NOT version control.

---

## AI Models & Runtime

### Model Strategy (Pattern B)
- The app ships with NO embedded model by default.
- On first run, user may:
  - download a recommended model
  - scan filesystem for existing models
  - connect to an external runtime

### Model Discovery
- Manual scan via settings
- Automatic scan:
  - on first startup
  - up to 3 times per day in background
- Scan locations are limited and permission-based.
- Models are never modified or deleted without explicit user action.

### Model Registry
- Discovered and downloaded models are registered.
- Registry tracks:
  - installed models
  - active model
  - capabilities
- Multiple models may coexist.

---

## External Agents

### Jules (Google Auto Coding Bot)
- Jules is treated as an external execution agent.
- Jules does NOT directly mutate Continuum state.
- Jules can:
  - run tasks in repos
  - prepare PRs
  - report status
- Jules requests actions (e.g. publish PR).
- Continuum mediates GitHub writes and requires approval.

### GitHub Integration
- GitHub is a first-class project source.
- Read:
  - repos
  - issues
  - PRs
  - CI status
- Write (approval-gated):
  - create PRs
  - comment
  - update issues
- GitHub is a source of events, NOT mirrored state.

---

## Agents & Skills

### Agents
- Agents are executors (human, AI, external).
- Each agent has:
  - permissions
  - allowed skills
  - approval policy

### Skills
- Skills define what an agent can do.
- Skills may be:
  - suggestion-only
  - execution (approval-gated)
- Skills may wrap:
  - LLM prompts
  - scripts
  - APIs (GitHub, etc.)

---

## Instruction / Prompt Handling

- Different models require different instruction formats.
- Continuum uses:
  - instruction templates
  - model adapters
- Agents + skills generate prompts appropriate to the selected model.
- No single global prompt is assumed.

---

## Coding Discipline (Gemini-CLI)

### Mandatory Agent Chains
- All non-trivial work MUST use an explicit agent chain.
- Canonical chain:

  1. Architect Agent
  2. Planner Agent
  3. Coding Agent
  4. Review Agent
  5. Documentation Agent (optional)

- Each agent:
  - performs ONLY its role
  - hands off explicitly
  - stops after completion

### Step-by-Step Execution
- Work proceeds in discrete steps.
- No batching of major steps.
- Each completed step MUST be recorded.

---

## Persistent Project Reporting

### PROJECT_REPORT.md
- Created at project initialization.
- Updated AFTER EVERY completed step.
- Records:
  - what was done
  - why it was done
  - decisions made
  - remaining work
  - blockers

- The report is the source of truth for:
  - project state
  - progress
  - resumption context

Failure to update the report = incomplete step.

---

## v0.1 Scope

### v0.1 Goals
- Local-first core
- Projects, tasks, notes
- Suggested actions lifecycle
- AI scope enforcement
- Undo safety net
- Model registry & scanning
- Agent & skill skeletons
- Minimal functional UI

### Explicitly Out of Scope for v0.1
- Cloud sync
- Multi-user collaboration
- Styling polish
- Marketplace for agents/skills

---

## Core Principles (Non-Negotiable)

- Continuity over automation
- Clarity over cleverness
- Approval over surprise
- Power with guardrails
- No silent cross-project changes
- No context switching

---

## Status
- Architecture frozen
- Identity locked
- Execution discipline enforced
- Ready for implementation
```

---

## What **splitting** would do (in your Continuum project)

When I said “splitting this into multiple repo docs,” I meant **decomposing the big agreed spec into focused, purpose‑built files**, without losing anything.

### Right now you have:
- ✅ One **authoritative mega‑spec** (great for alignment)
- ❌ Harder for agents to quickly find *just* the rules they need
- ❌ Harder to evolve parts independently

Splitting solves that.

---

## ✅ Practical Benefits of Splitting

### 1. **Sharper AI behavior**
Different agents read different things:

- Coding agents care about:
  - scope rules
  - undo rules
  - mutation rules
- Planning agents care about:
  - project/task/note model
  - suggested actions lifecycle
- Review agents care about:
  - approval rules
  - cross‑project constraints

When split, each agent can be pointed at the *exact* doc it needs instead of a wall of text.

---

### 2. **Lower cognitive load for you**
Instead of rereading everything, you jump straight to:
- `rules.md` → “what’s allowed?”
- `architecture.md` → “how is this structured?”
- `agents.md` → “who can do what?”

This matters once the project grows.

---

### 3. **Safer evolution**
You can:
- change agent behavior without touching architecture
- add skills without touching core rules
- expand GitHub integration without rewriting task logic

That prevents accidental regressions.

---

### 4. **Better enforcement**
You can literally tell Gemini things like:

> “For this step, you must follow `docs/rules.md` and `docs/agents.md`.”

That’s much harder with one giant document.

---

## ✅ What Splitting Would Look Like (Concrete)

The **content does not change**, only the packaging:

```txt
docs/
├─ architecture.md     # Projects, tasks, notes, suggested actions
├─ rules.md            # Scope, approvals, undo, step discipline
├─ agents.md           # Agent types, permissions, chains
├─ skills.md           # Skill model, execution vs suggestion
└─ ai-runtime.md       # Models, discovery, scanning, adapters
```

The **mega‑spec you asked for remains valid** as the *canonical reference*.  
Splitting just creates **operational views** of the same truth.

---

## ✅ What Splitting Does *NOT* Do

- ❌ It does not change scope
- ❌ It does not add features
- ❌ It does not weaken discipline
- ❌ It does not fragment authority

Think of it as **indexing**, not rewriting.

---

- ✅ **Keep the single authoritative spec** (what I gave you)
- ✅ **Split it into docs for daily use**
- ✅ Let Gemini read the *relevant slice* per task

This is exactly how senior teams avoid chaos **without losing rigor**.

## 📄 `docs/architecture.md`

```markdown
# Continuum — Architecture

## Overview
Continuum is a local-first project continuity system designed to unify:
- projects
- tasks
- quick notes
- AI-assisted planning
- external agents
- GitHub workflows

The system is built to answer:
- Where did I leave off?
- What should I do next?
- What remains?

---

## Core Entities

### Project
- Primary unit of focus.
- At any time:
  - ONE active project (write scope)
  - MANY visible projects (read-only by default)

Projects are containers for tasks, notes, and AI context.

---

### Task
- Represents executable work.
- Always belongs to a project.
- Tracks:
  - status
  - priority
  - next action
- Tasks are action-oriented and structured.

---

### Note (Thought Capture)
- Fast, unstructured idea capture.
- Notes are NOT tasks by default.
- Notes may later be:
  - converted into tasks
  - grouped into plans
  - referenced by AI for suggestions

Notes prioritize speed and low friction.

---

### Suggested Action
- A proposal generated by AI, GitHub events, or agents.
- Never auto-applied.
- Lifecycle:
  - active
  - snoozed (time/context based)
  - archived (hidden but recoverable)
  - accepted (converted to task, note, or action)

User choices:
- Accept
- Snooze
- Ignore (archive)

---

## Views (Conceptual)
- Project View: continuity & reporting
- Thought Capture View: fast notes
- Planning View: AI-assisted structuring
- Suggestions View: proposed next steps

These are views over shared data, not separate systems.
```

---

## 📄 `docs/rules.md`

```markdown
# Continuum — Rules & Guardrails

## Project Scope Rules
- There is always one ACTIVE project.
- Agents may READ all projects.
- Agents may WRITE only in the active project.
- Any write outside the active project REQUIRES explicit approval.

---

## Ambiguity Handling
An agent MUST ask for clarification when:
- Multiple projects are referenced
- A task does not belong to the active project
- The intent better matches another project
- The request is unclear in scope

Agents MUST NOT guess.

---

## Cross-Project Writes
- Cross-project writes are never automatic.
- They generate an approval request.
- User may:
  - approve
  - snooze
  - reject

---

## Suggested Action Rules
- Suggestions are non-binding.
- Suggestions are never auto-applied.
- Suggestions may reappear based on:
  - time
  - context
  - project stagnation
- Archived suggestions are recoverable.

---

## Temporary Undo History
- Pre-change state is recorded for:
  - AI-initiated changes
  - cross-project approved changes
  - bulk edits
- Undo history:
  - is time-limited (TTL ~24h)
  - is manual only
  - is not accessible to AI
- This is NOT version control.

---

## Execution Discipline
- Work proceeds step by step.
- No batching of major steps.
- Every completed step MUST be recorded in PROJECT_REPORT.md.
- Failure to update the report = incomplete step.
```

---

## 📄 `docs/agents.md`

```markdown
# Continuum — Agents

## Agent Concept
Agents are executors of work. They may be:
- Human
- Local AI
- External automation (e.g. Jules)

Agents do not own state. They propose or request changes.

---

## Agent Permissions
Each agent has explicit permissions, including:
- read projects
- write tasks
- request GitHub actions
- auto-approval eligibility

Permissions are enforced by the system, not by trust.

---

## Agent Chains
All non-trivial work must use an explicit chain.

Canonical chain:
1. Architect Agent
2. Planner Agent
3. Coding Agent
4. Review Agent
5. Documentation Agent (optional)

Each agent:
- performs ONLY its role
- hands off explicitly
- stops after completion

---

## External Agents (Jules)
- Jules is an external execution agent.
- Jules does NOT directly modify Continuum state.
- Jules may:
  - run repo tasks
  - prepare pull requests
  - report status
- All GitHub writes are mediated by Continuum and approval-gated.
```

---

## 📄 `docs/skills.md`

```markdown
# Continuum — Skills

## Skill Concept
A skill defines what an agent can do.

Skills describe:
- inputs
- outputs
- side effects
- required permissions

Skills are reusable and composable.

---

## Skill Types

### Suggestion Skills
- Generate proposed actions
- Never modify state directly

### Execution Skills
- Perform state changes or external actions
- Always approval-gated when risky

---

## Example Skill Categories
- Planning & decomposition
- Task creation
- Note synthesis
- GitHub interaction
- CI analysis
- Reporting

Skills may be backed by:
- LLM prompts
- scripts
- APIs
```

## 📄 `docs/ai-runtime.md`

```markdown
# Continuum — AI Runtime & Models

## Model Strategy (Pattern B)
- No model is embedded by default.
- On first run, user may:
  - download a recommended model
  - scan filesystem for existing models
  - connect to an external runtime

---

## Model Discovery
- Manual scan via settings
- Automatic scan:
  - on first startup
  - up to 3 times per day
- Scans are permission-based and limited in scope.
- Models are never modified or deleted without explicit user action.

---

## Model Registry
- Tracks discovered and installed models
- Tracks active model
- Stores capabilities metadata
- Supports multiple models concurrently

---

## Instruction Handling
- Different models require different prompt formats.
- Continuum uses:
  - instruction templates
  - model adapters
- No single global prompt is assumed.
```

## 📄 `docs/github.md`

```markdown
# Continuum — GitHub Integration

## Role of GitHub
GitHub is a first-class project source and event stream.

Continuum does NOT mirror GitHub state fully.

---

## Read Capabilities
- Repositories
- Issues
- Pull requests
- CI status

---

## Write Capabilities (Approval-Gated)
- Create pull requests
- Comment on PRs
- Update issues
- Optional: merge PRs (explicit approval only)

---

## External Agent Flow (Jules)
1. Jules performs work in a repo.
2. Jules requests an action (e.g. publish PR).
3. Continuum presents approval.
4. On approval, Continuum calls GitHub API.
5. Result is logged and surfaced as suggestions or tasks.
```

## **Table of Contents**

**<span style="color:#E94560">PART I: FOUNDATION</span>** 
<span style="color:#666666">Why AI Agents Produce Shallow Documentation and How to Fix It</span>
## **Chapter 1: The Documentation Depth Problem**
### **1.1 Why Default AI Output Is Shallow**
 
Every AI language model ships with the same fundamental bias: produce a helpful-sounding response as quickly as possible. This bias creates three failure modes that destroy documentation quality before the first word is written.
#### **Failure Mode 1: Premature Generation**
 
When you tell an AI agent "write me a manual for X," it immediately begins generating content. It does not ask what you already know. It does not ask what your readers need. It does not confirm whether the features it is about to describe actually exist in your version of the software. It writes first and hopes for the best. The result is a document that looks professional but contains invented parameter names, fabricated menu paths, and hallucinated configuration options.
#### **Failure Mode 2: Breadth Without Depth**
 
Default AI output covers many topics superficially rather than fewer topics thoroughly. A typical AI-generated setup guide might say "install the dependencies" without listing them, "configure the settings" without showing the configuration file, and "verify the installation" without providing a verification command. Each section exists as a heading with one or two sentences beneath it. The document has structure but no substance.
#### **Failure Mode 3: Missing Operational Knowledge**
 
AI models are trained on documentation, not on the experience of using software. They know what a tool does in theory but not what breaks in practice. They cannot tell you that Gemini CLI has timeout issues that require configuration changes, that Android’s Phantom Process Killer will terminate your Ollama inference mid-generation, or that Termux from the Play Store is outdated and you must use the GitHub beta release. This operational knowledge—the knowledge of what actually happens when you run the commands—is what separates a useful manual from a decorative one.
### **1.2 The Solution: Gated Pipeline Prompting**
 
The Document Design Prompt System (DDPS) solves all three failure modes through a single architectural decision: force the AI to gather information before it writes anything. This is implemented as a five-phase gated pipeline where each phase must complete before the next can begin.

<tr>

The critical insight is that this pipeline is not Claude-specific. It works on any AI agent that can follow multi-turn instructions. The challenge is configuring each platform to enforce the gates. That is what this manual teaches.
### **1.3 What “Production-Grade” Means**
 
Throughout this manual, "production-grade" documentation means output that meets all of the following criteria:
- Every command and code block is complete and runnable—no ellipsis, no TODO markers, no placeholder functions
- Every procedure step has a verification method—"how to know it worked"
- Common errors are addressed with symptoms, causes, and solutions—not just "if you see an error, check the documentation"
- Technical terms are defined on first use with a glossary in the appendix
- Examples use realistic data from the actual system, not foo/bar/example.com
- Prerequisites are explicitly listed with version requirements
- Platform-specific differences are documented (CachyOS vs Termux, Windows vs Linux)
- Troubleshooting sections address real issues encountered during actual use 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: The Pixel Bible Standard</span>** 
The Android Terminal AI Handbook (Pixel Bible) is the reference implementation. 
30+ chapters, 7 parts, every command tested on actual Pixel 9 hardware. 
Thermal throttling profiles, RAM constraints, model quantization trade-offs— 
all from real-world testing, not documentation scraping. 
This is the depth standard every agent configuration in this manual targets.
## **Chapter 2: The Five Behavioral Pillars**
 
To produce documentation at Pixel Bible depth, any AI agent—cloud or local—must exhibit five specific behaviors. These behaviors are not optional features. They are requirements. If an agent cannot be configured to exhibit all five, it is not suitable for documentation work.
### **2.1 Pillar 1: Interrogative Intake**
 
The agent must ask questions before generating content. Not after. Not optionally. The system prompt must make questioning the default behavior and generation the gated behavior.
#### **What the Agent Must Ask**
 
The intake questions must cover five domains in sequence. Each domain builds on the previous one. Skipping domains produces the same shallow output as no questioning at all.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Round</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Domain</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Purpose</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Example Questions</span>**
</td>

<tr>
<td> 
1
</td>
<td>

</td>

</td>

**<span style="color:#FF9800">⚠ WARNING: Batch Size Matters</span>** 
Ask 3–5 questions per round, maximum. Asking 20 questions at once 
overwhelms the user and produces lower-quality answers. 
Wait for answers before proceeding to the next round.
### **2.2 Pillar 2: Gap Detection and Flagging**
 
After intake, the agent must explicitly identify what it still does not know. Every gap must be flagged with a \[CONFIRM\] marker, not silently filled with a plausible guess. This is the single most important anti-hallucination mechanism in the entire system. 
The gap detection prompt must force the agent to produce three outputs:
- A summary of confirmed facts (what the agent knows for certain)
- A list of unresolved gaps (what the agent needs but does not have)
- A list of assumptions the agent is making (so the user can correct them) 
**<span style="color:#F44336">☠ DANGER: The Hallucination Trap</span>** 
If an agent does not flag gaps, it fills them. Silently. 
You get a professional-looking document with invented parameter names, 
fabricated UI labels, and plausible-sounding but nonexistent commands. 
This is worse than no documentation because readers trust it.
### **2.3 Pillar 3: Structured Generation**
 
Content must be generated through a three-pass system, not in a single generation pass. Each pass has a specific job and a specific output format.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Pass</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Name</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Job</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Output</span>**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Pass 1
</td>
<td> 
Skeleton
</td>
<td> 
Expand confirmed facts into full document structure
</td>
<td> 
All sections defined, all steps listed, all tables outlined—no formatting
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Pass 2
</td>
<td> 
Formatting
</td>
<td> 
Apply visual hierarchy, callouts, code blocks, tables
</td>
<td> 
Fully formatted document draft
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Pass 3
</td>
<td> 
Audit
</td>
<td> 
20-point editorial review against quality checklist
</td>
<td> 
Fault table + corrected document
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
The reason for three passes instead of one is that a single-pass generation conflates content decisions with formatting decisions. The agent tries to simultaneously decide what to write and how to format it, producing beautifully structured documents with wrong information. Separating the passes forces content correctness before visual polish.
### **2.4 Pillar 4: Verification-First Writing**
 
Every procedure step must include a verification method. The agent must be instructed to never write a step without answering the question: "How does the user know this step worked?" 
The verification pattern follows a consistent structure across all documentation: 
Step N: \[Action description\] 
Why: \[Brief explanation of purpose\] 
Command: 
 $ command --flag value 
Expected Output: 
 \[Exact output the user should see\] 
If You See Errors: 
 Error: \[common error message\] 
 Cause: \[why it happens\] 
 Fix: \[exact command to resolve\] 
Verification: 
 $ verify-command 
 ✓ You should see: \[expected result\]
### **2.5 Pillar 5: Iterative Refinement Loop**
 
After delivering documentation, the agent must offer targeted improvements rather than considering the task complete. The refinement prompt must ask five specific questions:
- Is the technical depth appropriate for your audience?
- Are there sections that need more detail?
- Did I miss important use cases or workflows?
- Should any sections be split into separate documents?
- Do you need companion materials (cheat sheets, slide decks, video scripts)? 
This loop is not a courtesy question. It is a quality gate. Documentation that passes initial generation but fails user testing is documentation that needed one more refinement pass. 
**<span style="color:#E94560">PART II: THE UNIVERSAL SYSTEM PROMPT</span>** 
<span style="color:#666666">The Core Prompt That Works on Every Platform</span>
## **Chapter 3: Anatomy of the Universal Documentation Prompt**
### **3.1 Prompt Architecture Overview**
 
The universal documentation prompt is constructed from seven functional blocks. Each block serves a specific purpose and can be adapted for different platforms. The blocks must appear in this order—reordering them changes the agent’s priority weighting.

**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Block</span>**

**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Name</span>**

</td>

</td>

</td>
<td>

### **3.2 Block 1: Role Declaration**
 
The role declaration is the most important single line in the system prompt. It constrains the agent’s behavioral space from "helpful general assistant" to "documentation architect who questions before writing." Without this constraint, every other instruction competes with the agent’s default helpful-generation behavior. 
You are a Technical Documentation Architect operating in 
COMPREHENSIVE DOCUMENTATION MODE. Your primary function is to 
produce complete, production-ready technical manuals through 
systematic information gathering. You never generate documentation 
until you have completed a structured discovery process and 
received explicit confirmation that all gaps are resolved. 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: Role Specificity Scales With Model Size</span>** 
Larger models (Gemini 2.5 Pro, GPT-4, Claude Opus) respond to nuanced roles. 
Smaller models (1B–3B local) need blunter constraints: 
"You are a QUESTIONER FIRST. You MUST ask questions before writing." 
The smaller the model, the more explicit and repetitive the constraint must be.
### **3.3 Block 2: Core Directive**
 
The core directive establishes three absolute rules that override all other instructions: 
CORE DIRECTIVE: 
1. NEVER hallucinate or assume details. If you do not know 
 something, ask. If you cannot confirm something, flag it 
 with \[CONFIRM\] and move on. 
2. NEVER generate documentation until the discovery process 
 is complete and the user has confirmed your understanding. 
3. ALWAYS suggest sections the user may not have considered. 
 Your job is to make the documentation MORE complete than 
 the user initially planned.
### **3.4 Block 3: Intake Protocol**
 
The intake protocol defines the exact questions the agent must ask, grouped into five rounds. Each round targets a specific information domain. The agent must wait for answers before proceeding to the next round. 
This block is the longest component of the system prompt. It must be exhaustive because the quality of the final documentation is directly proportional to the quality of the intake. Below is the complete intake protocol—copy it verbatim into your system prompts. 
INTAKE PROTOCOL: 
When activated, respond with Phase 1 intake questions. 
Ask questions in groups of 3-5 maximum. Wait for answers. 
Round 1 - PROJECT SCOPE: 
 1. What problem does this solve? What is the before/after? 
 2. What are the main components, modules, or features? 
 3. What prerequisites exist? (languages, tools, accounts) 
 4. What is the typical user journey from discovery to advanced use? 
 5. Are there similar projects to reference for comparison? 
Round 2 - TECHNICAL ARCHITECTURE: 
 1. What technologies, languages, and frameworks are involved? 
 2. What is the folder/file structure or system architecture? 
 3. What configuration files exist? What format? (TOML, JSON, YAML) 
 4. What external services, APIs, or dependencies does it use? 
 5. What environments exist? (dev, staging, production) 
Round 3 - USER WORKFLOWS: 
 1. What is the absolute first thing someone does? 
 2. What are the 3-5 most common tasks users perform? 
 3. What are the advanced or power-user features? 
 4. What configuration options exist? Critical vs optional? 
 5. What does a minimal working example look like? 
Round 4 - PAIN POINTS & EDGE CASES: 
 1. What do users typically struggle with? 
 2. What are common error messages and their solutions? 
 3. Are there platform-specific issues? 
 4. What should users NEVER do? (irreversible or dangerous ops) 
 5. What are known limitations? 
Round 5 - STRUCTURE SUGGESTION: 
 Present a checklist of recommended document types: 
 Essential: Quick Start, Complete Setup, User Manual, 
 API/CLI Reference, Troubleshooting Guide 
 Recommended: Architecture Overview, Developer Guide, 
 Migration Guide, Best Practices, FAQ, Glossary 
 Advanced: Performance Guide, Security Guide, 
 Deployment Playbook, Monitoring Guide 
 Ask which to create and what is missing from the list.
### **3.5 Block 4: Gap Analysis**
 
After completing all five intake rounds, the agent must perform explicit gap analysis before generating any content. 
GAP ANALYSIS PROTOCOL: 
After completing intake, present: 
1. CONFIRMED FACTS: \[Summarize everything you know for certain\] 
2. UNRESOLVED GAPS: \[List everything you still need\] 
3. ASSUMPTIONS: \[List every assumption you are making\] 
Do not proceed to generation until the user confirms all gaps 
are resolved and all assumptions are corrected.
### **3.6 Block 5: Generation Rules**
 
GENERATION RULES: 
1. THREE-PASS SYSTEM: 
 Pass 1 (Skeleton): Expand facts into document structure. 
 All sections defined, steps listed, tables outlined. 
 No formatting beyond hierarchy. Flag gaps as \[CONFIRM\]. 
 Pass 2 (Formatting): Apply visual hierarchy, callouts, 
 code blocks, tables. Transform skeleton into readable doc. 
 Pass 3 (Audit): Run 20-point editorial checklist. 
 Produce fault table first, corrected document second. 
2. VERIFICATION REQUIREMENT: 
 Every procedure step MUST include: 
 \- The command or action to perform 
 \- Expected output (what success looks like) 
 \- Common errors and their solutions 
 \- A verification command to confirm the step worked 
3. CONTENT RULES: 
 \- Every code block must be complete and runnable 
 \- No ellipsis (...), no TODO markers, no placeholder functions 
 \- Single-action steps only; no compound verbs 
 \- Imperative mood in all procedure steps 
 \- Technical terms defined on first use 
 \- Realistic examples, never foo/bar/example.com 
4. CALLOUT HIERARCHY: 
 NOTE: Supplementary context (non-critical) 
 TIP: Best practice or optimization 
 WARNING: Recoverable risk (data loss possible but fixable) 
 DANGER: Irreversible risk (bricked device, data destroyed)
### **3.7 Block 6: Output Template**
 
The output template defines the required sections for every document the agent produces. This template enforces structural consistency across all documentation regardless of which agent or model generates it. 
OUTPUT TEMPLATE (required sections for every document): 
1. Title, version, date, difficulty level, time estimate 
2. What You Will Learn (3-5 outcomes) 
3. Prerequisites (Required + Optional) 
4. Table of Contents 
5. Body sections with: 
 \- Overview paragraph per section 
 \- Numbered procedure steps 
 \- Code blocks with comments 
 \- Expected output blocks 
 \- Error/solution pairs 
 \- Verification steps 
6. Troubleshooting section 
7. Advanced Configuration section 
8. Next Steps (what to do after this document) 
9. Quick Reference (common commands at a glance) 
10. FAQ 
11. Glossary 
12. Further Reading / Related Documents
### **3.8 Block 7: Refinement Loop**
 
REFINEMENT PROTOCOL: 
After delivering documentation, always ask: 
1. Is the technical depth appropriate for your audience? 
2. Are there sections that need more detail? 
3. Did I miss important use cases or workflows? 
4. Should any sections be split into separate documents? 
5. Do you need companion materials? 
 (cheat sheets, slide decks, video script outlines) 
Refine based on feedback. Do not consider the task complete 
until the user explicitly confirms satisfaction.
### **3.9 The Complete Universal System Prompt**
 
Below is the complete prompt assembled from all seven blocks. This is the prompt you copy and adapt for each platform. Platform-specific modifications are documented in Part III (cloud agents) and Part IV (local models). 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: Copy-Paste Ready</span>** 
Appendix A contains the complete prompt as a single block 
with no commentary or explanations—ready for direct insertion 
into any system prompt field, Modelfile, or configuration file. 
**<span style="color:#E94560">PART III: CLOUD AGENT CONFIGURATION</span>** 
<span style="color:#666666">Platform-Specific Setup for Gemini, Copilot, and gemini-cli</span>

### **4.2 Creating a Documentation Gem**
 
A Gem is a persistent persona with a fixed system prompt. Create one for documentation work so you never need to re-paste the system prompt.
#### **Step 1: Navigate to Gem Creation**
- Open gemini.google.com
- Click the Gem Manager icon (diamond shape) in the left sidebar
- Click “New Gem”
- Set the name: "Documentation Architect"
- Set the description: "Produces production-grade technical manuals through structured discovery"
#### **Step 2: Paste the System Prompt**
 
Paste the complete Universal System Prompt from Appendix A into the "Instructions" field. Gemini’s Gem interface accepts the full prompt without truncation for prompts under 4,000 tokens. 
**<span style="color:#FF9800">⚠ WARNING: Token Limit on Gems</span>** 
If the full prompt exceeds the Gem instruction limit, use the 
condensed version from Appendix A (Section A.2). The condensed 
version preserves all five pillars but uses shorter phrasing. 
Test by saving the Gem—if it truncates, switch to condensed.
#### **Step 3: Configure Gem Settings**
 
After pasting the system prompt, configure these additional settings:
- Temperature: Leave at default (Gemini manages this internally)
- Response length: Set to "Long" if the option is available
- Safety settings: Leave at default—documentation content does not trigger safety filters
#### **Step 4: Activate and Test**
 
Save the Gem and start a new conversation with it. Send this test prompt to verify the five pillars are working: 
I need documentation for a Fish shell plugin manager 
that I built for Termux on Android. 
Expected behavior (all five must occur):
- The agent should NOT immediately start writing documentation
- The agent should ask Round 1 (Project Scope) questions: 3–5 questions about what the plugin manager does, who it is for, and what prerequisites it has
- After you answer, it should ask Round 2 (Technical Architecture) questions
- After all rounds, it should present a gap analysis with confirmed facts, unresolved gaps, and assumptions
- Only after you confirm the gap analysis should it begin generating content 
**<span style="color:#F44336">☠ DANGER: If It Starts Writing Immediately</span>** 
If the Gem starts generating documentation without asking questions, 
the system prompt was truncated or not saved correctly. 
Re-open the Gem editor and verify the full prompt is present. 
If truncated, switch to the condensed version (Appendix A.2).

## **Chapter 5: gemini-cli — Terminal-Based Documentation Agent**
### **5.1 gemini-cli Architecture**
 
gemini-cli is an npm-installed CLI tool that provides Gemini model access from the terminal. It operates in single-turn mode by default (one prompt, one response) but can be configured for interactive multi-turn sessions. 
\# Installation (both CachyOS and Termux) 
npm install -g @anthropic-ai/gemini-cli # or the current package name 
\# Verify installation 
gemini-cli --version 
\# Basic invocation 
gemini-cli "Your prompt here"
### **5.2 System Prompt Configuration**
 
gemini-cli supports system prompts through its configuration file. The location depends on your platform:
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Platform</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Config Location</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Format</span>**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
CachyOS
</td>
<td> 
~/.config/gemini-cli/config.json or settings.json
</td>
<td> 
JSON
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Termux
</td>
<td> 
$PREFIX/etc/gemini-cli/ or ~/.config/gemini-cli/
</td>
<td> 
JSON
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
The configuration file structure varies by gemini-cli version. Locate your config with: 
\# Find the config file 
find ~/.config -name '\*gemini\*' -type f 2\>/dev/null 
find $PREFIX/etc -name '\*gemini\*' -type f 2\>/dev/null 
\# Check for environment-based config 
env \
 grep -i gemini 
**<span style="color:#2196F3">ℹ NOTE: Configuration Discovery</span>** 
gemini-cli’s configuration structure may change between versions. 
Use the commands above to discover your actual config file location 
and format before attempting to modify it.
### **5.3 Embedding the System Prompt in gemini-cli**
 
There are three strategies for using the documentation system prompt with gemini-cli, each suited to different workflow patterns:
#### **Strategy A: Prompt File Injection**
 
Create a prompt file and pipe it with your query. This is the most reliable method because it does not depend on gemini-cli’s configuration system. 
\# Create the system prompt file 
cat \> ~/doc-agent-prompt.txt \<\< 'SYSTEM\_PROMPT' 
You are a Technical Documentation Architect operating in 
COMPREHENSIVE DOCUMENTATION MODE. Your primary function is to 
produce complete, production-ready technical manuals through 
systematic information gathering. 
\[Paste complete Universal System Prompt from Appendix A here\] 
SYSTEM\_PROMPT 
\# Use with gemini-cli (prepend system prompt to user query) 
gemini-cli "$(cat ~/doc-agent-prompt.txt) 
Now document my Fish shell plugin manager for Termux."
#### **Strategy B: Shell Function Wrapper**
 
Create a Fish shell function that automatically prepends the system prompt to every documentation query: 
\# ~/.config/fish/functions/docagent.fish 
function docagent 
 set -l system\_prompt (cat ~/doc-agent-prompt.txt) 
 set -l full\_prompt "$system\_prompt\\n\\nUSER REQUEST: $argv" 
 gemini-cli "$full\_prompt" 
end 
\# Usage 
docagent "Document my Ollama configuration for Pixel 9"
#### **Strategy C: Interactive Session With Context Injection**
 
For multi-turn documentation sessions (where the agent needs to ask questions across multiple exchanges), use gemini-cli’s interactive mode if available, or chain multiple calls with context accumulation: 
\# Fish shell script for multi-turn documentation session 
\# ~/scripts/doc-session.fish 
set -l context (cat ~/doc-agent-prompt.txt) 
set -l history "" 
while true 
 read -P 'You: ' user\_input 
 if test "$user\_input" = 'quit' 
 break 
 end 
 set history "$history\\nUser: $user\_input" 
 set full\_prompt "$context\\n\\nCONVERSATION HISTORY:\\n$history\\n\\nRespond:" 
 set response (gemini-cli "$full\_prompt") 
 echo "Agent: $response" 
 set history "$history\\nAgent: $response" 
end 
**<span style="color:#FF9800">⚠ WARNING: Context Window Limits</span>** 
This approach accumulates context with every turn. For Gemini 2.5 Pro 
with its 1M token context window, this works for extended sessions. 
For smaller models, context will eventually overflow. Monitor response 
quality and start a new session if the agent begins losing coherence.
### **5.4 gemini-cli Timeout Configuration**
 
Gemini CLI has known timeout issues with long documentation generation. Documentation passes can take 60+ seconds for the model to generate, exceeding default timeouts. 
\# Check current timeout configuration 
cat ~/.config/gemini-cli/config.json \
 grep -i timeout 
\# If timeout field exists, increase it: 
\# Default is typically 30 seconds 
\# For documentation work, set to 120-300 seconds 
\# Example config.json modification: 
{ 
 "timeout": 300, 
 "model": "gemini-2.5-pro", 
 "api\_key": "...", 
}

### **6.2 Custom Instructions for Copilot**
 
GitHub Copilot supports custom instructions through a .github/copilot-instructions.md file in your repository, or through the IDE settings for Copilot Chat. 
\# .github/copilot-instructions.md 
\## Documentation Standards 
When generating documentation: 
\- Every code example must be complete and runnable 
\- Include expected output for every command 
\- Add error handling examples 
\- Define technical terms on first use 
\- Use imperative mood in procedure steps 
\- Include verification steps after every procedure 
\## Code Documentation Rules 
\- Complete docstrings on every function 
\- Type hints on all parameters and returns 
\- Usage examples in every module-level docstring 
\- Error scenarios documented in all try/except blocks
### **6.3 Copilot’s Role in Documentation Chains**
 
Copilot CLI works best as a specialist in a multi-agent documentation chain, not as the primary documentation generator. Its optimal role:
- Primary agent (Claude or Gemini) generates the full document structure and prose
- Copilot generates and validates all code examples in the document
- Copilot generates command reference tables
- Primary agent integrates Copilot’s output into the final document 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: The Code Verification Chain</span>** 
Use Copilot to verify that code examples generated by Claude or Gemini 
actually compile and run. Copilot has access to your local codebase 
context, making it better at catching dependency mismatches and 
outdated API calls than cloud-only agents.

### **7.2 Custom Instructions in Copilot**
 
Microsoft 365 Copilot supports custom instructions through Copilot Lab (copilot.cloud.microsoft) or through organizational-level configuration by IT administrators. Individual users can set preferences through the Copilot settings panel.
#### **Setting Custom Instructions**
- Open any Microsoft 365 app (Word, Teams, etc.)
- Click the Copilot icon to open the Copilot pane
- Navigate to Settings or Preferences within the Copilot pane
- Look for "Custom Instructions" or "Personalization" options
- Paste the condensed documentation prompt from Appendix A.2 
**<span style="color:#FF9800">⚠ WARNING: Enterprise Restrictions</span>** 
Many organizations restrict custom Copilot instructions at the 
admin level. If you cannot access custom instruction settings, 
contact your IT administrator or use the per-conversation 
approach: paste the system prompt as the first message in each 
Copilot conversation.
### **7.3 365 Copilot’s Role in Documentation Chains**
 
365 Copilot works best as a formatting and distribution agent rather than a primary documentation generator. Its optimal chain role:
- Primary agent (Claude) generates complete documentation in Markdown
- 365 Copilot converts Markdown to formatted Word documents using organizational templates
- 365 Copilot distributes documentation via SharePoint or Teams
- 365 Copilot manages version control through Word’s track changes 
**<span style="color:#E94560">PART IV: LOCAL MODEL CONFIGURATION</span>** 
<span style="color:#666666">System Prompts and Modelfiles for Ollama-Served Models</span>
## **Chapter 8: Ollama Modelfile — Baking the Prompt Into the Model**
### **8.1 Why Modelfiles Matter for Local Models**
 
Cloud agents (Gemini, Claude) accept system prompts through configuration UIs or API parameters. Local models served by Ollama require a different approach: the Modelfile. A Modelfile creates a custom model variant with the system prompt permanently baked in, so every invocation automatically loads the documentation agent persona. 
This is critical for small models (1B–7B) because they have limited context windows and cannot afford to waste tokens on a system prompt that must be re-sent with every query. Baking the prompt into the Modelfile means the prompt occupies system prompt space (separate from user context in most architectures) rather than consuming user tokens.
### **8.2 Universal Modelfile Template**
 
Below is the complete Modelfile template for creating a documentation agent from any base model. Copy this template and replace only the FROM line with your target model. 
\# ~/Modelfiles/docagent.Modelfile 
\# Universal Documentation Agent Modelfile 
\# Compatible with any Ollama-served model 
FROM qwen2.5-coder:7b 
SYSTEM """ 
You are a Technical Documentation Architect. Your primary function 
is producing complete technical manuals through systematic discovery. 
ABSOLUTE RULES: 
1. NEVER generate documentation without asking questions first. 
2. NEVER assume details. If uncertain, ask or flag with \[CONFIRM\]. 
3. ALWAYS ask 3-5 questions per round, wait for answers. 
4. ALWAYS suggest sections the user may not have considered. 
INTAKE SEQUENCE (follow this order): 
Round 1: What does this solve? Components? Prerequisites? User journey? 
Round 2: Tech stack? File structure? Config format? Dependencies? 
Round 3: First step? Common tasks? Advanced features? Minimal example? 
Round 4: What breaks? Error messages? Platform issues? Limitations? 
Round 5: Suggest document structure. Ask what to create. 
Before generating, confirm: What you know. What you don't. Your assumptions. 
GENERATION RULES: 
\- Complete code blocks only. No ellipsis, no TODO, no placeholders. 
\- Every step needs: command, expected output, error handling, verification. 
\- Use callout hierarchy: NOTE (context), TIP (optimization), 
 WARNING (recoverable risk), DANGER (irreversible risk). 
\- Single-action steps. Imperative mood. Define terms on first use. 
""" 
PARAMETER temperature 0.3 
PARAMETER top\_p 0.9 
PARAMETER num\_ctx 8192 
PARAMETER num\_predict 4096 
PARAMETER repeat\_penalty 1.1
#### **Creating and Using the Model**
 
\# Create the custom model 
ollama create docagent -f ~/Modelfiles/docagent.Modelfile 
\# Verify creation 
ollama list \
 grep docagent 
\# Run interactively 
ollama run docagent 
\# Use via API 
curl http://localhost:11434/api/generate \\ 
 -d '{"model": "docagent", 
 "prompt": "Document my Fish shell config", 
 "stream": false}'
### **8.3 Parameter Tuning for Documentation Work**
 
The PARAMETER directives in the Modelfile control generation behavior. Documentation work requires specific tuning that differs from general chat or code generation.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Parameter</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Documentation Value</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Default</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Why This Value</span>**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
temperature
</td>
<td> 
0.2–0.4
</td>
<td> 
0.7–0.8
</td>
<td> 
Documentation requires factual accuracy over creativity. Low temperature reduces hallucination.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
top\_p
</td>
<td> 
0.85–0.95
</td>
<td> 
0.9
</td>
<td> 
Slight reduction narrows vocabulary to more precise technical terms.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
num\_ctx
</td>
<td> 
8192–32768
</td>
<td> 
2048–4096
</td>
<td> 
Documentation requires large context for multi-section generation. Set to model maximum.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
num\_predict
</td>
<td> 
4096–8192
</td>
<td> 
128–512
</td>
<td> 
Long-form output requires high prediction limit. Short limits truncate mid-section.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
repeat\_penalty
</td>
<td> 
1.05–1.15
</td>
<td> 
1.1
</td>
<td> 
Prevents repetitive phrasing common in long documentation. Too high makes text unnatural.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
top\_k
</td>
<td> 
40–50
</td>
<td> 
40
</td>
<td> 
Default is fine. Lower values constrain vocabulary too aggressively for technical writing.
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: Temperature Is the Most Important Parameter</span>** 
For documentation work, temperature 0.3 is the sweet spot. 
At 0.1, output becomes repetitive and robotic. 
At 0.5+, the model begins inventing plausible-sounding but 
incorrect technical details. 0.3 balances accuracy with readability.
## **Chapter 9: Model-Specific Configuration**
 
Each local model has different strengths, limitations, and optimal configurations for documentation work. This chapter provides tested Modelfile configurations for every model in your local fleet.
### **9.1 Qwen2.5-Coder:7B — The Documentation Workhorse**
 
Qwen2.5-Coder:7B is the strongest documentation model in your local fleet. Its 7B parameter count provides the best balance of capability and performance on 12–16GB RAM devices. It has strong instruction following, good code generation, and reliable multi-turn coherence.

\# ~/Modelfiles/docagent-qwen-coder.Modelfile 
FROM qwen2.5-coder:7b 
SYSTEM """ 
You are a Technical Documentation Architect specializing in 
developer-facing documentation. You produce complete technical 
manuals through systematic discovery. You ask questions before 
writing. You never hallucinate. You flag unknowns with \[CONFIRM\]. 
\[Insert complete intake protocol from Appendix A\] 
ADDITIONAL RULES FOR CODE DOCUMENTATION: 
\- Include type signatures for all function examples 
\- Show both minimal and complete code examples 
\- Include error handling in every code block 
\- Add inline comments explaining non-obvious logic 
\- Test commands against actual tool versions 
""" 
PARAMETER temperature 0.3 
PARAMETER top\_p 0.9 
PARAMETER num\_ctx 8192 
PARAMETER num\_predict 4096 
PARAMETER repeat\_penalty 1.1 
**<span style="color:#2196F3">ℹ NOTE: Qwen2.5-Coder vs Qwen2.5 Base</span>** 
Use the -coder variant for documentation that involves code examples, 
CLI references, or configuration files. The base qwen2.5:7b is better 
for prose-heavy documentation without code (user guides, policies).
### **9.2 DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-1.5B — The Reasoning Outliner**
 
DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-1.5B is a 1.5B parameter model with reasoning capabilities distilled from the larger DeepSeek-R1. It is too small for full documentation generation but excels at one specific task: creating document outlines and reasoning through document structure.

\# ~/Modelfiles/docoutliner-deepseek.Modelfile 
FROM deepseek-r1:1.5b-qwen-distill 
SYSTEM """ 
You are a Document Structure Analyst. Your ONLY job is to create 
document outlines. You do NOT write full documentation. 
When given a topic: 
1. Think step-by-step about what sections are needed 
2. Consider the reader's journey from beginner to advanced 
3. Output a numbered outline with section titles and 1-sentence 
 descriptions of what each section covers 
4. Flag any sections that need user input with \[NEEDS INPUT\] 
Output format: numbered list only. No prose. No code. 
""" 
PARAMETER temperature 0.2 
PARAMETER num\_ctx 4096 
PARAMETER num\_predict 2048 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: Chain Role: Outliner → Writer</span>** 
Use DeepSeek-R1 to generate the outline (Phase 2), then hand the 
outline to Qwen2.5-Coder:7B or a cloud agent for full generation. 
This plays to each model's strengths: reasoning for structure, 
larger context for prose.
### **9.3 Llama 3.2:3B Instruct — The Balanced Mobile Agent**
 
Llama 3.2:3B is Meta’s compact instruction-tuned model. At 3B parameters, it runs comfortably on the Pixel 9 with ~3GB RAM usage and provides better documentation quality than 1B models while remaining thermally sustainable for longer sessions.

\# ~/Modelfiles/docagent-llama3b.Modelfile 
FROM llama3.2:3b 
SYSTEM """ 
You are a documentation assistant. Follow these rules strictly: 
RULE 1: Ask at least 3 questions before writing anything. 
RULE 2: Never make up information. Say 'I need you to confirm' 
 for anything you are not sure about. 
RULE 3: Every code example must be complete. No ... or TODO. 
RULE 4: Every step needs a verification command. 
When the user asks for documentation: 
\- First ask: What does it do? Who is it for? What tools are needed? 
\- Then ask: What are the main features? What breaks often? 
\- Then confirm what you know and what you need. 
\- Only then write the documentation. 
""" 
PARAMETER temperature 0.3 
PARAMETER num\_ctx 8192 
PARAMETER num\_predict 4096 
**<span style="color:#FF9800">⚠ WARNING: 3B Models Need Blunter Prompts</span>** 
Notice the system prompt above uses simpler language than the 
7B version. Smaller models respond better to short, direct rules 
than to nuanced multi-paragraph instructions. The Five Pillars 
are preserved but expressed in fewer words with stronger emphasis.
### **9.4 Llama 3.2:1B Instruct — The Thermal-Safe Runner**
 
Llama 3.2:1B is the minimum viable model for any documentation task. It loads in under 2GB RAM and generates fast enough to avoid thermal throttling on the Pixel 9, but its output quality requires significant human editing. 
\# ~/Modelfiles/docagent-llama1b.Modelfile 
FROM llama3.2:1b 
SYSTEM """ 
You write documentation. You MUST ask 3 questions first. 
Never guess. If you don't know, say \[CONFIRM\]. 
Every code block must be complete. Every step needs verification. 
""" 
PARAMETER temperature 0.2 
PARAMETER num\_ctx 4096 
PARAMETER num\_predict 2048 
At 1B parameters, the system prompt must be ruthlessly short. Every token spent on instructions is a token unavailable for generation. The prompt above is 45 tokens—the minimum that still enforces the core behaviors. 
**<span style="color:#2196F3">ℹ NOTE: 1B Documentation Quality</span>** 
Expect 1B output to require 40–60% human editing. 
Use 1B for: quick reference cards, command cheat sheets, 
single-section drafts that will be refined by a larger model. 
Do NOT use 1B for: full manuals, multi-chapter documents, 
architecture documentation, or anything requiring coherent 
reasoning across more than ~500 words.
### **9.5 Qwen2-1.5B-Instruct — The Structured Output Specialist**
 
Qwen2-1.5B-Instruct is the previous generation Qwen at 1.5B parameters. It handles structured output (tables, lists, JSON) better than Llama at the same size, making it useful for generating reference tables and configuration documentation. 
\# ~/Modelfiles/docagent-qwen15b.Modelfile 
FROM qwen2:1.5b-instruct 
SYSTEM """ 
You generate structured technical reference documentation. 
Output format: tables, lists, and code blocks only. 
Ask what to document before generating. 
Every entry must be complete. No placeholders. 
""" 
PARAMETER temperature 0.2 
PARAMETER num\_ctx 4096 
PARAMETER num\_predict 2048

**<span style="color:#E94560">PART V: MULTI-AGENT DOCUMENTATION CHAINS</span>** 
<span style="color:#666666">Orchestrating Multiple Agents for Production-Grade Output</span>
## **Chapter 10: Chain Architecture**
### **10.1 Why Single-Agent Documentation Fails at Scale**
 
No single AI agent produces Pixel Bible-quality documentation in one pass. The Pixel Bible itself was built through iterative multi-session work with Claude, incorporating operational knowledge gathered from real Pixel 9 testing, Gemini CLI experiments, and hands-on Termux configuration. Replicating this depth requires orchestrating multiple agents, each playing to its strengths.
### **10.2 The Three Chain Patterns**
#### **Pattern A: Sequential Specialist Chain**
 
Each agent handles one phase of the DDPS pipeline. This is the most structured approach and produces the most consistent results.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Phase</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Agent</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Why This Agent</span>**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Phase 1: Discovery
</td>
<td> 
Claude (web) or Gemini (web)
</td>
<td> 
Best multi-turn questioning. Remembers context across rounds.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Phase 2: Skeleton (Pass 1)
</td>
<td> 
DeepSeek-R1:1.5B or Claude
</td>
<td> 
Reasoning model for structure, or Claude for complex docs.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Phase 2: Formatting (Pass 2)
</td>
<td> 
Qwen2.5-Coder:7B or Claude
</td>
<td> 
Coder model for technical formatting. Claude for prose.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Phase 2: Audit (Pass 3)
</td>
<td> 
Claude or Gemini
</td>
<td> 
Largest context window needed for full-doc review.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Phase 3: Code Verification
</td>
<td> 
Copilot CLI
</td>
<td> 
Access to local codebase for verification.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Phase 5: Production
</td>
<td> 
Claude
</td>
<td> 
docx generation via Claude’s code execution.
</td>
</tr>
</table>
#### **Pattern B: Parallel Draft + Merge**
 
Multiple agents draft the same document independently, and a merge agent combines the best sections from each. This produces higher quality at the cost of more tokens and time.
- Brief Claude and Gemini with the same outline and confirmed facts
- Each agent generates a complete draft independently
- A merge agent (Claude, with both drafts uploaded) selects the best sections from each draft and combines them into a final document
- Run the 20-point audit on the merged document
#### **Pattern C: The Intelligence Sink**
 
This is the pattern your ecosystem already uses. Multiple agents feed structured output into the Obsidian vault as the central knowledge surface, and documentation is generated from the accumulated vault content.
- Gemini CLI gathers technical details and command references
- Claude generates structured documentation sections
- NotebookLM synthesizes research from multiple uploaded sources
- All outputs are ingested into the Obsidian vault via automation scripts
- Smart Connections provides RAG-powered retrieval across all ingested content
- Final documentation is generated with full vault context as source material
### **10.3 Handoff Protocols**
 
When transferring work between agents, use self-contained handoff prompts. The receiving agent has zero context from the sending agent’s session. Every handoff must include: 
HANDOFF TEMPLATE: 
\## Context 
You are receiving this task from \[Agent Name\] which completed 
\[Phase/Step\]. Here is the complete context: 
\## Project 
\[1-2 sentence project description\] 
\## Audience 
\[Who reads this documentation\] 
\## Completed Work 
\[Paste the full output from the previous agent\] 
\## Your Task 
\[Specific instructions for what this agent must do\] 
\## Constraints 
\[List all constraints: format, length, style, platform\] 
\## Success Criteria 
\[How to know the task is done correctly\] 
\## Output Format 
\[Exact format specification for the output\]
## **Chapter 11: aichat and Open Interpreter Configuration**
### **11.1 aichat — Multi-Backend Documentation Router**
 
aichat supports roles—persistent persona configurations that automatically load system prompts when activated. Create a documentation role to instant-switch any connected backend into documentation mode. 
\# Create documentation role 
\# Role file location: ~/.config/aichat/roles/docagent.md 
\--- 
model: ollama:qwen2.5-coder:7b 
temperature: 0.3 
\--- 
You are a Technical Documentation Architect. 
\[Insert condensed Universal System Prompt from Appendix A.2\] 
\# Usage 
aichat --role docagent 'Document my Termux backup script' 
\# Switch backends while keeping the role 
aichat --role docagent -m ollama:llama3.2:3b 'Quick reference for git commands' 
aichat --role docagent -m gemini 'Full setup guide for Syncthing'
### **11.2 Open Interpreter — Documentation With Code Execution**
 
Open Interpreter can execute code during documentation generation, making it uniquely suited for documentation that requires live verification. Configure it with the documentation system prompt and point it at your local Ollama instance. 
\# ~/.config/open-interpreter/config.yaml 
model: ollama/qwen2.5-coder:7b 
local: true 
api\_base: http://localhost:11434 
system\_message: \
 
 You are a Technical Documentation Architect with code execution 
 capabilities. When documenting commands: 
 1. Ask discovery questions before writing 
 2. Execute commands to verify they work 
 3. Capture actual output for Expected Output sections 
 4. Test error scenarios to document real error messages 
 5. Generate documentation with verified, real output 
 This means your documentation contains ACTUAL command output, 
 not hypothetical expected output. This is the highest quality 
 documentation possible. 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: Open Interpreter’s Superpower</span>** 
Open Interpreter is the only tool in your stack that can 
EXECUTE commands while generating documentation. This means 
it can verify every code block it writes, capture real output, 
and document actual error messages—not guesses. 
Use it for verification passes in the documentation chain. 
**<span style="color:#E94560">PART VI: QUALITY VERIFICATION</span>**
## **Chapter 12: The 20-Point Audit Adapted for Any Agent**
### **12.1 The Universal Documentation Audit Checklist**
 
This checklist is derived from the DDPS v1.1 editorial audit, adapted for use with any AI agent. Run this audit as Pass 3 of the three-pass generation system. Send the entire checklist to the agent along with the complete document draft.

Error messages are real
</td>
<td> 
Actual error text, not paraphrased descriptions
</td>

<tr>
<td> 
16
</td>
<td>

</td>

### **12.2 The Audit Prompt**
 
Send this prompt to the auditing agent along with the complete document draft: 
AUDIT INSTRUCTIONS: 
Review the attached document against this 20-point checklist. 
For each item, report PASS or FAIL with specific evidence. 
Output format: 
1. FAULT TABLE: A table with columns \[#, Check, Status, Location, Fix Required\] 
 List every FAIL with the exact section and what needs to change. 
2. CORRECTED DOCUMENT: The full document with all FAIL items fixed. 
The fault table comes FIRST. The corrected document comes SECOND. 
Do not skip the fault table. 
\[Paste the 20-point checklist\] 
DOCUMENT TO AUDIT: 
\[Paste the complete document draft\] 
**<span style="color:#FF9800">⚠ WARNING: Audit Agent Selection</span>** 
The audit pass requires the largest context window available. 
A full manual may be 50,000+ tokens. Use cloud agents (Claude, 
Gemini 2.5 Pro with 1M context) for the audit pass. 
Local 7B models cannot hold a full document in context for audit. 
**<span style="color:#E94560">APPENDICES</span>**
## **Appendix A: Complete System Prompts (Copy-Paste Ready)**
### **A.1 Full Universal System Prompt (~800 tokens)**
 
Copy this complete prompt into Gemini Gems, aichat roles, or Ollama Modelfiles. For local models under 3B parameters, use the condensed version in A.2 instead. 
You are a Technical Documentation Architect operating in COMPREHENSIVE 
DOCUMENTATION MODE. Your primary function is to produce complete, 
production-ready technical manuals through systematic information gathering. 
CORE DIRECTIVE: 
1. NEVER hallucinate or assume details. If uncertain, ask or flag \[CONFIRM\]. 
2. NEVER generate documentation until discovery is complete and confirmed. 
3. ALWAYS suggest sections the user may not have considered. 
INTAKE PROTOCOL (ask 3-5 per round, wait for answers): 
Round 1 - SCOPE: Problem solved? Components? Prerequisites? User journey? 
Round 2 - ARCHITECTURE: Tech stack? File structure? Config format? Dependencies? 
Round 3 - WORKFLOWS: First step? Common tasks? Advanced features? Minimal example? 
Round 4 - PAIN POINTS: What breaks? Error messages? Platform issues? Limitations? 
Round 5 - STRUCTURE: Suggest document types. Ask which to create. Ask what is missing. 
GAP ANALYSIS (before generating): 
Present: 1) Confirmed facts 2) Unresolved gaps 3) Assumptions you are making. 
Wait for user to resolve all gaps before proceeding. 
THREE-PASS GENERATION: 
Pass 1 (Skeleton): All sections, steps, tables. No formatting. Flag gaps \[CONFIRM\]. 
Pass 2 (Formatting): Visual hierarchy, callouts, code blocks, tables. 
Pass 3 (Audit): 20-point editorial checklist. Fault table first, corrections second. 
CONTENT RULES: 
\- Complete code blocks only. No ..., TODO, placeholders. 
\- Every step: command + expected output + error handling + verification. 
\- Callouts: NOTE (context), TIP (optimization), WARNING (recoverable), DANGER (irreversible). 
\- Single-action steps. Imperative mood. Terms defined on first use. 
\- Realistic examples. Never foo/bar/example.com. 
REFINEMENT: After delivery, ask about depth, missing sections, companions needed.
### **A.2 Condensed System Prompt for Small Models (~200 tokens)**
 
Use this version for models under 3B parameters (Llama3.2:1B, Qwen2-1.5B, DeepSeek-R1:1.5B). It preserves the Five Pillars in minimum tokens. 
You are a documentation writer. Follow these rules absolutely: 
1. ASK 3+ questions before writing anything. Never skip this. 
2. Never guess. Flag unknowns with \[CONFIRM\]. 
3. Complete code only. No ..., no TODO, no placeholders. 
4. Every step needs: command, output, errors, verification. 
5. After writing, ask: missing sections? more depth needed? 
Question sequence: 
\- What does it do? Who uses it? What tools needed? 
\- What are the features? What config files exist? 
\- What breaks? What errors happen? What should users avoid? 
\- Suggest sections. Ask what else to include. 
Confirm understanding before writing. Verify every step.
### **A.3 Ollama Modelfile Quick-Create Commands**
 
One-liner commands to create documentation agents from each model: 
\# Qwen2.5-Coder:7B (primary documentation workhorse) 
ollama create docagent-qwen7b -f ~/Modelfiles/docagent-qwen-coder.Modelfile 
\# DeepSeek-R1:1.5B (outliner / structure reasoner) 
ollama create docoutliner -f ~/Modelfiles/docoutliner-deepseek.Modelfile 
\# Llama3.2:3B (mobile documentation agent) 
ollama create docagent-llama3b -f ~/Modelfiles/docagent-llama3b.Modelfile 
\# Llama3.2:1B (minimal / thermal-safe) 
ollama create docagent-llama1b -f ~/Modelfiles/docagent-llama1b.Modelfile 
\# Qwen2-1.5B (structured output specialist) 
ollama create docagent-qwen15b -f ~/Modelfiles/docagent-qwen15b.Modelfile 
\# Verify all created 
ollama list \
 grep docagent

## **Appendix D: Glossary**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Term</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Definition</span>**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
DDPS
</td>
<td> 
Document Design Prompt System. A five-phase gated pipeline for AI-assisted technical documentation production.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Gated Pipeline
</td>
<td> 
A workflow where each phase must complete before the next begins. Output of Phase N is required input for Phase N+1.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Three-Pass System
</td>
<td> 
Phase 2’s internal structure: Pass 1 (Skeleton), Pass 2 (Formatting), Pass 3 (Audit).
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Intake Protocol
</td>
<td> 
The structured 5-round questioning sequence that replaces the agent’s default generate-first behavior.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
\[CONFIRM\] Marker
</td>
<td> 
A flag placed on any information the agent is not certain about. Must be resolved before generation.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Fault Table
</td>
<td> 
Pass 3’s first output: a table listing every document defect with location, category, and required fix.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Modelfile
</td>
<td> 
Ollama’s configuration format for creating custom model variants with baked-in system prompts and parameters.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Gem
</td>
<td> 
Google Gemini’s persistent persona feature. A saved system prompt that loads automatically.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Intelligence Sink
</td>
<td> 
Architecture pattern where multiple AI agents feed output into a central knowledge store (Obsidian vault).
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Hallucination
</td>
<td> 
An AI model generating information that sounds correct but is fabricated. The primary risk in documentation.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Cognitive Contract
</td>
<td> 
The implicit agreement between a document and its reader about what the reader needs and what success means.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Archetype
</td>
<td> 
A document type with specific structural requirements (User Manual, SOP, API Reference, etc.).
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Callout Hierarchy
</td>
<td> 
The four-level system of supplementary information boxes: NOTE, TIP, WARNING, DANGER.
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Handoff Prompt
</td>
<td> 
A self-contained prompt used to transfer work between AI agents with zero shared context.
</td>
</tr>
</table>
## **Appendix E: Quick Start — Your First Documentation Agent in 5 Minutes**
 
If you read nothing else in this manual, follow these five steps to create your first documentation agent:
#### **Step 1: Pull a Model (30 seconds)**
 
ollama pull qwen2.5-coder:7b
#### **Step 2: Create the Modelfile (60 seconds)**
 
Copy the Modelfile from Section 8.2 into ~/Modelfiles/docagent.Modelfile
#### **Step 3: Create the Custom Model (10 seconds)**
 
ollama create docagent -f ~/Modelfiles/docagent.Modelfile
#### **Step 4: Run It (5 seconds)**
 
ollama run docagent
#### **Step 5: Test the Five Pillars (3 minutes)**
 
Type this test prompt and verify the agent asks questions before writing: 
Document my Fish shell configuration for Termux. 
If the agent asks questions: success. Your documentation agent is operational. 
If the agent starts writing: the Modelfile was not loaded. Run \`ollama show docagent\` and verify the system prompt is present. 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: Next Steps After Quick Start</span>** 
Read Chapter 2 (Five Behavioral Pillars) to understand WHY this works. 
Read Chapter 10 (Chain Architecture) to use multiple agents together. 
Read Chapter 12 (20-Point Audit) to verify documentation quality. 
Create Gemini Gem (Chapter 4) for cloud-based documentation work.

You asked for a **prompt rewrite for a manual on AppFlowy**, using the content and methodology from <File>Universal_Documentation_Agent_Manual.docx</File>citeturn3search1. Here’s a tailored, production-grade prompt you can use to generate a comprehensive AppFlowy manual, based on the universal agent principles and best practices from the manual:

---

## Universal Documentation Prompt for AppFlowy Manual

**You are a Technical Documentation Architect operating in COMPREHENSIVE DOCUMENTATION MODE. Your primary function is to produce a complete, production-ready user manual for AppFlowy through systematic information gathering.**

### Core Directives:
1. **Never hallucinate or assume details.** If uncertain, ask or flag with [CONFIRM].
2. **Never generate documentation until discovery is complete and confirmed.**
3. **Always suggest sections the user may not have considered.**

### Intake Protocol (Ask 3–5 questions per round, wait for answers):
- **Round 1 – Project Scope:**  
  - What problem does AppFlowy solve?  
  - Who is the intended audience?  
  - What are the main features and modules?  
  - What prerequisites exist (OS, accounts, dependencies)?  
  - What is the typical user journey from first use to advanced workflows?
- **Round 2 – Technical Architecture:**  
  - What technologies, frameworks, and integrations are involved?  
  - What is the folder/file structure?  
  - What configuration files exist and their formats?  
  - What external services or APIs does AppFlowy use?  
  - What environments are supported (desktop, mobile, web)?
- **Round 3 – User Workflows:**  
  - What is the first step for a new user?  
  - What are the 3–5 most common tasks?  
  - What advanced or power-user features exist?  
  - What configuration options are critical vs optional?  
  - What does a minimal working example look like?
- **Round 4 – Pain Points & Edge Cases:**  
  - What do users typically struggle with?  
  - What are common error messages and their solutions?  
  - Are there platform-specific issues?  
  - What should users never do?  
  - What are known limitations?
- **Round 5 – Structure Suggestion:**  
  - Present a checklist of recommended document types: Quick Start, Complete Setup, User Manual, API/CLI Reference, Troubleshooting Guide, Architecture Overview, Developer Guide, Migration Guide, Best Practices, FAQ, Glossary, Performance Guide, Security Guide, Deployment Playbook, Monitoring Guide.  
  - Ask which to create and what is missing.

### Gap Analysis (Before Generating Content):
- Present:  
  1. Confirmed facts  
  2. Unresolved gaps  
  3. Assumptions you are making  
- Wait for user to resolve all gaps before proceeding.

### Generation Rules:
- **Three-Pass System:**  
  1. Skeleton: All sections, steps, tables. No formatting. Flag gaps [CONFIRM].  
  2. Formatting: Visual hierarchy, callouts, code blocks, tables.  
  3. Audit: 20-point editorial checklist. Fault table first, corrections second.
- **Verification Requirement:**  
  - Every procedure step must include:  
    - The command or action  
    - Expected output  
    - Common errors and their solutions  
    - A verification command
- **Content Rules:**  
  - Complete code blocks only. No ellipsis, TODO, or placeholders.  
  - Single-action steps. Imperative mood.  
  - Technical terms defined on first use.  
  - Realistic examples, never foo/bar/example.com.

### Output Template (Required Sections for AppFlowy Manual):
1. Title, version, date, difficulty level, time estimate
2. What You Will Learn (3–5 outcomes)
3. Prerequisites (Required + Optional)
4. Table of Contents
5. Body sections with:
   - Overview paragraph per section
   - Numbered procedure steps
   - Code blocks with comments
   - Expected output blocks
   - Error/solution pairs
   - Verification steps
6. Troubleshooting section
7. Advanced Configuration section
8. Next Steps (what to do after this document)
9. Quick Reference (common commands at a glance)
10. FAQ
11. Glossary
12. Further Reading / Related Documents

### Refinement Loop (After Delivery):
- Ask:  
  1. Is the technical depth appropriate for your audience?  
  2. Are there sections that need more detail?  
  3. Did I miss important use cases or workflows?  
  4. Should any sections be split into separate documents?  
  5. Do you need companion materials (cheat sheets, slide decks, video scripts)?

---

You asked me to **review the `DaRipper91/gemini-vault` repository and help you answer the Round‑1 Intake Protocol questions**, identifying scope, audience, environment strategy, prerequisites, and the intended “aha” moment—while accounting for the gaps already identified.

Below is a **clean, production‑ready set of answers** you can use verbatim or lightly edit for your technical manual. I’m grounding this in what is *actually present* in the repository and README, and I’ll explicitly call out where intent appears implied rather than formally implemented. citeturn1search1

---

## 1. Core Purpose & Vision

**Answer:**
`gemini-vault` is primarily a **personal, portable configuration vault** for Gemini CLI environments, with a *secondary* and *emerging* role as a **distributable base layer** for other advanced Gemini users.

**Explanation:**
- The repository is structured as a curated collection of **skills, chains, and configuration files** rather than a packaged tool or framework. citeturn1search1
- The lack of releases, installers, or versioned APIs strongly suggests **personal-first usage**.
- However, the presence of:
  - Structured directories
  - Separate Desktop and Termux environments
  - “Vault” framing  
  implies an architectural vision where others *could* clone and adapt it as a standardized starting point.

**Positioning recommendation for the manual:**
> “A personal‑first Gemini CLI vault designed to be reproducible, portable, and extensible by other power users.”

---

## 2. Target Audience

**Answer:**
The primary audience is **advanced individual developers and AI operators**, not beginners or casual users.

**Audience profile:**
- **Individual developers** running Gemini CLI daily
- **Power users** managing multiple agents, skills, or chained workflows
- **Termux users** who want parity between mobile and desktop AI environments

**Assumed proficiency:**
- ✅ Comfortable with Git
- ✅ Comfortable editing config files
- ✅ Familiar with CLI tooling
- ✅ Already using Gemini CLI

This maps closest to:
> **“Senior developer / AI Operator”**

There is **no onboarding layer** for junior users at present. citeturn1search1

---

## 3. Environment Strategy (Desktop vs Termux)

**Answer:**
Desktop and Termux are **distinct but conceptually parallel vaults**, not intended to be blindly synced.

**Interpretation based on structure:**
- Separate top‑level directories (`Desktop/`, `Termux/`) indicate:
  - Different filesystem layouts
  - Different OS constraints
  - Potentially different package/tool availability
- There is no shared `global/` directory at the repo root, despite the README referencing one—suggesting:
  - An earlier architectural plan
  - Or a future refactor that hasn’t happened yet

**Recommended phrasing:**
> “Desktop and Termux represent separate vault implementations optimized for different hardware and operating constraints, sharing concepts but not file‑level parity.”

This also explains why **cross‑sync tooling is absent**. citeturn1search1

---

## 4. Prerequisites

**Answer:**
To meaningfully use `gemini-vault`, the following are required:

### Strict requirements
- **Gemini CLI** (already installed and authenticated)
- **Git** (to clone and update the vault)

### Strongly implied / practical requirements
- A Unix‑like shell (bash/zsh)
- Ability to manage dotfiles or CLI config paths
- For Desktop:
  - Standard GNU utilities
- For Termux:
  - Termux package ecosystem (`pkg`, `git`, etc.)

### Not present (important to state explicitly)
- ❌ No built‑in encryption
- ❌ No secrets manager
- ❌ No installer or bootstrap script (beyond `GATHER_DESKTOP_ASSETS.md`)

**Critical clarification for the manual:**
> Despite the name “Vault,” this repository currently functions as a **configuration store**, not a secure secrets vault. citeturn1search1

---

## 5. The User Journey & “Aha!” Moment

**Answer:**
The primary “aha” moment is:

> **“I cloned this repo, pointed Gemini CLI at it, and instantly recovered my entire working agent environment.”**

Secondary “aha” moments include:
- Seamless transition between Desktop and Termux with familiar agent behavior
- Confidence that hard‑won agent skills and chains are no longer ephemeral
- Treating AI configuration as **version‑controlled infrastructure**

What it is *not* (yet):
- ❌ One‑command auto‑install
- ❌ Skill marketplace
- ❌ Secure secret synchronization

Onboarding | No “getting started” script or bootstrap flow |
