Gemini-Vault Scope and Audience


============================================================
03/30/2026 12:56:20 +00:00
Great—Round 2 is exactly the right place to be explicit about **what exists today vs. what is implicit or aspirational**. Below are **clear, defensible answers** you can use in a production-grade manual. Where behavior is inferred rather than enforced by code, I label it plainly so the documentation stays honest.

---

# 🏗️ Round 2: Technical Architecture

## 1. Integration Mechanism (How Gemini CLI consumes the vault)

**Answer (current-state accurate):**  
`gemini-vault` is consumed via **manual filesystem integration**, not via a formal Gemini CLI plugin or environment-variable–driven loader.

### Practical integration model
- The user is expected to:
  - **Symlink** or **copy** selected directories from the vault into the Gemini CLI config location (typically `~/.gemini/` or equivalent), **or**
  - Manually place vault files into paths already read by Gemini CLI
- There is **no evidence** of:
  - A `GEMINI_CONFIG_PATH` override
  - A loader script that dynamically registers vault contents

### Documentation-safe phrasing
> “The vault is integrated through explicit filesystem placement (copy or symlink). Gemini CLI consumes the vault implicitly by discovering files in its standard configuration paths.”

**Implication:**  
This is a *Unix-style* model: the vault is the source of truth, but **the user controls what is activated**.

---

## 2. Asset Management (`stage_assets.sh`)

**Answer:**  
`stage_assets.sh` functions primarily as a **harvesting / backup script**, not a deployment script.

### What “staging” means here
- The script appears designed to:
  - Scan an existing Gemini CLI environment
  - Identify relevant configuration, skill, and chain files
  - Copy (“stage”) them into the vault repository
- This is reinforced by:
  - The presence of `found_assets.txt`
  - The absence of an inverse “apply” or “deploy” script

### One-line definition for the manual
> “Asset staging is the process of extracting live Gemini CLI configuration from a system and committing it into the vault for versioned persistence.”

**Important clarification:**  
There is **no automated push-back** from vault → system today.

---

## 3. Skill & Chain Schemas

**Answer:**  
The vault uses **standard Gemini-compatible file formats**, organized by **directory convention**, not by embedded schema metadata.

### What’s standard
- Skills and chains are represented as:
  - `.md`
  - `.toml`
  - Plain-text configuration files
- There is **no custom DSL**, registry file, or manifest format.

### How differentiation is achieved
- **Environment-specific logic** is separated by top-level directory:
  - `Desktop/`
  - `Termux/`
- **Shared vs. specialized behavior** is implied by *placement*, not tagging:
  - Same-named skills may exist in both environments
  - Divergence is handled manually

### Documentation-safe summary
> “Skill identity and scope are defined structurally by directory location, not by metadata or schema flags.”

---

## 4. Hardware Parity & Upstream Changes

**Answer:**  
Upstream propagation between Desktop and Termux is **manual and operator-driven**.

### Current strategy (explicitly)
- There is:
  - ❌ No sync script
  - ❌ No shared core directory
  - ❌ No diff-based promotion workflow
- Improvements made in one environment must be:
  - Manually copied
  - Manually adapted (due to OS/tooling differences)
  - Manually committed

### Conceptual model (use this wording)
> “Desktop and Termux vaults evolve independently, with selective, manual backporting of improvements when portability is desired.”

This is a **deliberate tradeoff**, not an omission:
- It avoids accidental breakage on constrained Termux systems
- It preserves environment realism

---

## 5. Dependency Handling (External Binaries)

**Answer:**  
`gemini-vault` follows a **pure bring-your-own-runtime model**.

### What the vault does NOT do
- No dependency checks
- No installer scripts
- No OS package manifests
- No runtime validation hooks

### What is assumed
- The operator:
  - Knows which skills require which binaries
  - Installs those binaries manually (`ffmpeg`, `ripgrep`, `python`, etc.)
  - Accepts responsibility for environment readiness

### Clean phrasing for the manual
> “The vault intentionally does not manage external dependencies. Runtime availability is an operator responsibility.”

---

# 🔍 Addressing the Identified Gaps (Explicitly)

## Gap 1: `found_assets.txt`
**Reality:**  
- This file is best understood as:
  - A **discovery log**
  - A snapshot of what was detected during asset staging
- It is **not** a registry and **not authoritative**.

**Suggested wording:**
> “`found_assets.txt` documents discovered configuration during staging but does not define active state.”

---

## Gap 2: No Master Registry
**Reality:**  
- There is **no central index** of:
  - Enabled skills
  - Environment applicability
  - Dependency requirements

**Interpretation (important):**
This reinforces that gemini-vault is:
- A **configuration archive**
- Not a **policy engine**

---

# ✅ Architectural Characterization (One Paragraph)

If you want a single, authoritative description for the manual:

> *gemini-vault is a filesystem-native, operator-curated configuration vault for Gemini CLI. It relies on directory structure and manual integration rather than loaders, registries, or automation. This design prioritizes transparency, portability, and operator control over convenience abstractions.*

---

## Optional Next Steps (You Choose)

- xterm.js integration components

**Why**
- Complexity hotspots
- React anti‑patterns
- Performance risks
- Maintainability

---

### 5. Styling System
I need to see how UI styles are applied.

- `tailwind.config.*`
- Global CSS (if any)
- Any design‑system components (buttons, panels, modals)

**Why**
- Consistency
- Scalability
- Avoiding Tailwind abuse / class explosion

---

## ✅ STRONGLY RECOMMENDED (Quality & Robustness)

These aren’t strictly required, but they **dramatically improve** the quality of the review.

### 6. Utility & Helper Files
Examples:
- `src/utils/*`
- `src/lib/*`

**Why**
- Business logic leaking into UI
- Testability
- Reuse patterns

---

### 7. Types & Interfaces
- `src/types/*`
- Shared interfaces

**Why**
- Type safety
- Future extensibility
- Avoiding `any` creep

---

### 8. Error Handling & Async Code
Anything touching:
- API calls
- WebSockets
- File IO
- Config serialization

**Why**
- UI crash risks
- Race conditions
- Bad UX under failure

---

## 🟡 OPTIONAL (Advanced / Deep Review)

Only include these if you want **elite‑level polish feedback**.

### 9. Performance‑Sensitive Areas
- Virtualized lists
- Heavy re‑renders
- xterm render loop
- Drag‑and‑drop handlers

### 10. Tests (if any)
- Component tests
- Hook tests

---

  main.tsx

## **SECTION 1 — How This System Works**
 
This manual documents a five-phase workflow for producing professional technical documentation with AI assistance. The system was developed and validated through the production of the RootlessJamesDSP Technical Manual — a 1,300-line, fully formatted, OOXML-validated Word document produced in a single session using Claude. 
The workflow is not a collection of prompts you mix and match. It is a gated pipeline: each phase gates the next. You cannot produce a clean document by skipping phases. Every structural problem in a finished document traces back to work that was done in the wrong order.
### **1.1 — The Core Principle**
 
**Structure first. Paint second.** 
This means: confirm your information before writing, validate your structure before formatting, and validate your formatting before generating output files. The moment you add visual design to a structurally broken document, you have made the problem harder to find and harder to fix. 
**_ℹ NOTE: This principle comes from the Document Design Manual (Version 2.0, 2025): "Aesthetic enhancement applied to a chaotic structure will fail — the visual noise will amplify, not resolve, the reader's confusion."_**
### **1.2 — Why AI Requires a Gated Pipeline**
 
AI language models have two failure modes in documentation work:
- **Hallucination:** Inventing parameter names, UI labels, values, or steps that do not exist in the actual system being documented. This is the most dangerous failure mode because the output looks correct.
- **Premature formatting:** Applying structure and formatting before the content is validated. This produces beautifully designed documents with wrong information. 
The five-phase pipeline addresses both failures. Phase 1 (Discovery) prevents hallucination by forcing information confirmation before any writing begins. Phase 2 (Structure) prevents premature formatting by requiring a validated skeleton before formatting is applied.
### **1.3 — What Each Phase Produces**
<table>
<tr>
<td>

</td>

</td>

### **1.4 — Reading This Manual**
 
Each section covers one phase of the workflow in full: what it does, when to use it, the complete prompt text, and worked examples drawn from the RootlessJamesDSP production process. The appendices collect the reusable reference material — the 20-point audit checklist, callout hierarchy, archetype selector, glossary, and tool reference. 
**_✔ TIP: If you are starting a new document from scratch, read Sections 1–3 in order. If you are refactoring an existing document, go directly to Section 4. If you have a finalized Markdown document and need the .docx output, go to Section 6._**
## **SECTION 2 — Phase 1: Discovery & Information Architecture**
 
Phase 1 has one job: gather all real information before a single word of documentation is written. This phase prevents hallucination entirely. You cannot hallucinate about information you have confirmed. 
Phase 1 ends when you can answer all five intake questions with specific, confirmed answers — not approximations, not guesses, not "probably something like this."
### **2.1 — The Five Intake Questions**
 
Send these questions to your AI in a single batch before any documentation begins. Do not begin writing until all five are answered.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**\#**
</td>
<td> 
**Question**
</td>
<td> 
**What a Bad Answer Looks Like**
</td>
<td> 
**What a Good Answer Looks Like**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**1**
</td>
<td> 
**What is the exact name, version, and platform of the system?**
</td>
<td> 
**"Some audio app on Android"**
</td>
<td> 
**"RootlessJamesDSP v1.6.3, Android 14, Pixel 9, non-rooted"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**2**
</td>
<td> 
**Who is the reader? Describe them in one sentence.**
</td>
<td> 
**"Someone who uses the app"**
</td>
<td> 
**"A non-technical Android user who has never used Shizuku, following instructions on their phone"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**3**
</td>
<td> 
**What screenshots, notes, or confirmed data can you provide?**
</td>
<td> 
**"I'll describe it from memory"**
</td>
<td> 
**Actual screenshots of every app screen, with exact UI labels visible**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**4**
</td>
<td> 
**What parameter names, values, and UI labels are confirmed from the actual interface?**
</td>
<td> 
**"It has some EQ settings"**
</td>
<td> 
**"Multimodal equalizer, FIR Minimum phase, Piecewise Cubic Hermite Interpolating Polynomial — exact text from screenshot"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**5**
</td>
<td> 
**What is the single most important task a reader needs to complete?**
</td>
<td> 
**"Understand the app"**
</td>
<td> 
**"Enter both preset configurations correctly and verify DSP is active"**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
**_✖ DANGER: Never allow "I'll fill that in later" as an answer to any intake question. A gap that is not flagged in Phase 1 will be hallucinated in Phase 2. There is no such thing as a harmless assumption in technical documentation — a wrong parameter value, a wrong command, or a wrong UI path causes real failure for a real user._**
### **2.2 — The Phase 1 Prompt**
 
Copy this prompt in full and send it before any documentation work begins. Replace <span style="color:#880000">{{YOUR PROJECT DESCRIPTION}}</span> with a one-sentence description of the system you are documenting. 
<span style="color:#212121">\<system\_instructions\></span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> \<role\_definition\></span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> You are a Senior Technical Documentation Architect.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> Your primary directive is ZERO HALLUCINATION.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> You never invent parameter names, values, UI labels, or steps.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> If information is missing, you ask. You do not fill gaps with assumptions.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> \</role\_definition\></span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> \<cognitive\_config\></span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> Before writing any content, complete this hidden analysis:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 1. DECONSTRUCT: What does the user actually need documented?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 2. INVENTORY: What information do I have confirmed vs. unconfirmed?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 3. GAP LIST: What am I missing that I must ask for before writing?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 4. AUDIENCE MAP: Skill level, usage context, and reader goal?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 5. STRUCTURE PLAN: What sections are required? In what order?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> \</cognitive\_config\></span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> \<intake\_protocol\></span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> Ask the user these questions in ONE batch before writing anything:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 1. Exact name, version, and platform of the system being documented?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 2. Who is your reader? One sentence.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 3. What screenshots, notes, or raw data can you provide?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 4. What parameter names, values, and UI labels are confirmed from</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> the actual interface?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 5. What is the single most important task a reader must complete?</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> Do not proceed until all five are answered.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> \</intake\_protocol\></span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\</system\_instructions\></span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\<user\_query\>{{YOUR PROJECT DESCRIPTION}}\</user\_query\></span>
### **2.3 — How the XML Structure Works**
 
The prompt uses XML-style delimiter tags. This is not decorative — it serves a specific function in how AI models process instructions.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Tag**
</td>
<td> 
**Purpose**
</td>
<td> 
**Why It Matters**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
<span style="color:#880000">\<system\_instructions\></span>
</td>
<td> 
**The root container for all directives to the AI**
</td>
<td> 
**Establishes the highest-priority instruction set. Content here overrides default AI behavior.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
<span style="color:#880000">\<role\_definition\></span>
</td>
<td> 
**Defines what the AI is acting as**
</td>
<td> 
**Activates the relevant knowledge and behavioral constraints for the assigned role.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
<span style="color:#880000">\<cognitive\_config\></span>
</td>
<td> 
**Defines the reasoning process the AI must use**
</td>
<td> 
**Forces "System 2" thinking — the AI plans before it writes, rather than generating stream-of-consciousness output.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
<span style="color:#880000">\<intake\_protocol\></span>
</td>
<td> 
**Defines the specific actions the AI must take**
</td>
<td> 
**Creates a gated sequence: the AI cannot proceed to documentation without completing the intake.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
<span style="color:#880000">\<user\_query\></span>
</td>
<td> 
**The specific input to be processed**
</td>
<td> 
**Separated from instructions so the AI does not confuse your project description with its operating rules.**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
**_ℹ NOTE: Gemini 1.5/2.0 models show the highest adherence to XML-delimited instructions. Claude and GPT-4 also respond well to this format. The key benefit is not the tags themselves — it is the separation of role, reasoning process, and task into distinct blocks that the model can address independently._**
### **2.4 — The \[CONFIRM\] Convention**
 
Any information the AI cannot confirm from your provided materials must be marked <span style="color:#880000">\[CONFIRM: what is needed\]</span> — never guessed, never approximated, never left blank. 
**Examples of correct \[CONFIRM\] usage:** 
<span style="color:#212121">\*\*Step 3:\*\* Tap \[CONFIRM: exact label of the settings button in your screenshot\].</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Set Maximum gain to \[CONFIRM: confirm whether this value is in dB or % in your UI\].</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Navigate to Settings → \[CONFIRM: exact sub-menu name for audio processing in your app version\].</span> 
When a <span style="color:#880000">\[CONFIRM\]</span> marker appears in your skeleton output, stop. Answer the question. Do not proceed to Pass 2 until all markers are resolved. 
**_⚠ WARNING: Unresolved \[CONFIRM\] markers in Pass 2 or later are the primary cause of plausible-sounding but wrong documentation. The AI will fill them in with something reasonable — which is exactly the problem. Reasonable-sounding wrong information is worse than an obvious gap because readers will follow it._**
## **SECTION 3 — Phase 2: Structure & The Three-Pass System**
 
Phase 2 converts your confirmed information into a validated document. It uses three sequential passes. Each pass has a different job, uses different AI instructions, and produces a different kind of output. The passes are not interchangeable and must happen in order.
### **3.1 — The Three Passes**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Pass**
</td>
<td> 
**Name**
</td>
<td> 
**Input**
</td>
<td> 
**Output**
</td>
<td> 
**Key Prompt Verb**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**1**
</td>
<td> 
**Expansion / Structure**
</td>
<td> 
**Raw confirmed notes and data from Phase 1**
</td>
<td> 
**Full document skeleton with every section defined and every gap marked \[CONFIRM\]**
</td>
<td> 
**Refactor, Outline, Organize**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**2**
</td>
<td> 
**Formatting**
</td>
<td> 
**The approved skeleton (all \[CONFIRM\] markers resolved)**
</td>
<td> 
**Formatted document with tables, numbered steps, code blocks, callout boxes**
</td>
<td> 
**Format, Convert, Structure**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**3**
</td>
<td> 
**Finesse / Audit**
</td>
<td> 
**The formatted draft**
</td>
<td> 
**Final, tone-correct, passive-voice-free, error-free document**
</td>
<td> 
**Rewrite, Elevate, Audit**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
**_✖ DANGER: Do not attempt to combine Pass 1 and Pass 2 in a single prompt. "Refactor my notes into a formatted document" is the single most reliable way to produce well-formatted wrong content. Structure and formatting require different cognitive tasks — separating them produces dramatically better results._**
### **3.2 — Pass 1 Prompt: The Skeleton**
 
This prompt transforms raw notes, screenshots, and confirmed data into a structured skeleton. The output must contain every section, every step sequence, and every table — but no formatting beyond basic structure. 
<span style="color:#212121">Act as a senior technical writer.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Refactor the following raw notes into a structured technical document skeleton.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Create these sections in this exact order:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 1. Purpose & Scope (one paragraph)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 2. Prerequisites (bulleted list)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 3. Step-by-Step Procedure (numbered, imperative mood — one action per step)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 4. Verification (how to confirm each major step worked)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 5. Troubleshooting (Symptom → Cause → Solution format)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 6. Quick Reference (key commands or values at a glance)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Rules:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Use imperative mood exclusively.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> Write "Click the button" not "The button should be clicked."</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Do not add information not present in the source notes.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Do not fill gaps with assumptions.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> Mark missing information as \[CONFIRM: what is needed\].</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Every numbered step must contain exactly one discrete action.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Source notes:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\[PASTE RAW NOTES / SCREENSHOTS / DATA HERE\]</span> 
**_✔ TIP: After receiving the Pass 1 skeleton, read every \[CONFIRM\] marker and provide the missing information before sending the Pass 2 prompt. If you have more than five unresolved markers, go back to Phase 1 and gather the missing information first._**
### **3.3 — Pass 2 Prompt: Formatting**
 
This prompt applies full formatting to the validated skeleton. Send it only after all <span style="color:#880000">\[CONFIRM\]</span> markers in the skeleton are resolved. 
<span style="color:#212121">Take the skeleton above and convert it into a fully formatted document.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Apply these formatting rules:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Tables: header row bold, alternating row shading</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Step sequences: numbered lists with verification after each critical step</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Warnings: clearly labeled WARNING or DANGER, never buried in body text</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Code or commands: in monospace code blocks, never inline in prose</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Parameter values: bold when appearing in body text</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- No placeholder text: every \[CONFIRM\] marker must be resolved before</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> this pass completes.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">If any \[CONFIRM\] markers remain unresolved, stop.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Ask the user to provide that information.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Do not proceed past unresolved gaps.</span>
### **3.4 — Pass 3 Prompt: The Strict Editor**
 
This prompt runs a linguistic and structural audit on the formatted draft. It produces a fault table first, then a corrected document. Never skip this pass — passive voice in procedure steps, ambiguous pronouns, and weasel words are invisible at the writing stage and only obvious to a reader following the instructions in practice. 
<span style="color:#212121">Act as a strict technical editor.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Review the document above and identify every instance of:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 1. Passive voice — flag it and provide the active voice correction</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 2. Ambiguous pronouns — "it", "they", "this" with unclear referents</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 3. Weasel words — "may", "might", "could", "should" in procedure steps</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 4. Undefined acronyms — any term not spelled out on first use</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 5. Steps with more than one action combined into one numbered item</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 6. Any \[CONFIRM\] or TODO markers not resolved</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Output format:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> A table: Issue Type \
 Original Text \
 Corrected Version</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">After the table, output the fully corrected document.</span>
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Issue Type**
</td>
<td> 
**Example of the Problem**
</td>
<td> 
**Corrected Form**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Passive voice**
</td>
<td> 
**"The Start button should be clicked"**
</td>
<td> 
**"Click the Start button"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Passive voice**
</td>
<td> 
**"Shizuku must be re-paired after rebooting"**
</td>
<td> 
**"Re-pair Shizuku after rebooting"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Ambiguous pronoun**
</td>
<td> 
**"After it is installed, open it"**
</td>
<td> 
**"After installing Shizuku, open the Shizuku app"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Weasel word**
</td>
<td> 
**"You should reduce Post gain"**
</td>
<td> 
**"Reduce Post gain"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Multi-action step**
</td>
<td> 
**"Click File, then click Save"**
</td>
<td> 
**Split: Step 1: Click File. Step 2: Click Save.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Undefined acronym**
</td>
<td> 
**"Configure the DSP settings"**
</td>
<td> 
**"Configure the Digital Signal Processor (DSP) settings"**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
## **SECTION 4 — Phase 3: Document Anatomy by Archetype**
 
Phase 3 defines the internal anatomy of your document before any content is written. Different document types serve different cognitive contracts with the reader — what they need, how fast they need it, and what "success" looks like for them. Using the wrong anatomy for a document type creates confusion even when the content is correct.
### **4.1 — Universal Anatomy: Elements Required in Every Document**
 
Every professional technical document must contain these elements, in this order, regardless of document type:
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Position**
</td>
<td> 
**Element**
</td>
<td> 
**What It Contains**
</td>
<td> 
**Why It Cannot Be Omitted**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**1**
</td>
<td> 
**Purpose Block**
</td>
<td> 
**One paragraph: what this document covers and explicitly what it does NOT cover**
</td>
<td> 
**Sets the reader's expectations before they invest time reading**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**2**
</td>
<td> 
**Audience Block**
</td>
<td> 
**Who should read this and at what assumed skill level**
</td>
<td> 
**Lets readers self-qualify and sets the writing register**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**3**
</td>
<td> 
**Prerequisites**
</td>
<td> 
**Everything that must be true, installed, or known before Step 1**
</td>
<td> 
**Prevents the most common support query: "I followed the guide but it didn't work"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**4**
</td>
<td> 
**Table of Contents**
</td>
<td> 
**Linked to actual section headers; must be accurate**
</td>
<td> 
**Documents above ~4 pages without a TOC impose a search burden on the reader**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**5**
</td>
<td> 
**Body Sections**
</td>
<td> 
**Content, organized by the correct archetype structure**
</td>
<td> 
**The subject matter of the document**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**6**
</td>
<td> 
**Verification**
</td>
<td> 
**How to confirm each major step worked**
</td>
<td> 
**Converts passive reading into active confirmation — reduces errors dramatically**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**7**
</td>
<td> 
**Troubleshooting**
</td>
<td> 
**Symptom → Cause → Solution for known failure modes**
</td>
<td> 
**Answers the questions that will be asked anyway; better to answer them in the document**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**8**
</td>
<td> 
**Quick Reference**
</td>
<td> 
**Critical values, commands, or decisions at a glance**
</td>
<td> 
**Serves returning users who already know the procedure and just need a specific value**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**9**
</td>
<td> 
**Glossary**
</td>
<td> 
**Every technical term defined on first use, collected here**
</td>
<td> 
**Allows non-expert readers to function without blocking document flow for expert readers**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
### **4.2 — Archetype: User Manual / Setup Guide**
 
A user manual is the authoritative instruction set for a product or system. Its primary obligation is sequential clarity — the reader must never be uncertain about what step comes next, what it will do, or how to recover if it fails. 
**Additional required sections beyond universal anatomy:**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Section**
</td>
<td> 
**Contents**
</td>
<td> 
**Placement**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Signal Chain / Dependency Map**
</td>
<td> 
**A visual or tabular map of what depends on what — processing order, setup sequence, or system architecture**
</td>
<td> 
**After Table of Contents, before body sections**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Warning Hierarchy**
</td>
<td> 
**DANGER / WARNING / CAUTION / TIP callouts with consistent semantic color coding**
</td>
<td> 
**Defined once in the document; used throughout body sections**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Step-Capture Visual Reference**
</td>
<td> 
**Annotated screenshots with numbered badges corresponding to numbered procedure steps**
</td>
<td> 
**Adjacent to the steps they illustrate; never separated**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Per-Module or Per-Feature Reference**
</td>
<td> 
**One sub-section per feature: what it does, every parameter, preset values for each use case**
</td>
<td> 
**Body sections, one H2 per major feature**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
**_✔ TIP: The step-capture visual pattern — numbered badges on screenshots correlating to numbered text steps — is the single most effective technique for instruction-type documentation. Readers follow annotated screenshots with 40–60% fewer errors than text-only procedures._**
### **4.3 — Archetype: Standard Operating Procedure (SOP)**
 
An SOP is a compliance document. Unlike a user manual, which may describe many optional paths, an SOP is prescriptive — there is one correct path, and deviation from it must be explicitly prohibited. 
**Additional required sections beyond universal anatomy:**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Section**
</td>
<td> 
**Contents**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Roles & Responsibilities**
</td>
<td> 
**Who performs this SOP and who approves the output. Required in regulated environments.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Document Control Block**
</td>
<td> 
**Version number, effective date, review date, approver name and signature. Must appear on the cover page.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Quality Checks**
</td>
<td> 
**Verification steps embedded at critical junctures within the procedure, not only at the end.**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
**_⚠ WARNING: SOPs must use imperative mood exclusively. "Click the Start button" — never "The Start button should be clicked" or "You should click the Start button." Passive voice in an SOP creates legal ambiguity and is non-compliant in regulated industries (FDA, ISO, GxP environments)._**
### **4.4 — Archetype: API Reference**
 
API documentation is consumed primarily by developers who scan, not read. They arrive with a specific question and want the answer in under 10 seconds. Every design decision must serve searchability and scannable structure. 
**Additional required sections beyond universal anatomy:**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Section**
</td>
<td> 
**Contents**
</td>
<td> 
**Critical Rule**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Authentication**
</td>
<td> 
**How to authenticate to the API — auth method, token format, header name**
</td>
<td> 
**Placed at the very top. Every developer hits this first.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Endpoint Overview Table**
</td>
<td> 
**Method (GET/POST/etc), URL, one-line description in a scannable matrix**
</td>
<td> 
**Must be the first thing visible after authentication**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Request/Response Examples**
</td>
<td> 
**Complete, real-world examples for every endpoint**
</td>
<td> 
**Never use placeholder values like "foo", "bar", or "example.com". Use realistic values.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Parameter Tables**
</td>
<td> 
**Name, Type, Required/Optional, Description, Example Value — one row per parameter**
</td>
<td> 
**Every parameter documented, no exceptions**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Error Code Reference**
</td>
<td> 
**Every possible error code, its meaning, and how to resolve it**
</td>
<td> 
**Never omit this. Every developer will hit at least one error.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Code Samples**
</td>
<td> 
**Working examples in at least two languages (e.g., Python and cURL)**
</td>
<td> 
**Must be complete and runnable. "// rest of code" is not acceptable.**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
### **4.5 — Archetype: Comparison Matrix**
 
The comparison matrix is a decision-support document. Its sole purpose is to help the reader choose between two or more options. Every design decision must serve that goal.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Design Rule**
</td>
<td> 
**Implementation**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Use icons instead of text for binary data**
</td>
<td> 
✅** for Yes/Available, **❌** for No/Unavailable, ⚠ for Partial. Icons reduce visual complexity by ~40%.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Highlight the preferred column**
</td>
<td> 
**Apply a subtle background tint to the recommended option column — the Von Restorff isolation effect makes it memorable.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Lead with the most important rows**
</td>
<td> 
**Place the deciding factors at the top. Readers who skim will see the critical differences first.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Group rows semantically**
</td>
<td> 
**Cluster related features under bold sub-headers (e.g., "Security", "Pricing", "Support").**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Add a Verdict row at the bottom**
</td>
<td> 
**A single-sentence recommendation. The reader should not have to synthesize the decision themselves.**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
### **4.6 — Archetype: Executive Report**
 
The executive report inverts the traditional writing structure. Unlike a technical guide where you build to a conclusion, the executive report leads with the conclusion. This is the Inverted Pyramid model.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Layer**
</td>
<td> 
**Content**
</td>
<td> 
**Target Reader**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Lead**
</td>
<td> 
**The single most important finding or recommendation — one sentence**
</td>
<td> 
**Executives who read only the first paragraph**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**TL;DR Box**
</td>
<td> 
**3–5 bullet points summarizing the full report — placed immediately after the lead**
</td>
<td> 
**Decision-makers with 30 seconds**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Supporting Evidence**
</td>
<td> 
**Data, charts, and analysis substantiating the lead**
</td>
<td> 
**Stakeholders who need to understand the basis for the recommendation**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Detailed Methodology**
</td>
<td> 
**How the data was gathered and analyzed**
</td>
<td> 
**Reviewers who need to verify the process**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Appendices**
</td>
<td> 
**Raw data, footnotes, supplementary material**
</td>
<td> 
**Analysts and auditors**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
## **SECTION 5 — Phase 4: Refactoring Existing Documents**
 
Phase 4 applies when you have an existing document that needs to be improved — restructured, elevated in tone, corrected for consistency, or audited for completeness. The process is the same whether the document was originally written by a human or an AI.
### **5.1 — The Refactoring Audit Prompt**
 
This prompt runs the 20-point editorial audit. It always produces two outputs: the fault table first, the corrected document second. Never skip the fault table — it makes the issues visible and fixable. 
<span style="color:#212121">Act as a senior technical editor conducting a complete document audit.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Review the document below against this 20-point checklist.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Produce two outputs:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> A. A table listing every item that FAILS:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> columns: Issue # \
 Category \
 Location \
 Problem \
 Fix Required</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> B. The fully corrected document after all fixes are applied.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">STRUCTURE</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 01. Reading only H1 and H2 headers reveals the complete document scope</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 02. Each numbered step contains exactly one discrete action</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 03. TOC entries match actual heading text exactly</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 04. Prerequisites are listed before Step 1 (not embedded mid-procedure)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 05. All instructions use imperative mood</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">CONTENT INTEGRITY</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 06. Every parameter name or UI label matches the actual interface</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 07. Every value has a source or is marked \[UNVERIFIED\]</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 08. No placeholder text (no TODO, TBD, lorem ipsum, YOUR\_API\_KEY)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 09. All acronyms are defined on first use</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 10. No passive voice in any procedure section</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">WARNINGS & CALLOUTS</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 11. DANGER callouts cover only irreversible or destructive actions</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 12. WARNING callouts cover recoverable errors and significant risks</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 13. TIP callouts are not overused (max 1 per 300-400 words)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 14. No critical information conveyed by formatting or color alone</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">VISUAL DESIGN (flag for human review)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 15. H1, H2, H3 headings have visually distinct weight and size</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 16. Code and commands are in monospace blocks, not inline prose</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 17. Tables use alternating row shading or clear border separation</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 18. Parameter values are bold when referenced in body text</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">COMPLETENESS</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 19. Troubleshooting section exists (Symptom → Cause → Solution format)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> 20. Quick Reference section exists with critical values at a glance</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Source document:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\[PASTE DOCUMENT HERE\]</span>
### **5.2 — Common Refactoring Patterns**
 
These are the most frequently found issues when auditing AI-generated or rapidly written technical documents:
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Issue**
</td>
<td> 
**Where It Appears**
</td>
<td> 
**Why It Happens**
</td>
<td> 
**Fix**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Passive voice**
</td>
<td> 
**Procedure steps, troubleshooting**
</td>
<td> 
**Default AI writing style for technical content**
</td>
<td> 
**Run the Pass 3 strict editor prompt before delivering any document**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Multi-action steps**
</td>
<td> 
**Setup procedures**
</td>
<td> 
**"Click File, then click Save" feels like one action to the writer but two actions to the reader**
</td>
<td> 
**Split every step at "then", "and then", or any comma joining two verbs**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Prerequisites buried mid-document**
</td>
<td> 
**Installation guides, API guides**
</td>
<td> 
**Writer discovers prerequisites as they write and inserts them at the point of need**
</td>
<td> 
**Extract all prerequisites into the Prerequisites section; remove them from body**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Undefined acronyms**
</td>
<td> 
**Technical sections**
</td>
<td> 
**Writer is domain-expert and forgets not all readers know the acronym**
</td>
<td> 
**First use: spell out in full, then acronym in parentheses: "LDAC (Low Complexity Subband Coding)"**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Callout box overuse**
</td>
<td> 
**Documents with many edge cases**
</td>
<td> 
**Every caveat becomes a callout; warnings stop being warnings**
</td>
<td> 
**Maximum 1 callout per 300–400 words. Embed minor caveats in body text.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**TOC drift**
</td>
<td> 
**Long documents edited iteratively**
</td>
<td> 
**Headings are changed after TOC is written**
</td>
<td> 
**Regenerate TOC after every structural edit; check exact text match**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
### **5.3 — The Tone Shift Prompt**
 
When the document content is structurally correct but the tone is wrong — too informal, too verbose, or not precise enough — use this targeted prompt: 
<span style="color:#212121">Rewrite the following paragraph to be more formal and precise.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Remove any jargon a non-technical reader would not understand.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Replace every instance of passive voice with active voice.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">The output must be no longer than the input.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Do not add new information.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\[PASTE PARAGRAPH HERE\]</span>
## **SECTION 6 — Phase 5: Production Output (Docx / PDF)**
 
Phase 5 converts a finalized, validated Markdown document into a production .docx file using the Node.js <span style="color:#880000">docx</span> library (v9+). This phase only begins after the document content is completely locked — all \[CONFIRM\] markers resolved, all passes complete, all edits applied. 
**_✖ DANGER: Never begin Phase 5 on a document that has not completed Phase 2 Pass 3 (the strict editorial audit). A beautiful .docx built on structurally broken or content-wrong Markdown is significantly more time-consuming to fix than the Markdown would have been._**
### **6.1 — Phase 5 Prerequisites**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Requirement**
</td>
<td> 
**How to Verify**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Node.js installed**
</td>
<td> 
**Run **<span style="color:#880000">node --version</span>** in terminal. Should return v16 or higher.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**docx npm package installed globally**
</td>
<td> 
**Run **<span style="color:#880000">npm list -g docx</span>**. Should return **<span style="color:#880000">docx@9.x.x</span>** or higher.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Source Markdown locked**
</td>
<td> 
**All \[CONFIRM\] markers resolved. Pass 3 audit complete. No TODOs remaining.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Validation tools available**
</td>
<td> 
**Python scripts at **<span style="color:#880000">scripts/office/validate.py</span>**, **<span style="color:#880000">unpack.py</span>**, **<span style="color:#880000">pack.py</span>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
### **6.2 — The Docx Generation Prompt**
 
Send this prompt to the AI with your finalized Markdown as the source. The AI will generate a complete Node.js script that builds the .docx. 
<span style="color:#212121">You are a document engineer using the npm \`docx\` library (v9+).</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Convert the finalized Markdown document below into a production .docx file.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Before writing code, apply these absolute rules:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">CONTENT RULES</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Every section from the source Markdown must appear in the output.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- All tables: reproduce exactly — same columns, same rows, same values.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- All code blocks: use Courier New in a shaded container.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- All numbered steps: use docx numbering config, not manual "1." text.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Never insert unicode bullets (•) as text — use LevelFormat.BULLET.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">TECHNICAL RULES (critical for Google Docs compatibility)</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Page size: always US Letter (12240 x 15840 DXA). Never A4 default.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Table widths: always WidthType.DXA. Never WidthType.PERCENTAGE.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Tables: set columnWidths on Table AND width on each TableCell.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Cell shading: always ShadingType.CLEAR. Never ShadingType.SOLID.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Table-level borders (tblBorders): after generating, validate with</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> validate.py. If w:left errors appear in tblBorders, unpack the docx,</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> replace w:left → w:start and w:right → w:end in tblBorders blocks,</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> then repack.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Never use \\n inside TextRun — use separate Paragraph elements.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- PageBreak must be inside a Paragraph, never standalone.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">HEADING STYLES</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Use HeadingLevel.HEADING\_1/2/3 for all structural headings.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Override built-in styles with exact IDs: "Heading1", "Heading2", etc.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Include outlineLevel in heading styles (0 for H1, 1 for H2, 2 for H3).</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- H1 background color: apply shading to Paragraph, not just TextRun.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">CALLOUT BOXES</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Implement as styled Paragraphs with left border + shading.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Never use a Table row as a callout container.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">VALIDATION</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- After generating, run: python scripts/office/validate.py output.docx</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- If validation fails: unpack → fix XML → repack.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\- Do not deliver an unvalidated file.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">Source Markdown:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">\[PASTE FINALIZED MARKDOWN HERE\]</span>
### **6.3 — The Validation & Fix Sequence**
 
After the AI generates the Node.js script and you run it to produce the .docx, validate it before doing anything else. Validation catches XML schema errors that may not be visible in the rendered document.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Step**
</td>
<td> 
**Command**
</td>
<td> 
**What It Checks**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**1 — Validate**
</td>
<td> 
<span style="color:#880000">python scripts/office/validate.py output.docx</span>
</td>
<td> 
**OOXML schema compliance. Returns PASSED or a list of errors with line numbers.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**2 — Unpack (if errors)**
</td>
<td> 
<span style="color:#880000">python scripts/office/unpack.py output.docx unpacked/</span>
</td>
<td> 
**Extracts all XML files, pretty-prints them for editing, merges adjacent runs.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**3 — Fix XML**
</td>
<td> 
**Edit files in **<span style="color:#880000">unpacked/word/</span>
</td>
<td> 
**For tblBorders errors: replace **<span style="color:#880000">w:left</span>** → **<span style="color:#880000">w:start</span>** and **<span style="color:#880000">w:right</span>** → **<span style="color:#880000">w:end</span>** inside **<span style="color:#880000">\<w:tblBorders\></span>** blocks.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**4 — Repack**
</td>
<td> 
<span style="color:#880000">python scripts/office/pack.py unpacked/ output.docx --original output.docx</span>
</td>
<td> 
**Re-validates on pack. Returns PASSED or remaining errors. Repeat from step 2 if errors remain.**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
**_ℹ NOTE: The most common validation error is \`w:left\` in \`tblBorders\`. This occurs because the \`docx\` library emits the deprecated attribute name. The fix is a find-and-replace in the unpacked XML — it takes under 30 seconds once you know the pattern. See Section 6.3 Step 3 above._**
### **6.4 — Google Docs Import**
 
A validated .docx opens in Google Docs without reformatting issues. The import process:
- Go to **drive.google.com**
- Click **New → File upload** and select your <span style="color:#880000">.docx</span> file
- After upload, right-click the file → **Open with → Google Docs**
- Google Docs converts and renders the document, preserving all heading styles, table formatting, and paragraph formatting
- To save as a Google Doc permanently: **File → Save as Google Docs** 
**_✔ TIP: For any document containing colored paragraph shading (like H1 backgrounds), use the Google Docs import path — not a direct copy-paste. Copy-paste does not preserve shading from Word/docx format._**
## **SECTION 7 — The Complete Prompt Stack: Quick Reference**
 
This section collects all five phase prompts in the order you send them. Each prompt is numbered and labeled. Copy the full text of each prompt. Do not paraphrase, abbreviate, or split them. 
**_⚠ WARNING: The prompts must be sent in order. Sending Prompt 3 before Prompt 2 is complete produces a formatted document with structural errors baked in. Sending Prompt 5 before Prompt 4 is complete produces a beautifully formatted document that fails the editorial audit._**
### **7.1 — Prompt Sequence Summary**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Prompt #**
</td>
<td> 
**Name**
</td>
<td> 
**Send When**
</td>
<td> 
**Expected Output**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Prompt 1**
</td>
<td> 
**Phase 1 Intake**
</td>
<td> 
**Before any documentation begins**
</td>
<td> 
**A list of questions you must answer before the AI writes anything**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Prompt 2**
</td>
<td> 
**Pass 1 Skeleton**
</td>
<td> 
**After answering all Prompt 1 questions**
</td>
<td> 
**Full document skeleton with \[CONFIRM\] markers for all gaps**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Prompt 3**
</td>
<td> 
**Pass 2 Formatting**
</td>
<td> 
**After all \[CONFIRM\] markers in the skeleton are resolved**
</td>
<td> 
**Fully formatted document draft**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Prompt 4**
</td>
<td> 
**Pass 3 Audit**
</td>
<td> 
**After the formatted draft is complete**
</td>
<td> 
**Fault table + corrected document**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Prompt 5**
</td>
<td> 
**Phase 5 Docx Output**
</td>
<td> 
**After the corrected document is fully approved**
</td>
<td> 
**.docx generation script + validation instructions**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
### **7.2 — Prompt 1: Phase 1 Intake**
 
Activate this at the start of every new documentation project. Send the XML prompt from Section 2.2, followed by your project description. 
<span style="color:#212121">How to send:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">1. Copy the full XML prompt from Section 2.2.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">2. Replace {{YOUR PROJECT DESCRIPTION}} with one sentence</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> describing what you are documenting.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">3. Send.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">4. Answer all five intake questions the AI asks.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">5. Do not proceed to Prompt 2 until all five are answered.</span>
### **7.3 — Prompt 2: Pass 1 Skeleton**
 
Send after completing Phase 1 intake. Include all your confirmed information, screenshots descriptions, and notes as the source material. 
<span style="color:#212121">How to send:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">1. Copy the Pass 1 prompt from Section 3.2.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">2. Replace \[PASTE RAW NOTES / SCREENSHOTS / DATA HERE\]</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> with everything you gathered in Phase 1.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">3. Send.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">4. Review every \[CONFIRM\] marker in the output.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">5. Answer each one before proceeding to Prompt 3.</span>
### **7.4 — Prompt 3: Pass 2 Formatting**
 
Send only after all \[CONFIRM\] markers in the skeleton are resolved. The AI needs the complete skeleton and your resolved answers as context. 
<span style="color:#212121">How to send:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">1. Copy the Pass 2 prompt from Section 3.3.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">2. If the AI does not already have the skeleton in context,</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> paste the full skeleton above the Pass 2 prompt.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">3. Send.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">4. Review the formatted output for completeness.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">5. Do not send Prompt 4 until you are satisfied</span> 
<span style="color:#212121"> with the structure.</span>
### **7.5 — Prompt 4: Pass 3 Audit**
 
Send after reviewing the formatted draft. This prompt must always produce a fault table as its first output — if you receive only the corrected document with no fault table, ask the AI to re-run the audit and produce the table first. 
<span style="color:#212121">How to send:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">1. Copy the Pass 3 prompt from Section 3.4.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">2. Paste the full formatted document as context.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">3. Send.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">4. Read the fault table.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">5. Verify all faults are addressed in the corrected document.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">6. If you disagree with a fault finding, resolve it manually.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">7. Approve the final corrected document before proceeding.</span>
### **7.6 — Prompt 5: Docx Output**
 
Send only after the corrected document is fully approved. The output is a Node.js script — you run it in your terminal to produce the .docx file. 
<span style="color:#212121">How to send:</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">1. Copy the Docx Generation prompt from Section 6.2.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">2. Paste the full finalized Markdown as the source.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">3. Send.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">4. Save the generated Node.js script as build.js.</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">5. Run: node build.js</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">6. Validate: python scripts/office/validate.py output.docx</span> 
<span style="color:#212121">7. If validation fails: follow the fix sequence in Section 6.3.</span>
## **APPENDIX A — The 20-Point Editorial Audit Checklist**
 
Run this checklist on every document before it is delivered. A document that passes all 20 points is ready for distribution.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**\#**
</td>
<td> 
**Category**
</td>
<td> 
**Audit Item**
</td>
<td> 
**Pass Criteria**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**01**
</td>
<td> 
**Structure**
</td>
<td> 
**Header Scan**
</td>
<td> 
**Reading only H1 and H2 headers reveals the complete document scope**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**02**
</td>

</td>

</td>

</td>

</td>

</td>

</td>

<td>

</td>

**_✔ TIP: Run this checklist twice: once when you believe the document is done, and once 24 hours later with fresh eyes. Many formatting issues that are invisible after intensive editing become immediately obvious the following morning._**
## **APPENDIX B — Callout Box & Warning Hierarchy Reference**
 
Callout boxes are the most powerful typographic tool in documentation. They interrupt the reader's linear scan and signal: "This information is different in kind from what surrounds it." There are four semantic types. Each has a strict icon, color, and usage protocol.
### **B.1 — The Four Callout Types**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Type**
</td>
<td> 
**Icon**
</td>
<td> 
**Background**
</td>
<td> 
**Border Color**
</td>
<td> 
**Use For**
</td>
<td> 
**Never Use For**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**DANGER**
</td>
<td> 
**✖**
</td>
<td> 
**Light red**
</td>
<td> 
**#C62828**
</td>
<td> 
**Irreversible actions, data loss, security breaches, safety hazards. Conditions that WILL cause immediate harm.**
</td>
<td> 
**Anything recoverable. If the user can undo it, it is not DANGER.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**WARNING**
</td>
<td> 
**⚠**
</td>
<td> 
**Light amber**
</td>
<td> 
**#F9A825**
</td>
<td> 
**Recoverable errors, significant risks, potential data loss if the step is performed incorrectly.**
</td>
<td> 
**Stylistic preferences, minor variations, optional steps.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**NOTE / INFO**
</td>
<td> 
**ℹ**
</td>
<td> 
**Light blue**
</td>
<td> 
**#3949AB**
</td>
<td> 
**Context, background, supplementary facts that help the reader understand but are not required for the core task.**
</td>
<td> 
**Replacing body text. Notes must supplement, not substitute.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**TIP**
</td>
<td> 
**✔**
</td>
<td> 
**Light green**
</td>
<td> 
**#2E7D32**
</td>
<td> 
**Best practices, shortcuts, and helpful context that enhances the reader's experience. Not required for core task success.**
</td>
<td> 
**Mandatory steps. If the reader must do it, it is a step, not a tip.**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
### **B.2 — Callout Usage Limits**
 
Callout box overuse is one of the most common document design failures. When every other paragraph is a callout, none of them are callouts — they become the baseline and lose their power to interrupt the reader's attention.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Callout Type**
</td>
<td> 
**Recommended Maximum Frequency**
</td>
<td> 
**Consequence of Overuse**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**DANGER**
</td>
<td> 
**1–2 per major section, only where genuinely irreversible risk exists**
</td>
<td> 
**DANGER loses meaning; readers stop treating it as a hard stop**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**WARNING**
</td>
<td> 
**1 per major workflow step where genuine risk exists**
</td>
<td> 
**WARNING becomes background noise; readers skip it**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**NOTE / INFO**
</td>
<td> 
**1 per 300–500 words of body text**
</td>
<td> 
**Document looks fragmented; body text loses authority**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**TIP**
</td>
<td> 
**1 per 300–400 words of body text**
</td>
<td> 
**Tips stop feeling helpful; they become friction**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
### **B.3 — ISO/ANSI Warning Hierarchy (for safety-critical documents)**
 
Documents that cover physical, electrical, or chemical safety must use the ISO/ANSI Z535-compliant warning hierarchy. This is distinct from the information callout system and uses specific standardized language:
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Signal Word**
</td>
<td> 
**Meaning**
</td>
<td> 
**Example Condition**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**DANGER**
</td>
<td> 
**Indicates an imminently hazardous situation which, if not avoided, WILL result in death or serious injury**
</td>
<td> 
**High-voltage contact, explosive gas ignition**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**WARNING**
</td>
<td> 
**Indicates a potentially hazardous situation which, if not avoided, COULD result in death or serious injury**
</td>
<td> 
**Pinch points, hot surfaces, electrical shock risk**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**CAUTION**
</td>
<td> 
**Indicates a potentially hazardous situation which, if not avoided, MAY result in minor or moderate injury**
</td>
<td> 
**Ergonomic strain, sharp edges, tripping hazards**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**NOTICE**
</td>
<td> 
**Indicates information considered important but not hazard-related**
</td>
<td> 
**Equipment damage, data loss, process deviation**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
## **APPENDIX C — Document Archetype Quick-Select Guide**
 
Use this table to identify the correct archetype for your document before beginning Phase 3. Choosing the wrong archetype creates structural mismatch between the document's design and the reader's needs.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**If the reader's primary question is...**
</td>
<td> 
**Use this archetype**
</td>
<td> 
**Design priority**
</td>
<td> 
**Typical length**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"How do I perform this task step by step?"**
</td>
<td> 
**User Manual / Setup Guide**
</td>
<td> 
**Sequential clarity, numbered steps, verification at each stage**
</td>
<td> 
**10–100+ pages**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"What are the exact steps I must follow with no deviation?"**
</td>
<td> 
**Standard Operating Procedure (SOP)**
</td>
<td> 
**Precision, compliance, imperative mood, document control**
</td>
<td> 
**1–20 pages**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"What does this endpoint / parameter mean?"**
</td>
<td> 
**API Reference**
</td>
<td> 
**Searchability, code examples, parameter tables**
</td>
<td> 
**Varies by API size**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"Get me running in under 10 minutes"**
</td>
<td> 
**Quick Start Guide**
</td>
<td> 
**Speed, minimal friction, copy-paste commands**
</td>
<td> 
**1–4 pages**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"Which option should I choose?"**
</td>
<td> 
**Comparison Matrix**
</td>
<td> 
**Visual contrast, scannability, decision support**
</td>
<td> 
**1–3 pages**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"Give me the conclusion quickly"**
</td>
<td> 
**Executive Report / Briefing**
</td>
<td> 
**Inverted pyramid — conclusion first, evidence second**
</td>
<td> 
**2–10 pages**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"What changed in this version?"**
</td>
<td> 
**Release Notes**
</td>
<td> 
**Chronological, grouped by impact, clear severity labels**
</td>
<td> 
**1–5 pages**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"What does this term mean?"**
</td>
<td> 
**Glossary / Reference Guide**
</td>
<td> 
**Alphabetical, hanging indents, cross-references**
</td>
<td> 
**1–30 pages**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"How do I contribute to or extend this system?"**
</td>
<td> 
**Developer / Contributor Guide**
</td>
<td> 
**Architecture diagrams, conventions, build steps**
</td>
<td> 
**10–50 pages**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**"What happened and what did we learn?"**
</td>
<td> 
**Incident Report / Post-Mortem**
</td>
<td> 
**Timeline, root cause, corrective actions, blame-free tone**
</td>
<td> 
**2–10 pages**
</td>
</tr>
</table>
## **APPENDIX D — Glossary of Terms**
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**Term**
</td>
<td> 
**Definition**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**\[CONFIRM\] marker**
</td>
<td> 
**A placeholder inserted by the AI when information is missing or unverified. Must always be resolved before the document moves to the next pass. Never left in a final document.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**20-Point Audit**
</td>
<td> 
**The editorial checklist used in Pass 3 of the Three-Pass System. Covers structure, content integrity, callout usage, visual design, and completeness.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Archetype**
</td>
<td> 
**A document type with a specific cognitive contract with the reader. Examples: User Manual, SOP, API Reference, Comparison Matrix. Each has a different required section structure.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Callout box**
</td>
<td> 
**A visually distinct paragraph containing supplementary information (NOTE), best practices (TIP), recoverable risks (WARNING), or irreversible risks (DANGER). Not to be used as a substitute for body text.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Cognitive contract**
</td>
<td> 
**The implicit agreement between a document and its reader about what the reader needs, how fast they need it, and what "success" looks like. Different archetypes have different cognitive contracts.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Discovery phase**
</td>
<td> 
**Phase 1 of the workflow. Its job is to gather all confirmed information before writing begins. Output: a complete information inventory with all gaps flagged \[CONFIRM\].**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**DXA**
</td>
<td> 
**A unit of measurement used in the OOXML format for Word documents. 1440 DXA = 1 inch. Used for page size, table widths, and margins in the docx library.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Fault table**
</td>
<td> 
**The first output of the Pass 3 audit prompt. A table listing every document issue with its location, category, and required fix. The corrected document is the second output.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Gated pipeline**
</td>
<td> 
**A workflow structure where each phase produces output that is required as input by the next phase. You cannot skip phases — the gate prevents it.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Hallucination**
</td>
<td> 
**An AI model's generation of information that sounds correct but is not present in the source material — invented parameter names, values, steps, or UI labels. The primary risk in AI-assisted documentation.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Imperative mood**
</td>
<td> 
**The grammatical form used in procedure steps. "Click the button" (imperative) vs "The button should be clicked" (passive). Required in all procedure sections.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Inverted Pyramid**
</td>
<td> 
**A document structure borrowed from journalism. Leads with the conclusion (most important), followed by supporting evidence, followed by methodology and detail. Used in executive reports and briefings.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**OOXML**
</td>
<td> 
**Office Open XML — the ISO standard XML format underlying .docx files. The docx npm library generates OOXML. Validation checks schema compliance.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Pass 1 / Pass 2 / Pass 3**
</td>
<td> 
**The three sequential steps of Phase 2 (Structure). Pass 1 = expansion/skeleton. Pass 2 = formatting. Pass 3 = strict editorial audit.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Passive voice**
</td>
<td> 
**A grammatical construction where the subject receives the action rather than performs it. "The button is clicked" vs "Click the button." Prohibited in procedure sections because it creates ambiguity about who performs the action.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Phase**
</td>
<td> 
**One of the five stages of the Document Design Prompt System workflow. Phases are gated — each phase's output is the next phase's input.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Signal chain**
</td>
<td> 
**A section in a user manual or setup guide that shows processing order, setup sequence, or system architecture — what depends on what and in what order. Placed before body sections.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Skeleton**
</td>
<td> 
**The output of Pass 1. A structured document with all sections defined, all steps listed, all tables outlined — but no formatting beyond basic hierarchy. All gaps flagged \[CONFIRM\].**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**SOP**
</td>
<td> 
**Standard Operating Procedure. A compliance document defining the exact, reproducible steps required to complete a regulated or repeatable task. One correct path; no optional deviations.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**tblBorders**
</td>
<td> 
**The XML element in OOXML that defines border styling at the table level. The docx library emits deprecated **<span style="color:#880000">w:left</span>**/**<span style="color:#880000">w:right</span>** attributes here; these must be replaced with **<span style="color:#880000">w:start</span>**/**<span style="color:#880000">w:end</span>** for strict OOXML compliance.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Three-Pass System**
</td>
<td> 
**The structured approach in Phase 2: Pass 1 (expansion/skeleton), Pass 2 (formatting), Pass 3 (audit/finesse). Must be executed in order. Passes cannot be combined.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Von Restorff effect**
</td>
<td> 
**A cognitive principle: a visually distinct item in a list is more easily remembered than items that appear similar. Used in comparison matrices to highlight the recommended option column.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**Weasel word**
</td>
<td> 
**Words that reduce the precision of a statement: "may", "might", "could", "should" in a procedure context. "You should click Save" vs "Click Save." Prohibited in procedure steps.**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
**XML delimiter tags**
</td>
<td> 
**Semantic XML tags used to structure AI prompts: **<span style="color:#880000">\<system\_instructions\></span>**, **<span style="color:#880000">\<role\_definition\></span>**, **<span style="color:#880000">\<cognitive\_config\></span>**, etc. Not HTML — they serve as structural separators that AI models parse for instruction hierarchy.**
</td>
</tr>
</table>

## **Table of Contents**
 
**<span style="color:#2196F3">ℹ NOTE: Table of Contents Rendering</span>**

**<span style="color:#E94560">PART I: FOUNDATION</span>** 
<span style="color:#666666">Why AI Agents Produce Shallow Documentation and How to Fix It</span>
## **Chapter 1: The Documentation Depth Problem**
### **1.1 Why Default AI Output Is Shallow**
 
Every AI language model ships with the same fundamental bias: produce a helpful-sounding response as quickly as possible. This bias creates three failure modes that destroy documentation quality before the first word is written.
#### **Failure Mode 1: Premature Generation**
 
When you tell an AI agent "write me a manual for X," it immediately begins generating content. It does not ask what you already know. It does not ask what your readers need. It does not confirm whether the features it is about to describe actually exist in your version of the software. It writes first and hopes for the best. The result is a document that looks professional but contains invented parameter names, fabricated menu paths, and hallucinated configuration options.
#### **Failure Mode 2: Breadth Without Depth**
 
Default AI output covers many topics superficially rather than fewer topics thoroughly. A typical AI-generated setup guide might say "install the dependencies" without listing them, "configure the settings" without showing the configuration file, and "verify the installation" without providing a verification command. Each section exists as a heading with one or two sentences beneath it. The document has structure but no substance.
#### **Failure Mode 3: Missing Operational Knowledge**
 
AI models are trained on documentation, not on the experience of using software. They know what a tool does in theory but not what breaks in practice. They cannot tell you that Gemini CLI has timeout issues that require configuration changes, that Android’s Phantom Process Killer will terminate your Ollama inference mid-generation, or that Termux from the Play Store is outdated and you must use the GitHub beta release. This operational knowledge—the knowledge of what actually happens when you run the commands—is what separates a useful manual from a decorative one.
### **1.2 The Solution: Gated Pipeline Prompting**
 
The Document Design Prompt System (DDPS) solves all three failure modes through a single architectural decision: force the AI to gather information before it writes anything. This is implemented as a five-phase gated pipeline where each phase must complete before the next can begin.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Phase</span>**

**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Name</span>**

The critical insight is that this pipeline is not Claude-specific. It works on any AI agent that can follow multi-turn instructions. The challenge is configuring each platform to enforce the gates. That is what this manual teaches.
### **1.3 What “Production-Grade” Means**
 
Throughout this manual, "production-grade" documentation means output that meets all of the following criteria:
- Every command and code block is complete and runnable—no ellipsis, no TODO markers, no placeholder functions
- Every procedure step has a verification method—"how to know it worked"
- Common errors are addressed with symptoms, causes, and solutions—not just "if you see an error, check the documentation"
- Technical terms are defined on first use with a glossary in the appendix
- Examples use realistic data from the actual system, not foo/bar/example.com
- Prerequisites are explicitly listed with version requirements
- Platform-specific differences are documented (CachyOS vs Termux, Windows vs Linux)
- Troubleshooting sections address real issues encountered during actual use 
**<span style="color:#4CAF50">✔ TIP: The Pixel Bible Standard</span>** 
The Android Terminal AI Handbook (Pixel Bible) is the reference implementation. 
30+ chapters, 7 parts, every command tested on actual Pixel 9 hardware. 
Thermal throttling profiles, RAM constraints, model quantization trade-offs— 
all from real-world testing, not documentation scraping. 
This is the depth standard every agent configuration in this manual targets.
## **Chapter 2: The Five Behavioral Pillars**
 
To produce documentation at Pixel Bible depth, any AI agent—cloud or local—must exhibit five specific behaviors. These behaviors are not optional features. They are requirements. If an agent cannot be configured to exhibit all five, it is not suitable for documentation work.
### **2.1 Pillar 1: Interrogative Intake**
 
The agent must ask questions before generating content. Not after. Not optionally. The system prompt must make questioning the default behavior and generation the gated behavior.
#### **What the Agent Must Ask**
 
The intake questions must cover five domains in sequence. Each domain builds on the previous one. Skipping domains produces the same shallow output as no questioning at all.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Round</span>**

<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Domain</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Purpose</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Example Questions</span>**
</td>

<tr>
<td>

</td>
<td>

</td>
<td>

</td>
<td>

</table>
 
**<span style="color:#FF9800">⚠ WARNING: Batch Size Matters</span>** 
Ask 3–5 questions per round, maximum. Asking 20 questions at once 
overwhelms the user and produces lower-quality answers. 
Wait for answers before proceeding to the next round.
### **2.2 Pillar 2: Gap Detection and Flagging**
 
After intake, the agent must explicitly identify what it still does not know. Every gap must be flagged with a \[CONFIRM\] marker, not silently filled with a plausible guess. This is the single most important anti-hallucination mechanism in the entire system. 
The gap detection prompt must force the agent to produce three outputs:
- A summary of confirmed facts (what the agent knows for certain)
- A list of unresolved gaps (what the agent needs but does not have)
- A list of assumptions the agent is making (so the user can correct them) 
**<span style="color:#F44336">☠ DANGER: The Hallucination Trap</span>** 
If an agent does not flag gaps, it fills them. Silently. 
You get a professional-looking document with invented parameter names, 
fabricated UI labels, and plausible-sounding but nonexistent commands. 
This is worse than no documentation because readers trust it.
### **2.3 Pillar 3: Structured Generation**
 
Content must be generated through a three-pass system, not in a single generation pass. Each pass has a specific job and a specific output format.
<table>
<tr>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Pass</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Name</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Job</span>**
</td>
<td> 
**<span style="color:#FFFFFF">Output</span>**
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Pass 1
</td>
<td> 
Skeleton
</td>
<td> 
Expand confirmed facts into full document structure
</td>
<td> 
All sections defined, all steps listed, all tables outlined—no formatting
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Pass 2
</td>
<td> 
Formatting
</td>
<td> 
Apply visual hierarchy, callouts, code blocks, tables
</td>
<td> 
Fully formatted document draft
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> 
Pass 3
</td>
<td> 
Audit
</td>
<td> 
20-point editorial review against quality checklist
</td>
<td> 
Fault table + corrected document
</td>
</tr>
</table>
 
The reason for three passes instead of one is that a single-pass generation conflates content decisions with formatting decisions. The agent tries to simultaneously decide what to write and how to format it, producing beautifully structured documents with wrong information. Separating the passes forces content correctness before visual polish.
### **2.4 Pillar 4: Verification-First Writing**
 
Every procedure step must include a verification method. The agent must be instructed to never write a step without answering the question: "How does the user know this step worked?" 
The verification pattern follows a consistent structure across all documentation: 
Step N: \[Action description\] 
Why: \[Brief explanation of purpose\] 
Command: 
 $ command --flag value 
Expected Output: 
 \[Exact output the user should see\] 
If You See Errors: 
 Error: \[common error message\] 
 Cause: \[why it happens\] 
 Fix: \[exact command to resolve\] 
Verification: 
 $ verify-command 
 ✓ You should see: \[expected result\]
### **2.5 Pillar 5: Iterative Refinement Loop**
 
After delivering documentation, the agent must offer targeted improvements rather than considering the task complete. The refinement prompt must ask five specific questions:
- Is the technical depth appropriate for your audience?
- Are there sections that need more detail?
- Did I miss important use cases or workflows?
- Should any sections be split into separate documents?
- Do you need companion materials (cheat sheets, slide decks, video scripts)? 
This loop is not a courtesy question. It is a quality gate. Documentation that passes initial generation but fails user testing is documentation that needed one more refinement pass. 
**<span style="color:#E94560">PART II: THE UNIVERSAL SYSTEM PROMPT</span>** 
<span style="color:#666666">The Core Prompt That Works on Every Platform</span>
## **Chapter 3: Anatomy of the Universal Documentation Prompt**
### **3.1 Prompt Architecture Overview**
 
The universal documentation prompt is constructed from seven functional blocks. Each block serves a specific purpose and can be adapted for different platforms. The blocks must appear in this order—reordering them changes the agent’s priority weighting.
